Navvy Songs
Played Out.
The Red Page
As a bullock falls in the crooked ruts, he fell
when the day was o’er,
The hunger gripping his stinted guts, his
body shaken and sore.
They pulled it out of the ditch in the dark,
as a, brute is pulled from its lair,
The corpse of a navvy, stiff and stark, with
the clay on its face and. hair.
In Christian lands, with calloused hands, he
labored for others’ good.
In workshop and mill, ditchway and drill,
earnest, eager and rude;
Unhappy and gaunt with worry and want, a
food to the whims of fate,
Hashing it out and booted about at the will
of the goodly and great.
To him was applied the scorpion lash, for
him the gibe and the goad —
The roughcast fool of our moral wash, the
rugous wretch of the road.
Willing to craiol for a pittance small to the
swine of the tinsel sty.
Beggared and burst from, the very first, he
chooses the ditch to die —
Go, pick the dead from the sloughy bed, and
hide him from mortal eye.
He tramped through the colorless winter
land, or sunned in the scorching heat,
The dry skin hacked on his sapless hands, or
blistering on his feet;
He ical lowed in mire unseen, unknown.
where your houses of pleasure rise.
And hapless, hungry, and chilled to the bone,
he builded the edifice.
In cheerless model and filthy pub his sinful
hours were passed.
Or footsore , weary, he begged his grub, in
the sough of the hail-whipped blast,
So some might riot in wealth and ease, with
food and wine be crammed.
He wrought like a mule, in muck to the
knees, dirty, dissolute, damned.
Arrogant, adipose, you sit in the homes he
builded high;
Dirty the ditch, in the depths of it he chooses
a spot to die.
Foaming with nicotine-tainted lips, holding
his aching breast,
Dropping down like a cow that slips, smitten
with rinderpest;
Drivelling yet of the work and the wet,
swearing as sinners swear.
Raving the rule of the gambling school, mix-
ing it up with a prayer.
He lived like a brute, as the navvies live,
and went as the cattle go.
No one to sorrow, and no one to shrive, for
Heaven ordained it so —
He handed his check to the shadow in black,
and went to the misty lands,
Never a mortal to close his eyes or a woman
to cross his hands.
As a bullock falls in the rugged ruts
He fell when the day was o’er.
Hunger gripping Iris weasened guts,
But never to hunger more —
They pulled it out of the ditch in the dark.,
The chilling frost on its hair.
The mole-skinned navvy, stiff and stark,
From no particular where.
The Navvy Chorus.
’Twas in the beginning of ages,
To the make of the navvy there came
Work and the lowest of wages
Ever a mortal could claim,
Bread, with its age for leaven,
Rows, and the prison cell,
Few of the gifts of Heaven,
And most of the vices of Hell,
Time, and dislike to do it.
Love, for the wine when red.
And a bibulous leaning to it
Despite what the sages said.
And the demons took in hand
Moleskin, leather and clay,
Oaths embryonic and
A longing for Saturday,
Kneesirups and blood and flesh,
A chest exceedingly stout,
.1 soul —(which is a ques-
tion open to many a doubt)—
And fashioned with pick and shovel,
And shapened in mire and mud,
With life of the road and. hovel,
And death of the line or hod,
With fury and frenzy, and fear,
That his strength might endure for a
span
From birth, through beer to bier.
The link ’twixt the ape and the man.
They gave him a will to strive.
And earn the pittance which
Can barely keep him alive,
To slave in the dirty ditch —
Poor ho use and prison they wrought,
Bo he might enter therein
When idleness fell to his lot
Or poverty led to sin.
They have given him transient joys.
They have given him space for delight,
The model, its riot and noise.
The night, and the fleas of the night,
The jeer of the better-dressed neighbor,
And curses to every breath.
Labor, and dodging of labor.
Foreknowledge of sudden death —
Foredoomed to go to the Devil,
He carries a swearing gift.
His life is a path of evil
Between a shift and a shift.
The Navvy.
Remote from mansign and from mart.
Beyond our outer, furrowed fields —
One with the rock he cleaves apart.
One with the weary pick he wields.
Bowed with his weight of discontent,
Beneath the heavens sagging grey.
ITis steaming shoulders stark and bent,
He drags his joyless years away.
For dreamy dames with haughty eves.
And cunning men with soft white hands
Tim e offered you in sacrifice
Lone outcast of the outcast lands.
For all tiie furs that keep them mar.::,
For all the food that keeps them, fit.
Through all the pears they’re wrought you
harm.
And take a churlish pride in it.
Brutish we're hashed it far and near,
I’ve shared your woe and dull despair;
We’ve sung our songs and none to hear,
And told our wrongs and none to care.
Some day—how soon we may not tell —•
We’ll rend the riven fetters free.
Till then, may Heaven guard you well,
And God he good to you—and me.
From “Songs of the Dead End,” by
Patrick Mac Gill.
Patrick Mac Gill, the author of Songs of
the Head End (Year Book Press, London),
was born in Ireland 21 years ago. When 12
years old he had to become a farm hand, his
working day the year through being from
5 a.m. to 13 p.m. At 14 he crossed to *
Scotland, and for several years he was either
farm hand, drainer, tramp, navvy, platelayer,
or wrestler. At 1!) he published Gleanings
from a 'Savvy’s Scrap-Book, of which 8000
copies were sold. A London newspaper
appointed him to its editorial staff, but in
the following year he left his job and pub-
lished Songs of a Navvy. Both books being
now out of print, he has built from them,
with important additions, his present vol-
ume. Ilis portrait shows the picture of a
long-haired beauty actor.
As the spokesman of a class hitherto, in a
literary sense, inarticulate. Patrick Mac Gill
is Welcome. But it must be remembered in
criticism of his work that he represents the
voteless British navvy, and not the Aqs-
tralasian navvy, a member of a union, fully
seized of his rights and ready and able to
enforce them. Thus his savagery, his fierce
class hatred, his bitterness, his hopelessness,
are but the necessary tools of his trade. In
him the millions on the verge of starvation,
the mute victims of the soulless modern sys-
tem of capitalism, have found voice; and if
he shouts, it is only because nothing but the
false emphasis of a shout will attract atten-
tion in Britain to their wrongs. Here the
navvy has no necessity to yell. With this
qualification Mac Gill must be judged. With-
out it his work will be found forced and
turgid, yet its special quality of strength
will be felt. lie writes of what he has felt,
and bitterlv felt: and he writes with crude
craftsmanship. His wearmn i« not a pen,
but a viciously-swung pick. Mac Gill is a
specialised English Lawson :
These the Songs of a Savvy, hearing the
taint of the hrute,
Unasked, uncouth, unworthy, out to the
tcorld 1 put,
Stamped with the brand of labor, the heel of
a navvy’s hoot. . . .
The cold grub eaten in the dawn.
The wet shag smouldering as you smoke,
For ever being down and broke,
You learn to like it—later 0n....
You’ll likewise learn the useful rule,
The motto of the navvy man,
To do as little as you can.
And keep your pipe and stomach fu11....
Hashing it out like niggers on a two and a
tanner sub.
Everything sunk with your uncle, little to
burn at the pub,
Fifty-and-six were our hours, and never an
extra shift.
And whiles we were plunging at banker, and
whiles wc were studying thrift —
Sewing and patching the trousers, till their
parts were more than the whole.
Tailoring, cobbling and darning, grubbed on
a sausage and roll —
Thrift on a fourpenny hour, a matter of
nineteen bob,
But we glanced askew at the gaffer, and
stuck like glue to the job,
We of the soapless legion, we of the hammer
and hod,
Human swine of the muck-pile, forever for-
gotten of God. . . .
Have you tramped about in winter, whew
your boots were minus soles ?
Have you wandered sick and sorry with your
pockets full of — holes?
Have yon wondered which was better, when
your capita! was light,
A plate of fish and 'taters, or a hammock
for the night?
Have ”on sought through half the kingdom
for the job you could not get?
Have you eyed the city gutters for a stump
of cigarette?. . . .
These arc incidental worries which are
wrong to fuss about;
But, God! they matter greatly to the man
who’s down and out. .. .
They are tabid and outworn, unpresentable,
unshorn.
Occupants of many a model, wooers of the
harridan,
Workers of the wildernesses, dressing as the
savage dresses.
Crawling in the rear of progress, follow-
ing the march o' man....
The dirge of the loosened boulder,
And the thing that gasps beneath.
While the hod is yet on the shoulder,
The pipe is yet in the teeth.
Of the dynamite in the boring.
That didn’t go fiff when it should.
And the pica that ivent exploring,
And the pal who left for good—-
There is humor, too, in Patrick Mac Gill.
navvy-nioet. And he can tell a storv. in a
Bret Hartian way. But the chief fault in
these verses is the author’s obvious pose. lie
insists on being regarded as a navvv, not as
a Poet. Perhaps he is right; for lie has in-
cluded in this volume many pieces which
have no reference to the navvy, and in these
he shows that, lacking the vivid memories
of his experiences, he is capable oulv of
vapid and conventional verse.
Meips
E. A. Helps’ idea, as expounded in the
preface to Sungs and Ballads of Greater
Britain (Dent) : “We (in England) may
have written better poetry, they (the Greater
Britons) have lived it. Their poetry has a
Charm of its own, due to its freshness, orig-
inality, virility and variety of subject....l
determined to omit poetry of the passions
as being outside the aims and scope of this
selection. For the same reason I omit poetry
of a sacred or devotional character. I have,
however, brought together much patriotic,
legendary and historical verse, as well as
descriptive lyrics and ballads, and poems of
the imagination. . . .Nor can I claim to com-
pleteness (unfortunately in the cases of a
few writers in Australia and S. Africa I
have been unable to obtain permission) ;
doubtless, also, good work has escaped my
notice....As regards some of the poetry in-
cluded, it cannot be said to be of the highest
order. .. .That there should be some short-
comings is not a matter for surprise.... In
Australia, until comparatively recently, the
conditions of life of .her people have, been
unfavorable to reflection and literary expres-
sion.... There is abundant evidence of the
influence of Wordsworth, Shelley, Longfel-
low, Mrs. Hemans and Kipling, whose influ-
ence has appealed perhaps rather too strongly
to the virile minds of those engaged in strug-
gling with the forces of nature or man....
The reader must no( look for any highly
finished productions of the boudoir type
The Canadian poets, a maturer people, with
great variety and spaciousness in their sur-
roundings, naturally strike more strings, and
some with greater sureness than the other
Dominions. . . .It seems there are few women
among the poets of Australia, or else I have
not been fortunate in discovering them.” E.
A. Helps, who was formerly one of “lI.M.
inspectors of schools” in England, is right.
The contents of Songs and Ballads of Greater
Britain prove that we can live poetry better
than we can write it. He is right in not
claiming completeness, neither O’Dowd,
McCrae of Quinn is included, and good work
has, as he expects, escaped his notice, though
his net has been wide enough to sweep in
Dugald Ferguson, ,T. Liddell Kelly, Alex-
ander Bathgate, John Christie, Arthur
Patchett Martin, A. R. Wills and Garnet
Walsh. Helps is also right in stating that
some of the poetry included cannot be said
to be of the highest order. That there should
be some shortcomings is not a matter for
surprise, considering the lack of critical judg-
ment shown hv Helps in his selection. And
there is abundant evidence of the influence
of Mrs. Hemans in the verses chosen by
E. A. Helps, ex-inspector of H.M. schools.
And. according to the contents-list in this
compilation, there are few women,among the
poets of Australia. Helps is right; he has
not been fortunate in discovering them.

The Secret of the Pacific
0. Reginald EnoCk lias tackled a large
and lumpy problem in The Secret of the
Pacific (Unwin, per Dymock’s Library) ;
but though he has toted the bulky thing a
few thousand miles he has not succeeded in
discovering the nature of the merchandise
on his back. His hook is a discussion of the
origin of the early civilisation of America, the
Toltecs, Aztecs. Mayas, Incas, etc., and the
race or races buried beneath these peoples,
and of the origin of man in the two Ameri-
cas. Man either just happened or he arrived.
If he arrived, the probability is that be came
from Asia, across the then existing land-bridge
of Behring Strait. But Knock declines to
decide this important point, as indeed he
declines to decide other important points.
His method is to describe the marvellous
remains of that immense and widely scat-
tered civilisation in pre-Columbus America
—the territory on which ruins have been
found extends for 5000 miles —then to quote
authorities on both sides and leave the
matter balancing on its fence. But he does
make clear some salieut points. For in-
stance, while this pre-historic civilisation
covered not only an immense area, hut
extended throughout the Pacific, ancient
civilisation in the Old World was.
geographically, comparatively a suburban
matter. From the Euphrates to the
Nile, or from Chaldea to Egypt, is
only 1000 miles, and the country be-
tween offered facilities for intercourse
such as were entirely lacking among the
rugged, mountain chains and in fhe inclem-
ent regions of the barren western coasts of
the two Americas. And the numerous illus-
trations make plain what a marvellous civil-
isation that of pre-historic America was.
The ruins are grandiose; but it is the pot-
tery that give us astonishingly vivid and
personal portraits of the actual people; And
these laughing, grimacing character studies,
these stern, forceful physiognomies, have a
remarkable modernity. Well, they’re all
dead now, and without serving any purpose
in the evolution of mankind.
There is something almost pathetic in-
volved in a contemplation of the praise-
worthy arts and civilisations of prehistoric
America. They were cut off at a blow in
the mere act of discovery, ravished wanton v
and unnecessarily by Kith-century Enron/
and they seeni to have formed no link in th
march of human progress which intelligent
man bids himself to think is the destiny r f
the world. They were utterly swept aside
and became mere shells of ruins, scattered
monoliths and other fragments. The ner
ished civilisations of Asia and Africa were
at least links in a chain which reached in
ever-growing importance to the world of to-
day, but prehistoric America set no seal on
time, influenced no act on posterity, added
nothing to the plan of mankind. Yet we mav
reflect that the mbnuments of these ancient
isolated American peoples have survived by
virtue of their good qualities of solidity and
conscientiousness in construction. At least
we know that they were in the main the
perpetuation in stone of a religious purpose
and we must concede to their industrious
builders the lofty sentiment of a desire to
create and bequeath something which should
endure long after they themselves had passed
away.
But this civilisation spread itself beyond
America; it dotted % all the islands of the
Pacific with monoliths. Prof. Macmillan
Brown, of M.L. r has put forth a theory that
these monoliths were but the mile-stones of
the slow advance of a Caucasian prehistoric
and neolithic race from Europe, coming by
way of the Malay Peninsula to America-
and this theory, nowhere mentioned by
Knock, who seems strangely not to have
heard of Macmillan Brown, seems at least
a useful working hypothesis, with much evi-
dence to buttress it. Both Macmillan Brown
and Knock postulate, on good geological
grounds, the existence in prehistoric ages of
a great Pacific continent, another submerged
Atlantis, of which the islands of the Pacific
are but the peaks of its mountain ranges, and
the atolls but coral pillars erected on peaks
completely submerged.
If so, then the giant statues on Easter
Island and elsewhere throughout the Pacific
are the last vestiges of neolithic life on that
tremendous drowned continent.
The value of The Secret o* the Pacific lies
in the statement that there is a secret, and
the suggestion to archeologists that this
secret is more worth solving, and promises
more romance, than that of the beginnings
of civilisation in Asia.
Alleged Australian Literary Shrines
“Kola”: Will you allow. me to suggest
Casterton (Vic.) as a literary shrine? This
little village, on the Glenelg, has produced
Mary Gilmore, Donald Maclean and Grant
1 lorvey. None of these is perhaps entitled
to a top-notch ; but, taken together, they fur-
nish a respectable amount of literature, most
of it of the good Australian variety. I
cannot say if there are houses identified with
any or all of the three I have mentioned,
but there is sure to be one. Near at hand is
Coleraine, made immortal by Buvelot’s beau-
tiful picture —"A Waterhole at Coleraine.”
This is hardly literary, but it is art; and,
anyway, the pool is well worth a visit.
Buvelot was very partial to his picture of
(his pool, and painted replicas of it. One is
in the Warrnambool Art Gallery. By the
way, Maclean threatens, when he lias leisure,
to produce a really good Australian novel.
He complains that his published books were
written under disadvantages, and do not do
him justice. I hope not —I mean, I hope
they don’t do him justice.
“Luke Warm” : Apropos of literary shrines.
There is a little-known spot which, though
not exactly a shrine, is associated with the
name of one of our greatest poets. I refer
to Kendall’s Falls, four or five miles out of
Gosford, though they can be reached more
easily from Point Clare. The falls take their
name from Kendall’s rock, on which the
poet carved his name. Local rumor has it
that Kendall did much of his work in this
beautiful spot.
John D. Fitzgerald writes: —
Re Becke’s short account of his literary career, 1
desire to say that his recollection was not strictly
accurate in regard to this statement: “The editor
of The Bulletin sent copies of the book (‘ By Beef
and Palin’) to Harry Massinghanr, editor of the
London Daily Chronicle, who offered the collection
to Fisher Unwin.” The true version is that Mr.
Archibald wrote to me and asked line if I could do
anything to assist Becke to get a publisher (I had
just returned from London). I suggested that
proofs of the stories should be prepared, and sent to
my friend Mr. Massingham (who was quite unknown
to Mr. Archibald), with a covering letter from me
introducing Mr. Becke’s work. This was done.
(Becke’s letter thanking me enclosed herewith.) In
due course, I received a note from Mr. Massingham
saying: “1 read your friend Becke’s stories through
at one sitting, called a cal), and drove right rounu
to Fislier Unwin with the proofs. He read them.,
and will make Mr. Becke an offer in due course.
The result of this transaction was the beginning ot
the connection of Becke with Unwin, Jor a
time a most unsatisfactory one from Becke s point
of view. So long as Becke. remained in Australia
he was practically at the mercy of not oyer-generou.
London publishers, but, on his own showing, beg
a good deal from them when he was on the spot,
fact, when he got to London, in 1896, he was rat
nestered by publishers or their agents, all anx .
to buy his goods.; and in 1897, when 1 saw
again in London, he had “arrived” complete!',
was quite in the swim of literary and clu >

Do You Odolise P
Odol, the World’s Dentifrice, is the
preparation to use, for a few drops mixed
with a tumbler of water make an emulsion
which will thoroughly cleanse and purify
the oral cavity, destroying all injurious
bacteria.
It is the rinsing of the mouth and
the brushing of the teeth with this anti-
septic and delightfully refreshing mixture
that constitutes the process now known
as Odolising.
Odol penetrates the interstices in and
between the teeth and permeates the
gums and mucous membrane of the
mouth, exerting its marvellous powers
not only during the few moments while
using it, but for hours afterwards.

Business. Robbery, etc.
An illustration of the gambling done in overdue
ships is afforded by the big tramp steamer, Natal
Transport. The vessel left Newcastle (N.S.W.) for
Ocean Island on February 2, to load phosphates for
Borneo, and, on the way bad,, was to return to New-
castle for coal. Not showing up about April 7, it
gradually became more and more overdue, till it
was something like the long-missing Ark (Noah
Arkbottom, master and owner). The usual gambling
at Lloyd’s began. The rate kept rising, until, on
April 21, a cable arrived, stating that the re-insur-
ance rate had reached 1)0 guineas per cent. That is
to say, the brokers backed the ship at five to two to
turn up. Short odds in all conscience. The same
morning another wire reached Sydney to announce
the arrival of the Natal Transport at Borneo. Either
by a misunderstanding of his orddrs, or from some
subsequent cause, the skipper proceeed to Borneo
direct without returning to Newcastle. Anyhow, it
didn’t hurt him, and gave the gamblers at Lloyd’s a
chance to make a scoop. Also, of course, they had
a chance to lose a scoop.

“Sand Groper”: Has “Gouger” (B. 17/4/’l3) any
money, or does he know of anyone who has, to
speculate in sandalwood ? If so, I will undertake to
provide 1000 tons or more, cut and sap-barked
ready for shipment, at £lO per ton f.o.b. The £25
per ton mentioned by him is a myth. The best sandal-
wood grown on this continent comes from the W.A.
goldfields, principally between Yilgarn and North
Coolgardie (the Transcontinental line will go through
thousands of acres of good wood, which may have
the effect of further reducing the value), and for
some years the price thereabouts has not been higher
than £8 per ton. Quite recently it was as low as
£5 10s. for prime wood at Fremantle. “Mallara-
poonie,” who was through the West last year and
out as far as Laverton, will probably have some-
thing to say on this subject, for at the time of his
visit cutters were unable to earn tucker, the price
being so low and the freight to Fremantle so high
(£2 10s. per ton, plus ss. royalty). As for the
blacks bringing in wood, in the West the blacks are
just as likely to bring in hell in a paper-bag.
“Quarter Single”: There are two people in Mel-
bourne who would appreciate the attitude of Toddy,
in “Helen’s Babies.” Budge, Toddy’s brother, was
word-painting lavishly and at length the number of
presents he desired. Toddy declared for one simple
gift, remarking sententiousiy, “Don’t want to be bod-
dered wif yots of fings.” John Long, organiser of
the United Laborers’ Union, won a motor-car in the
St. Vincent’s Hospital raffle. Prior to that Pyrrhic
victory, says Long, ruefully, his wife could manage
on his salary. Now she has to struggle to pay for
the upkeep of the buzz-cart, which drinks petrol as
a wharfie drinks beer, wears out its tyres as swiftly
as a six-year-old his boots, and is in demand for
charity rides when its owner isn't using it. Long
is trying in vain to dispose of his juggernaut, and
is beginning to realise what the Northerner meant
when he said: “It would have been money in Jeff
Davis’s pocket if he had never been born.” The
other person who is suffering from excessive good
fortune is a Richmond woman, who won an allot-
ment of land at Laverton in a church rafHe. She is
unable to dispose of it, and- the charges for rates and
taxes are eating her out of house and home. In-
deed, the paddock may yet swallow the pantry, and
there is no longer any reliance to be placed on the
expression, “Dirt cheap.” When dirt is given away
it may be too dear for words.
“J.F.11.”: King George’s collection of Fiji stamps
has been on view here in London— the finest and most
complete in existence. Old Cannibal King Cakobau
had no use for stamps or post-offices, but the Fiji
Times wanted them, so it started a postal department
of its own. Its stamps were very crude; they were
printed with the Times’s own type on its own
premises at Levuka, and bore just three words —
“Fiji Times Express.” In the centre was the figure
or denomination —penny, threepenny, sixpenny or shil-
ling. They are now worth £lO. £l2, £l5 and £lB
respectively. In 1871 old Cakobau told the Fiji
Times people to shut up their stamp-shop and post-
office, as he had decided to go into the business him-
self, and, as they didn’t want to be converted into
“long pig,” they made haste to oblige. Cakobau had
his stamp engraved and electrotyped in Sydney. They
were headed “C.R.” (Cakobau Rex). After Hercules
Robinson, Governor of N.S.W., had added the group
to the British Empire, a V took the place of the C.
Far and awav the prettiest Fiji stamp is one designed
bv Leslie Walker, postmaster at Suva. It stows a
“pirogue” or native canoe steering towards the
rising sun.
* * * *
Thus R. M. Cochrane, from Perth, apropos -the
combinat’on among Australian master printers and
the “costing” system, the latter declared by “Quiz”
(B. 27/3/’l3) to be “the last possibility in the way
of exploitation”: The explanation of the costing
scale is as follows. A printing establishment is
liable to one thousand and one expenses which can-
not be directly charged to a customer. These are
rent, rates, taxes, insurance, depreciation of machin-
ery, buildings and stocks, clerical expenses, travel-
lers’ salaries and commissions, lighting, heating,
cleaning, power, telephones, postages, advertising, bad
debts, etc. When a printer is estimating on the scores
of small jobs which pass through his hands dailv, he
cannot all. day be adding on 1-S°nd of a penny for
insurance, l-64th for unavoidable spoils, a penny
for rent and a halfpenny for rates and taxes, and so
on. Instead of all this multitudinous estimating, a
book-keeping convention is adopted. It has been
found by experience that all these items, which can-
not be charged d'reet to a job, are about equal to
the wages paid in any one year. The printer, there-
fore, when estimating, doubles the estimated labor
cost, in order to arrive at an “omnibus rate,” which,
besides recovering the expenditure on wages, will auto-
matically recover the percentage of other expenditure
—rates, rent, taxes, etc., etc.-—which must be pro-
portioned to each particular job. This gives him
prime cost. He then adds his profit, which, in the
face of world-wide competition, must always be
reasonable. Since a penny or a twopenny stamp
will carry a client’s “copy” to any part of the
world where cheap printing may be had, and since
the tariff is of very little serv'ee, the truth is that
Che Australian printer is hard pressed to make a
living, while anvthing in the shane of extortion is
utterly impracticable. In the Freetrade port of
London, in direct competition with the cheap print-
ing “made in Germany,” an omnibus rate is charged,
which, allowing for the difference in wages, is higher
than our own. London charges Is. 9d. per hour on
an average wage of 9d., and even Germany cannot
materially undersell that rate. In Perth (W.A.)
the “omnibus” rate is 2s. 6d., wages being Is. 4d.,
as against 9d. in London. In this State there are
very few printers assessed to land and income tax,
and these only in small amounts. This is sufficient
proof that printing is not a profitable industry.
* * * *
Stanley Gibbons, pioneer of stamp shops in Lon-
don, died six weeks ago. He made a big fortune by
lucky finds. Thus, in 1879, he received a consign-
ment of twopenny “swans” from the postmaster at
Albany (Westralia). He put most of them by, and
lately traded them off at £2O a piece.
“Tripper” : “Trotter” (B. 20/3/’l3) brings me on
deck to support his objection to the demoralising
tipping system in a democratic community like Aus-
tralasia. Apart from its degrading influence on the
individual, it is a dangerous enemy to the wage-
earner, or will be if it gets a firm hold. Already
it has a settled place in the service of the shipping
cos. ; and in Maoriland, anyhow, it has been judi-
cially recognised by the Arbitration Court. For in-
stance, the stewards in the service of the big Union
Steam Ship Co. (and, no doubt, of the Huddart-Parker
Proprietary also) are paid £5 10s. per calendar
month, that, having been reckoned by the Court a
living wage when added to the tips paid direct by
the passenger to the steward. If the travellers do
not part up, the cos. would see the men boiled be-
fore they would pay over the difference between
£5 10s. and whatever is the recognised living wage.
The employee has then to go short; consequently, his
attitude i-n life is that of a worm, and the passenger
who looks as though he has the most money gets
the most worm-crawling. It’s a deplorable svstpm
for everybody but the cos. It pays them, otherwise
it would have been given the boot long ago.
“0.K.”: When the P.M.G. is making the talked-of
alteration in the red tint of the penny stamp of the
ghastly kangaroo series he may as well effect an im-
provement in the quality of the stamps. After all
the crudity of the design and poverty of the engrav-
ing offend only the assthetic sense of a community
that worries very little about it. But the cheapness
of the paper is a real grievance. The trouble with
a Commonwealth postage stamp is to prevent your-
self from tearing it at any part except the perfora-
tion. Considered as a piece of gummed paper its
name is sodden geological formation, or decayed
vegetable matter in a damp state. How comes it to
be so cheap and fragile? How much does a square
mile of kangaroo stamp cost the Government as com-
pared with the same area of stamp issued by a
State in the old days.
It doesn’t follow that the recent decision of Judge
Hollister, of the Federal Court, in the State of
Ohio, will be upheld on appeal; nevertheless, it is
worth while noting that his Honor, knowing that
heavy fines are ineffectual in such cases
cently, on conviction, handed the president
vice-president, directors and managers of a
cash register CO.-29 persons in all—terms
of imprisonment running from three months to one
year, for breaches of the Sherman antitrust law—
practically for organising an injurious monopoly Of
course, one can do a frightful lot of “appealing” in
the United States, but one can’t do so much in Aus
tralia, and there isn’t so much likelihood of an appeal
from a Supreme Court or a High Court judge being
upheld; so if the Australian anti-Trust law were
effective, and if then the managers of an Australian
combine were sentenced to imprisonment, there would
be more than an 'even chance of their going to gaol
And it isn’t much good bossing a monopoly that nets
you £lOO,OOO a year, when you have to go to Pent-
ridge or Yatala to “enjoy” it.
* * * *
A Melbourne black-and-white artist, who runs a
cozy bungalow in a southern suburb, tells a touch-
ing tale of a sad-eyed beggar-woman, who called at
his back door soliciting alms. She had two small
depressing infants in tow, and let off a hacking
“corf” every twentieth second. Could the “kind*
’ansum young gentleman ’elp a pore widder woman
what was bereaved of her ’usband, and left alone
with two orpings and a bed-rid mother?” The
artist was touched, and began to deliver the goods
He supplied bread and meat and tea; and another
’ackin’ corf brought up a shilling. The lone widder
moved off with her load, but got back in half a
minute with a further explosion of corfs and a deso-
late intimation that, if the nice, good, kind young
gentleman had a pair of cast-off trousers, they would
be received thankful. The artist raked up a pair
and handed them over. “But, look here, my good
woman,” he said, “if you have lost your husband
what do you want with trousers?” The beggar-ladv
brightened up a trifle. “Oh, these ain’t fer me
’usband,” she said ; “they’re for the gentleman I’m
keepin’ house for.”

“Gent Too”: I have run against a new industry
The correspondence from erring wives or husbands
in Divorce cases has always appeared to me to be an in-
teresting exhibition of one-eyed folly. Lately, however,
the mystery of the foolishness was elucidated after i
had plied a sinister-looking individual with whisky
and questions. The gentleman explained by degrees
that he was an inducer. He confided that a well-
known legal firm put a little work in his way. His
business was to call upon one of the parties to an
unfortunate matrimonial alliance, and infbrm h m
(or her —her mostly) that the other half intended
taking minor legal proceedings, but that these could
be obviated if a letter were written admitting mis-
conduct and promising reformation, whereqpon in
all probability a re-union might be effected. If any
pressure was necessary he would lay stress upon the
unpleasantness of having private affairs published in
the press. There were no intended proceedings as a
rule, but nine times out of 10 the damning docu-
ment was forthcoming, and when the writer was con-
fronted with the writ —well, he (or she) daren't do
anything but damn the inducer.

“Eryander” : Extract from letter just received from
friend attached to the big John Brown shipyard at
Clydebank (Scotland) : “I suppose you will have heard
by this time that we have gone one better than the
German Imnerator. Our new Cunarder measures just
the level 300yds.—well over one-sixth of a mile. I
can’t give you her exact gross till all the decks are
in, but I should put her at about 57,000 tons. Fancy
such a mass being hurled 20 knots an hour through
the water. . . .1 shall be glad when the riveting is
over. The noise is simply damnable, and many of
the men remain deaf for hours after knocking off. . . .
We’ve taken a hint from the Titanic horror, and
fitted this little skiff—Aquitania, she is to be called—
with strong bulkheads fore-and-aft, as well as thwart-
ships ....Thev’re talking about the Ist Feb. for the
launch, but I think we’ll be lucky if we get her into
the water by the middle of March.”
THE TYPEWRITER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Untiring brute! My energy you sap.
The days so blue.
The wasted seconds fly at every tap.
Oh! if you knew
The hatred hammered out on your cold keys,
The stifled love of sky and grass and trees!
Sad walls, so dull and drab, how many eyes
Have tired of you,
And longed in vain for turquoise-colored skies,
And dreamt of blue
As I am dreaming now. Dear blue, to me,
Through all the week you’re but a memory!
For I would be a vagabond, if so
I might be free
To live and roam where’er I chose to go
At liberty.
I curse these letters in a business vein ;
The neatly frozen products of the brain.
But I am purchased like a slave of old.
They chain me to
This ugly desk. A little piece of gold
Buys all I do,
While every moment of the young Spring day,
Love, winds and sunshine call me out to play.
N.S.W.
FREDA OLDMAN.
A GRAVE DEVELOPMENT
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In view of the general increase of wages, the trus-
tees of the two cemeteries in Geelong have decided to
increase the schedule of rates for burial. In some
instances charges have been raised as much as 50
per cent. —Neivs item.
The ancient on his final legs
Rose up and snorted hard,
And drivelled re “the good ole days,”
Ere Labor outed Lard.
The lower orders knew their place,
When he was young and hearty,
But now they asked a living wage!
He cursed the Labor Party!
“Scared Capital,” he said, “has gone,
And sought some foreign strand!
And, if we are not careful, Death
Will also flee this land!
This thing they call a ‘living wage,’
For which the mob’s been crying,
Is really what has brought about
The extra cost of dying!”
VICTORIA.
HARRISON O.

VARIOUS VERST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Lettice.
A star died upon her yellow bed
When morning wakened all the trams to
sing;
One last mosquito shrilling round my head
Wobbled a faint and disapnointed sting;
The early milkman howled like any-
thing ....
And I was dreaming that Lettice was wed.
Lettice, whose hot kiss burned against my
own,
Whose small brown hand had fluttered
so in mine,
Whose voice was as clear reed-pipes over-
blown
By young crisp breezes that the rains
refine
In happy springtime, when the closed
buds pine
For warm bee-kisses that their dreams have
known!
Lettice. whose eyes were like soft wells
made still
£t ev , enin K in s <> m e holy place remote,
u ben o er the pine-fringe of a sleeping hill
One happy cloud, sun-painted, seems to
float
Freighted with all desire—a golden boat
Whose sails the longings of the silence fill.
Lettice, whose dark hair from its wavy
braid,
Down-falling o’er her shoulders, seemed
' - to make
shadow in midmost heart of shade
Ihan all the darkness oldest Night can
shake
Out her dusky apron for the sake
Uf timid love that wooes a trembling maid.
Lettice, whose name was as a fragrance shed
A .°l er J- ose gardens when the petals fall
the hot sun-kiss, and the earth is red
v\ ith fainting sweetness by the moss-
grown wa11....
So faintly sweet the soft word holds me
thrall. ...
And I was dreaming that Lettice was wed.
Clear morning nursed the young day on her
knee.
Along the wires the trams began to sing,
To-1??!. m °SQuit o spread his wings to flee,
Wobbling a faint and disappointed
sting. ...
And scarce I recked of what the day
should bring,
For I had dreamt Lettice was wed to me!

D.M.W.
The Opium Pipe.
Upon a mat the Chinese smoker lay,
Resting his head
On bags of straw, beside a wooden tray
With quaint bowls spread,
And having filled his long-bulbed pipe, he
burned
Raw opium,
Inhaled sweet smoke, forgot the world, and
yearned
For dreams to come.
He did not see the haggard girls and men
Sprawl on the floor,
Nor that impassive keeper of the den
Guarding the door,
But down the pleasant river of his dream
He glided on,
Until he saw the colored lanterns gleam
In old Canton.
He watched the rice-fields, waterfalls and
rills,
The shine and shiver
Of slender bamboos, and the White Cloud
hills,
His own Pearl River.
Then, crooked streets with lacquered shops
arrayed,
Dim camphor-trees,
Flowery pagodas, gates of carven jade,
Brown junks, grey seas ;
Fantastic banners, coolies rushing by,
Boys flying kites,
Ancestral tombs, a dragon in the sky,
All strange delights,
A world created by some drowsy djinn,
Till sleep’s caress
Blotted the colors and the outlines in
Forgetfulness....
Mute, motionless, the seller of cheap dreams
Guarded the door.
Pale men and women, in the lamp’s dim
gleams,
Sprawled on the floor,
Unconscious of the jarring world, withdrawn
From dreamless folk ;
When, startled by a glimmer of the dawn,
The sleeper woke.
He left the den of dreams at break of day,
Unlucky he,
To change a bright illusion for the grey
Reality.
VICTORIA.
LOUIS ESSON.
Vigil.
By the wicker gate of love,
In the shadow of the pines,
In a transport of desire,
I have waited many nights—
Waited, dreaming sweet delights,
In my heart a glowing fire,
Watched a roseate star that shines
In phantasmal blue above.
I have stript my soul of sin,
Cleansed my heart and purged my mind ;
I am fit to woo you, quite;
I’ve a tale that must be told !
All without is dark and cold.
All within aglow with light—
Your dear shadow on the blind ;
Will you bid me enter in?
n.s.w.
W. M. WHITNEY.

THE INEVITABLE PEST.
We were conducted to the exact spot upon which will stand the Parliament House of Australia. It was occupied, gentlemen,
by two rabbits (laughter.) I saw them both.-Rider Hazard.

By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty-
Six Shillings Sterling you can have The Bulletin
posted regularly to you every week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., which should be crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to The
Manager.
How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some
care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertxsemenls, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention. .
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin, is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of. officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
lUSTh'e contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the form
of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short
Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Australian sub-
jects, and not exceeding two columns in length, or,
say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded with-
out stamps for return it will be understood that the
copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Personal
applications for return of MS. cannot receive atten-
tion. The Bulletin desires, further, those sending
MS. to distinctly understand that it is not respon-
sible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs In Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash, of
sufficient merit and interest and adapted for direct
reproduction, or any interesting photograph illus-
trative of a current event, will be paid for same on
acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea, but
unfitted for direct reproduction—i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, MAY 1, 1913.
SOME FURTHER ASPECTS, GRAVE
AND GAY, OF THE REFERENDA
SHINDY.
Out of the tornado of oratorical blither
which has been raging during the past
month, two main anti-Referenda arguments
emerge. The S. M. Herald mentioned one
of them recently, as follows: —
The Government says it cannot grapple with trusts
and monopolies, and it has no proper control of in-
dustrial conditions throughout the Commonwealth.
But, as Mr. W \t>e rightly insists, this is only be-
cause the Federal Government ivill not exercise or
help to exercise, powers which already exist.
Any stray or hedge-Liberal will be fiercely
delighted to supply the other. It is one of
those easily-assimilated political catch-cries
or gags which are a perfect boon to old
women, bagmen and others who hate to put
an undue strain on their minds. The gist
of it is that, by a piece of extraordinary
good fortune, the Australian Parliament
cannot “grapple with trusts,” and “has no
proper control of industrial conditions
throughout the Commonwealth.” The pre-
datory and dangerous body which the people
of Australia elect triennially on a universal
suffrage basis thought it had these powers,
but Chief Justice Griffith taught it bet-
ter. According to the habitual voicer of
ready-made gags, if this state of affairs is
altered there will be Chaos —in fact, “Social-
ism.” (While on the subject of political
gags, it is suggestive to remark how people
who live uncomplainingly in the very midst
of Socialism, who travel in Socialistic trains
and trams, wash in Socialistic water, send
their children to Socialistic schools,
clamor periodically for Socialistic ferries,
gas, bridges and the like, still retain a sort
of vague, theoretic dread of the exploded old
Horror.) The trouble which is forecasted
by these persons as a result of the carriage
of the Constitutional amendments asked for
by the Australian Government is such as
has never been experienced by any civilised
State on this earth. It is suggested that
the well-meaning bourgeois legislators whom
Australia is in the habit of electing, will
suddenly suffer an appalling mental and
moral change. Regardless of their well-paid
jobs, of their wives and families, of the re-
spect of their fellows, and of the ultimate
vengeance of a scandalised Providence,
they will start robbing people right and left
by the simple process of nationalising the
nation’s businesses. No one will be safe once
these demented ruffians get fairly into their
stride. A prominent Liberal speaker enu-
merated, the other day, 127 different indus-
tries which might be utterly devastated, in
N. S. Wales alone, if the Trade and Com-
merce clause was altered. In the heat of
argument, the lower order of Liberal goes
further, and states definitely that, if the
Australian Parliament is given the necessary
power, these things will be done; though it
is possible to indicate in all the ages, and all
over the civilised world, all manner of Par-
liaments, including that of Great Britain,
no better, take them all for all, than the
Commonwealth’s, in which full power to do
the wildest and most freakish things existed,
but was never used.
* * * *
The soundest way to meet the ridiculous
contention that the Federal Parliament has
power to properly control trusts and indus-
trial conditions throughout the Common-
wealth, and that accordingly there is no
present need to “tinker with the Constitu-
tion,” is to cite a few of the things which
it admittedly can’t do. For example, it
can’t insist on the registering of a mark
upon manufactured goods, by means of which
the consumer may learn whether his pur-
chases are the fruits of union or non-union
labor. It can’t do anything to compel the
Australian manufacturer to pay his em-
ployees adequate wages in return for the
tariff protection which he enjoys. And it
can’t repress State monopolies which tend to
restrain trade to the public detriment. A
Liberal Government, under Alfred Deakin,
put measures aiming at all these badly-
needed reforms on the statute book, but the
High Coqrt declared them to be invalid. A
Deakin administration tried to establish an
effective Federal Arbitration Court. It was
frustrated owing to the interpretation put
by the High Court on Parliament’s powers
under the Constitution. The Australian Par-
liament is impotent as regards any business
which nominally confines itself to one State,
no matter how injurious that business may
be to Australia at large. Thus, a large
body of ultra-pietists regard Tattersall’s
sweep as one of the last words in devilish-
ness. The Fisher Government has a few
members of this sainted brigade in its ranks.
Spurred on by them it has made repeated
assaults on Tattersall’s. In vain. Safely
entrenched behind the State Rights of Tas-
mania the big gambling concern is in a posi-
tion to cheerfully defy Fisher and his
myrmidons. The situation would be the same
if Tattersall’s were an Oil Trust, or a
Beef Trust. It may be said that the differ-
ent States have power to repress injurious
combines. But the experience of the only
other country whose Constitution closely
resembles ours is that individual States
cherish their Trusts, even though the nation
at large groans under them. A big Trust
is one of the most paying affairs that a small
State can have about the premises. Both
Labor and Liberal parties in the Federal
sphere feature on their platforms an Insur-
ance Bill after the Lloyd-George model.
This particular variety of Socialism insists
that the employer and the employee, as well
as the State, shall contribute weekly sums
towards workers’ sickness and unemploy-
ment pensions. Without an amendment of
our Trade and Commerce clause,, along the
lines suggested by the Fisher Government,
any measure of the sort would be declared
invalid the moment it came before the High
Court. Parliament wastes its time, to all
intents and purposes, over measures such as
the Seamen’s Comnensation Act. There
has been talk, of late, of irrigating the
Northern Territory. Provided the water came
from sources that were indisputably inside the
Territory, all would be well. But if, by
any chance the fluid which percolates from
(say) the Musgrave Ranges or the Deering
Hills were drawn upon, South Australian
State Righters would certainly arise in a
condition of fury and protest against the
infamy, and the Commonwealth’s third Par-
liament would endorse their action. Avia-
tion is going to be a feature of everyday life
within the next few years. It is an absurd
anomaly that, though the British Parlia-
ment has power to pass an Aviation Act
binding all over the United Kingdom, the
Australian Parliament has no power to pass
a uniform Aviation Act for the benefit of
Australia. It is profitless to extend this
dreary list further. As an outcome of what
Boutmy, in his “Studies in Constitutional
Law,” has termed that most “striking politi-
cal oaradox —the supremacy of a small oli-
garchy of non-elected, unremovable judges
in a democracy,” the Australian Parliament
is, so far as matters of industrial and trade
reform are concerned, practically impotent. It
has less power than it thought it had ; it has
less power than the people meant it to have:
it has less power than the rabidest anti-Fed-
eralist of 13 years ago feared that it might
have, and was darned glad that it didn’t
have.
★ * * *
A far greater constitutional authority
than J. Cook or C. G. Wade —in fact, one
of the greatest that the world has known,
made this profound remark: —
There ought to be in every Constitution an avail-
able authority somewhere. The sovereign power must
be come-at-able.
The whole trouble with the Australian Con-
stitution, as interpreted by a majority of the
High Court, is that it is not “come-at-able.”
Under it the sovereign power, which is to
say the will of the people,' is denied fulfil-
ment in scores of vitally important direc-
tions. Practically every section of the com-
munity favors the “New Protection.” A
Deakin Government originated it. The
Laborites of that period supported it. Lib-
erals of the calibre of W. H. Irvine, of
Victoria, and J. Garland,, of N. S. Wales,
acclaimed it. The Fusion people made
it one of their few planks in
1909. But three lawyers out of a
bench of five said that it must not be. The
Australian Industries Preservation Act
(1907) was a Deakin measure of which
every democrat in the Commonwealth ap-
proved. The same three lawyers declared
that Australia must not have it. If one ma,t
judge by the fact that both the big political
parties are going baldheaded for an Insur-
ance Act, the temper of the country is in
favor of invalidity and out-of-work pensions.
But the country is going' to have no say in
this urgent matter. The arbitrament will
be with a little band of lawyers. And as
time goes on, more reforms which only the
Australian Parliament can attempt will ob-
trude themselves. And always the lawyers
above-mentioned will decide as to whether
or no Australia is to be allowed to have
them. Can any more imbecile situation be
imagined? The sole coherent objection to a
change, that is forthcoming from any quar-
ter, is that the Australian Parliament, if
given enlarged powers over trade and com-
merce, may abuse those powers. The more
unintelligent type of Liberal imagines the
wildest impossibilities as adumbrated above.
And every type of Liberal imagines some-
thing calamitous; or, if he doesn’t, he affects
to, for party purposes. The answer to such
idiotic assumptions is that reasonable people
do not insist on their full pound of flesh, so
to speak, in the matter of doing all that they
are legally entitled to do. Bageiiot has
drawn our attention to a few of the things
that the British sovereign could do if he or
she cared to exercise the prerogative. The
King could, without consulting Parliament,
disband the army; dismiss all the officers
from the General Commanding-in-Chief
downwards ; dismiss all the sailors, and sell
the navy and naval stores; make a peace by
the sacrifice of Cornwall, and begin a war
for the conquest of Brittany; make every
citizen in the United Kingdom, male or
female, a peer; make every parish school in
the kingdom a “university”; pardon all
offenders, and so forth. Of course, no sove-
reign would dream of doing any of these
things. The modern monarch keeps his hand
with the utmost attention upon the public
pulse. Similarly, a modern Ministry which
was handed the battle-axe of office on the
understanding that it was to pass certain
measures of reform, and, for the rest, com-
port itself, in all things, in a sane and equit-
able fashion, would be one of the last insti-
tutions on earth to outrage the conscience of
the nation by wantonly bringing ruin upon
the business of electors whose only offence lay
in the fact that they were prosperous. In the
language of Ibsen, “People don’t do such
things”—least of all, politicians in the enjoy-
ment of a minimum stipend of £6OO per
annum, and free railway passes.
[John Bede Dalley, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Literary Hunger Strike.
On Thursday last a number of Liberal
dailies downed ink-bottles, so to speak, _as
regards editorial comment on the coming
Federal elections. They did it by way of
protest against what they have somewhat
bellowdramatically christened the “Press
Gag.” The alleged gag is, of course, the innoc-
uous provision in the Federal Electoral Act
which enjoins that for some weeks before each
election all Federal political matter must be
signed by the writer. This strike is very
similar to that with which the British Suf-
fragettes have familiarised the world. The
papers want to write about politics almost as
much as the Suffragettes want food. Like the
Suffragettes, they are refraining from that
which they yearn for, in the expectation that
a scandalised nation will come to their assist-
ance. It is all Collins-street to a fragment
of road-metal that the scheme will prove
futile. Consider, first, the factor of the
public. Speaking in a general way, the
great heart of the populace would remain
unmoved if the papers never wrote another
political article in their lives. A few people
would be embarrassed —chiefly the sort of
tiresome nuisances who, having no ideas of
their own, are driven to depend on the press
for the stock political gags which they fire
at their exasperated acquaintances. None of
the rest would be half as pained as they
would be if the beer supply failed tempo-
rarily, or if wet weather rendered the foot-
ball season, a failure. Many of them
wouldn’t even know of the calamity, were
their attention not attracted by ceaseless
moans on the subject in the news-columns of
the journals concerned. And what of the
politicians? An instructive line as to their
views on this question may be got from the
fact that not one of them, whether Libera,
or Labor, has pledged either his party or
himself to battle for the abolition of the
so-called infamy in the event of his being
returned to Parliament. This particular “in-
terference with the Briton’s inalienable privi-
lege of free comment,” and so forth, is, of
course, right into the politicians’ hands. It
operates, primarily, to protect them from
abuse and misrepresentation. There is a
common variety of political writer —the
suave scandal-monger of journalism —who,
while capable of tieing. absolutely excoriating
under a pen-name, is almost clerical in his
mild benignity over his own f signature. No
one will be more delighted than the politician
to see him brought into the open. Again,
the experience of the last Federal by-elec-
tion proved that candidates were far better
and more fairly reported where the reporters
had to put their names to their efforts. This
is an elementary proposition in human nature.
A writer doesn’t much mind what sort of
whiskers his editor wears, but when it comes
to appearing in such disfigurements himself,
it is another matter. And. above all, under
the new system, the politician, or, at any
rate, the Liberal politician, stands to get a
better advt. than he has ever enjoved
hitherto. He will be candidate and leader-
writer in one. The most sparkling phrases,
the profoundest utterances will be put into
his mouth. His grammar will be less dread-
ful than ever. By the time the unemployed
political leader-writers have finished sub-
editing him, for the furtherance of the Lib-
eral cause, he will be disposed to wonder
why, with his abilities, he is satisfied to fol-
low a comparative illiterate such as Joseph
Cook. In short, the daily papers have nr,
earthly prospect of bludgeoning the commn
nity into subjection by means of the strike
weapon, for the reason that, in the main
the community is, in its blindness, rather
pleased about the trouble than otherwise
Remains the ethical side of the question
For years and years the daily press of Aus
tralia has been mentioning, with manlv
frankness, its priceless educational value t
the public. The Sydney papers, for examnle
have frequently grown quite weak in the
knees at the bare idea of some ill-disposed
person interfering with that part of their
vocation. Their stated objections to the
discontinuance, by the See Government of
the free carriage of newspapers on ’the
N.S.W. railways, had nothing to do with
sordid business. The outrage went deeper-
also higher. It concerned the popular in-
tellect. The proprietaries saw in this mad
action a wanton blow at the very brain of
the populace. That is to say, they said thev
did. If in the expression of these and simi-
lar views they were insincere; if the sheets
which fought against the abolition of an
inequitable old system whereunder the State
of N. S. Wales paid their carriage account
did so on purely business lines; if, i n their
hearts, they do not believe that they are of
anv educational value to the people; then
they will continue the cruel and insensate
strike on which they have embarked. If, on the
other hand, they believe in Liberalism, aud
in their self-imposed task of instilling the
principles of that most elusive and baffling
creed into the minds and hearts of the elec-
tors, they will swallow their pride and get
back to work. If the principles of Liberal-
ism are of any value, and if the press has
any influence, every hour that this strike
lasts represents a serious public wrong.
Ignorance as regards the aims and meaning
of Liberalism is falling upon the habitual
leader-student like clods. Already he is
beginning to forget whether, for the purposes
of the coming election, he is an anti-Protec-
tionist or an anti-Federalist, or an anti-Con-
scriptionist, or an anti-Australian navyite, or
an anti-Socialist, or what. Presently ’he
will not care. And then unquestionably
Armageddon will be near, and eternal Sheol
more or less adjacent.
[John Bede Dalley, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]
Liberalism and Defence—and Anti-
Defence.
It is the custom of Conservatives all over
the world to oppose every new idea that is
advanced, and, later, when that idea has
been generally accepted as a sound one, to
claim blandly that they approved of it all
along. Reasonable people recognise the
desirability of this procedure. Even stu-
pidity is not eternal; and it is always well
that converts should be allowed to save their
faces wherever possible. But it is one of
the unwritten laws of the game, so to speak,
that the somersault shall not be executed
either too soon or too blatantly. Free-
traders have been turning unobtrusively
into advocates of “tariff peace” (which is
to say into Protectionists) all over Aus-
tralia of late years. No one has reminded
them of the days when they fought elec-
tions on the fiscal issue, and talked of Free-
trade as “a sacred cause,” a “cause that
was worth living and dying for,” and so
forth. One reason was that these ex-Free-
traders didn’t offensively claim very shortly
after their metamorphosis that they had
believed in a high tariff for Australia all
along, and that, as a matter of fact, it was
only the machinations of the Protectionists
which had delayed the utter ruin of Cobden-
ism. Liberalism has shown no such seemly
restraint in the matter of the defence ques-
tion. Actually the party which is led by
Joseph Cook, and which numbers in its
ranks Bruce Smith, W. H. Kelly, W. E.
Johnson, et al., has started, at this inde-
cently early hour, to claim that it founded
compulsory military service (the system
which every one of the above politicians has
denounced, quite recently, as “conscription,”
and “an un-English interference with the
liberty of the subject”) ; and that it origin-
ated the Australian navy idea. For ex-
ample, the S. M. Herald had the imperial
nerve to print the following last week: —
Some people argue that the Federal Labor party
deserves credit, because of its attitude towards Aus-
tralian defence. Here, again, however, we are up
against the party wall. The Commonwealth Govern-
ment, now on its trial, came into power with the
present defence system practically in being. The
Labor party had discovered that the people of Aus-
tralia were in deadly earnest about this vital ques-
tion. and that no appeal for their votes would count
for much unless there was a definite endorsement oI
the Liberal policy and programme.
The recital of a few bald historical facts is
the best corrective of this sort of mendacity.
In 1908-09 a Fisher Government held office.
In the forefront of its platform stood coastal
and land defence. There wasn’t an Anti-
Socialist” in those days who didnt howl
“conscription” every time he heard the word-
“national service.” The Liberal alternativ
to 'the navy proposed by the Govern-
ment was not some other _ sort ot local
navy, but an increased subsidy to Britain.
According to .Liberalism, the whole m e a 0
local fleets was tactically wrong. JVDu -
was quoted as an opponent of anything o
the sort. Also, various British admiral ■>
and Bruce Smith, and the presidents o
innumerable Liberal Women’s Leagues,
opposition to the axiom that Australia s -
as a nation, would be decided, not in t
waters, but in the North Sea, was brusneu
impatiently aside, in Liberal circles, as
tious and uninformed. The visit. ot .
United State’s fleet had stirred the lmag na
tion of the Commonwealth. The very suburb
were beginning to opine, hesitatingly, ,
the place was big enough to have a Mi f
its own. But Parliamentary Conservat •
astigmatic to the last, failed to P.ee it.
and his followers seized upon the
.war scare (a scare which we k noW . son
have been baseless, and which there 1 ‘ .
to suspect, had its disreputable or g
the Continental armament-shops) t
the Dreadnought agitation. This, a
succeeded, must, of course, have d -
Laborites,” since it would have amounted
a plain admission that -while me for
defence was all very well as a pa e y
swollen-headed young colonies w jj ave
to burn, the real business of war , e j, a if
to be undertaken on this count
on the other side of the earth. j n a
GRAPH well expressed the a J )OV f rn ua point-
cartoon, which represented Australia
ing to a Dreadnought, and say mg. w |th
to Fisher, who was depicted piayms


a toy boat: “That’s all very well for amuse-
ment, Andy, but this is what we will have
to rely on to keep the flag flying.” About
this time more than one Liberal M.P. stated,
amid cheers, that if a tinpot Australian
navy was built there would ue an attempt by
local “Disloyalists” to turn the guns on the
Mother-Country’s warships. Joseph Cook,
on being asked his naval policy, at a public
meeting, replied: “My policy is to give a
Dreadnought to England—and, if neces-
sary, 20 Dreadnoughts.” G. S. Little-
john, of the Sydney Chamber of Commerce,
in a speech (1./4/’O9) dealing with the
local navy question, compared the Govern-
ment to a demented father who set out to
teach his child the art of war:—
How foolish this is when a full-grown man, fully
equipped and trained, is willing to fight the child’s
battles for him in consideration of reasonable pay-
ment.
The Sydney Telegraph wrote (11/5/’O9)
as follows —and one might quote 20 pro-
nouncements similar to it from any given
Liberal journal of the period :
Mr. Cook and Mr. Deakin have both pronounced
publicly against Mr. Fisher’s naval policy. They
have both pronounced in favor of giving a particular
kind of assistance to the Imperial navy at the pre-
sent juncture, and the particular kind of assistance
which they desire to offer is entirely different from
the scheme to which Mr. Fisher stands committed.
Everybody knows what followed. The
dead failure of the Dreadnought and
increased subsidy movement taught the
Fusion that Fisher’s naval policy (really,
of course, though few Liberals, at that time,
appreciated it, the policy of the British
Admiralty) was the one the country wanted.
After ousting Fisher and his colleagues
from' office, the Fusionists got feverishly
busy with the identical Defence policy which
a few brief weeks before they had denounced
as foolish and disloyal. The staggering
defeat they endured at the next Federal
elections was some slight indication of Aus-
tralia’s opinion of their belated conversion
from the old-time doctrines of hired navies
and voluntary military service.
