♦
Cash, and Loans and Liabilities and
Principles and Sundries.
A few shiploads of money will have to be
spent on Sydney within the next 10 years, if
the place insists on swallowing people and
trade at the same old rate. The expensive
railway problem is still being grappled with
—or, perhaps, it would be more correct to
say that it is being pushed about and asked
to move on. Then, hovering in the back-
ground, are the financial nightmares of the
street tunnels or burrows, and the everlast-
ing North Shore Bridge. On top of all this
comes the statement of C. C. Lance, Presi-
dent of the Sydney Harbor Trust, before
the Dominions’ Royal Commission, that new
wharf facilities, the estimated cost of which
is £6,500,000, will be required inside the
next 10 years. If the stupendous job were
started now, it would be . completed just
when required; but, even in this time of
bounding prosperity, there is no money
available for a new lot of immense jobs, and
no immediate prospect of getting the money.
Only a few months ago, the financial pit-
cher that went too often to the pawnshop
well cracked with a loud report, and Gov-
ernment jobs everywhere dried and shed
their workmen. The golden trickle has since
been renewed in some measure; but it is
a very costly trickle (some of it is costing
£4 7s. per cent.), and the expenditure of the
reckless McGowen Government is so far
ahead of revenue. (£1,731,755 in the first
nine months of this financial year) that any
honest person with a regard for the future
prays that Cohenloup, of England, will
keep the brake on hard. McGowen, appar-
ently, does not know where the brake is ; in
fact, there is a suspicion that he has never
looked for it. As the seasons seem to be
arranged by Providence on the switchback
principle, and as Australia has been on the
crest of the wave for several years, it seems
reasonable to assume that the next 10 years
will not be more bountiful than those just
past. Even with trade flourishing every-
where, N. S. Wales is now borrowing at
top speed, and the steam is fairly thunder-
ing from the escape valve; but, even though
it is working at a daneerous pressure, it
can hardly keep up with the jobs it already
has on hand. Where are the mTlions for
the big fancy schemes to come from, and
what are they going to cost ultimately in
interest, curses and tears? The cost of mak-
ing a human anthill is a progressive sort of
madness. Still, if the Government resumed
the land around Port Stephens and Twofold
Bay, and built the two scraps of railway
that are required to open them up, and con-
structed a wharf or two, there would arise
two liew cities of which the State would be
sole ground landlord and sole collector of
ground rents. And the income from the land
would pay for wharves, docks, dredges,
breakwaters gnd every other old thing, and
yet leave a large and increasing profit. And
the people thereabouts, being allowed to ex-
port and import by tbe most adjacent port
instead of by the most remote one, would
save a vast amount in needless and absurd
railway freights. And the empty regions
north and south would begin to be settled,
which would add enormously to the value of
the public estate. And the McGowen
Government would escape from the suspicion
of being a mere agent or buttoner for the
private landlords of Sydney, and might almost
begin to live up to its pretence 9f being an
advocate of the public ownership of land.
But, unfortunately, the McGowen Ministry
appears to be so securely nailed down to'the
interests of the big private landlords of Syd-
ney that it isn’t worth while arguing with that
effete institution. Even though there are
millions to be made and millions to be saved
by adhering to its own alleged principles .of
decentralisation and public land ownership,
it wouldn’t, so far as the evidence goes, be
found dead in the same paddock with its own
alleged principles. Apparently it hates its
own alleged principles worse than a terrace
of devils.
The Mad Railway Gauges.
The Federal ownership of railways has
become a live question again. As a matter
of fact, it is of far more importance than
nine out of ten of the problems that are
shoved into the front of the electoral con-
test. The 4ft. BMn. Federal line (appar-
ently the mystery of that absurd odd 4in.
will never be cleared up), is actually being
built between Port Augusta and Kaleoorlie.
It will join on to a State-owned 3ft. bin.
line at one end, and at the other it will
connect with a State-owned 3ft. Gin. line,
also two State-owned sft. 3in. lines, likewise
another State-owned 3ft. Gin. system, and,
in between the State-owned sft. 3in. line and
the State-owned 3ft. 6in. line, it will have
a certain amount of intercourse with a
State-owned 4ft. 85m. line. And the
whole thing being a stupendous jest, and
the States being unable to decide how they
will apportion the bill for the obliteration
of the jest, the need for the central authority
to take the matter in hand becomes obvious.
Of course, the N.S.W. idea is that the other
States should conform to its gauge of four
feet, and half a foot, and two inches, and a
fool of an odd half inch, and should do
it at their own expense. And two of the
other States feel inclined to reply that as, so
far as they are concerned, N.S.W. was the
cause of the whole trouble, it should pay
their bill and say no more about it. The old
Ma State, in its vast pride because it was
lagged first and was the possessor of Sydney
Harbor, could never quite bring itself to
keep faith in its dealings with its neighbors.
It broke the agreement re the acceptance' of
the first and the second Federal Constitu-
tions. It broke the early agreement that
there should be a common tariff and inter-
state freetrade, and it built the first line of
border customs houses. And it tore up the
agreement re the uniform railway gauge. At
the beginning of things the first three rail-
way-building States —S.A., Victoria and
N.S.W. —decided to take the advice of
British authorities, and the 4ft. ar-
rangement was decided upon. Then N.S.W.
consulted an Irish railway engineer, who
enthused over the sft. 3in. idea which pre-
vails in his country, and the Ma State noti-
fied its neighbors that it was making a new
departure on its own responsibility. In the
vastness of its dignity as the place that was
lagged first it assumed certain special rights
in the way of dictation. S.A. and Victoria
grumbled a little at the extra cost and the
arrogance, but they gave way, and started
building vigorously on the sft. 3in. policy.
As a result Adelaide and Melbourne are the
only two State capitals between which the
train runs without a break. Meanwhile
N.S.W., owing to temporary brevity or
dwarfishness in the cash, hung up its rail-
way works. When it resumed business the
Irish engineer was gone, and a new man
brought about a return to the original 4ft.
BMn. idea —-the one on which the three
leading States started work, and which
N.S.W. had bounced its neighbors into re-
jecting. After that—as S.A. and Victoria
were too deeply committed to draw back—
every State went its own way, and chaos,
in the shape of railway gauges which vary
from 2ft. to s|ft., supervened. Now Vic-
toria and S.A. are urged to alter their rail-
ways to the N.S.W. measurement, at a cost
of over £6,000,000 apiece; and Queensland is
advised to do the same, and is told that its
bill will be over £12,000,000 ; and Westralia
is invited to,fall into line at a cost of nearly
£11,000,000, 1 while the arrogant Ma State,
which caused all the trouble by its chronic
inability to go straight, can put its one or
two odd-gauge lines on to the 4ft. 84in. basis
at a cost of £120,000. Naturally the States
which are not Ma look coldly on the proposi-
tion, and reckon that Ma ought to pay a
great deal more of the invoice than it, seem-
ingly, proposes to do. The uniform gauge
is plainly essential, but unless the central
authority takes hold of the job it is never
likely to come to _ pass. The question of
the invoice will be in the road all the time.
[James Edmond, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]
The Imperishable Strike.
The Bulletin’s Stipendiary Pessimist
writes gloomily:—
For sheer, blighted futility I readily hand the palm
to the quarrel which raged during portion of last
week between C. G. Wade and the McGowen party
on the subject of Strikes. Wade claimed that there were
only 203 strikes during his regime, as against 2156
under the McGowen Administration. (The above
figures may not be meticulously accurate, but they
are near enough.) The McGowen gang said that
Wade was a roarer, and that, owing to their masterly
handling of the State’s affairs, there had been but
300 or 400 strikes in their time, while Wade had
had to cope with unnumbered thousands of these un-
pleasantries.
The imbecility of this controversy lies in the plain
fact that, broadly speaking, Governments have no-
thing to do with the incidence of strikes. A good
Arbitration Act sympathetically administered by a
capable bench will, of course, avert a few. Reason-
able employers, and employees who are too hard-
headed to be stampeded into trouble, without good
cause, will prevent more. But nothing will wipe
them out to any great extent. They are features of
frail and erring humanity, as inevitably recurrent
as family rows. Anyone who takes the trouble to
study the history of strikes, here and elsewhere, will
see that they run in cycles. To state the case in
the jargon of the statistician; the culmination of the
prosperity curve precedes the culmination of the full
employment curve by about 12 to 18 months. It pre-
cedes the culmination of the industrial unrest curve
by from 18 months to two years. This rule is pretty
well invariable everywhere. For example, in N. S.
Wales in ’BB and ’B9 prosperity had reached its
highest point. Everything seemed right. The
Treasury was almost bursting. The banks had tons
of money to lend on almost any old lack of security.
There was a mighty land-boom in progress. Business
men were turning over their capital like wild-fire.
Everyone had cash in his pocket; or, at the worst,
a scheme in his head to get cash out of someone
else’s pocket. By the end of ’B9 the position of the
N.S.W. employee was amazingly good. There was
hardly any of him out of work, and all were earning
high wages as wages went in those days. Followed
the Wharf Laborers’ Strike, the Shearers’ Strike and
a host of others. Then came the bank smashes, the
explosion of the land boom and slump and misery—
and a decade of industrial‘peace.
To come nearer home, so to speak, the culmina-
tion of Australia’s most recent era of prosperity may
be fixed somewhere about the end of 1911. There
was a flaming and unabashed land-boom on then.
Hustling agents were selling suburban blocks to con-
genital mugs at anything from thrice the value of
the same upwards. Bogus companies were being un-
loaded on the populace at a terrific pace. Ridicu-
lous prices were being asked and obtained for sta-
tion properties. About February, 1912, the banks
started shutting down on the overdrafts —an ominous
move indicating that the prosperity wave had rolled
as far as it intended to go. Employment was at its
best and brightest about the middle of 1912. The
industrial unrest period is due here about now; in
fact, it may be said to be with us already.
These movements are quite natural, and nothing
but an alteration of the human character will correct
them. The employer, at the height of the pro-
sperity curve, comes to the conclusion —unless he is
a very thrifty citizen indeed —that he can do himself
and his family better than ever before. He accord-
ingly proceeds to enlarge his house, buy motor-cars,
give big and widely advertised entertainments, and
in general to hit things up. After a while his em-
ployees begin to take notice of his affluence. They
go after some share in it, and usually get what they
seek without undue effort. The employer finds that
his profits are thereby a little decreased. But he
still has to keep up his enlarged establishment, his
motors and his new social habits. So he sacks a few
men, That represents the small beginnings of un-
employment. Meanwhile, the employees, having
tasted financial blood, lust for more. They demand
a fresh rise. The employer, harassed by the problem
of how to maintain a £lO,OOO per annum establish-
ment on a miserable £l5O per week, rebuffs them.
They strike. The slump begins. An interesting ex-
ample of this tendency is observable in Germany to-
day. Under the uncompromising Bismarck tariff
that erstwhile poor and arid land has leaped to
vast wealth. The German has ceased, in consequence,
to be the saving, slaving person he was. Berlin is
gayer to-day, than Paris ever was. The inhabitants
stagger the remainder of humanity by the splendor
of their shows, and the luxury of their hotels and
restaurants. At Monte Carlo and such like resorts
the German has completely ousted the Englishman
as a scatterer of gold, and he is a serious rival, in
this respect, of the American and the Russian. The
explanation is that Germany, just now, is at the
height of its prosperity curve. Presently that slump
which, sooner or later, hits all people who live above
their incomes, will come; and then the Wades of
Germany will arise and exclaim wildly that it
wouldn’t have happened had they been in power,
while the McGowens of the period will howl, in
answer, that things were far, far worse in the Wade
time and that if such human calamities had to
handle the present situation there would be national
repudiation and insolvency and utter ruin. For the
purposes of the absurd old party game such pro-
testations are, no doubt, of some value. But to
thinking folks they are as the babbling of small
children.
The Complaint of the Inland Towns.
A Bendigo manufacturer, talking to Vic.
State Ministers and Railway bosses the
other day, made out a fair case for fair treat-
ment of country industries, as compared with
the concessions made to Melbourne influence.
He pointed out that the freight from Ben-
digo to Echuca (54 miles) is £1 3s. 3d. per
ton, and from Melbourne to Echuca (154
miles) it is £3 Os. 9d. per ton, which would
be equitable if it stayed at that. But a
rebate of £1 10s. 9d. is allowed on Melbourne
goods sent to Riverina, whereas the Bendigo
firms get no rebate whatever. The freight
from Adelaide to Nhill (235 miles) is
£2 Bs. Id. per ton; from Bendigo to Nhill
(228 miles) it is £4 7s. When John Mur-
ray remarked that he “couldn’t understand”
the granting of the Riverina rebate to Mel-
bourne, a Railway Commissioner artlessly ex-
plained that Bendigo has the advantage of
being so much nearer the Riverina trade,
whereby he admitted the very grievance
which the inland towns complain of. The
centralisation of manufactures in Melbourne
has been assisted by the Railway Depart-
ment with Government sanction. _ John
Murray seems to stand alone in his ina-
bility to account for the system of feeding
the fat hog from the leaner hogs’ trough.
Because Bendigo could naturally profit from
its comparative nearness to Echuca, the
manufacturers and traders of Melbourne
were advantaged by that rebate. To what
end is the Railwav freight concession on
goods sent “50 miles beyond Melbourne,” save
for the purpose of arresting the manufac-
tural development of up-country towns. A
Ballarat manufacturer complained that slack
coal from the State mine at Wonthaggi was
carried to Melbourne for id. per ton per mile,
but was charged nearly half as much again
between Melbourne and Ballarat. Melbourne
manufacturers pay only 3s. 2d. per
ton in freight for the State mine
coal on which Ballarat buyers pay
7s. 6d. To all these figures the
Acting-Premier replied, “Anyhow, the griev-
ances have almost reached a vanishing point.”
But the political folly of the endeavor to
strangle country-town industries, instead _ of
feeding and nursing them, is only beginning
to be perceived. The public grievance will
not reach a vanishing point until the State’s
immigration policy is logically associated
with an inland-town development policy.
Settlement on the land must radiate from
other industrial centres than Melbourne,
which seaport, by the way, is not surrounded
by land. Obviously, the rebates and conces-
sions hitherto' granted to Melbourne "would
be more wisely granted to Bendigo. Ballarat,
Castlemaine, etc. And, obviously, Melbourne
Trades Hall is in favor of preserving the old
order of things. Trades Hallism, nearly
everywhere in Australia, has developed under
a bad order of things, and is up against de-
centralisation all the Ume.

Land Aggregation in Maoriland.
The land question in Maoriland is a toler-
ably unhappy business. When Ballance
took office, in 1891, he did so with the main
object of straightening out, once and for all,
everything connected with the tenure of the
soil. Bub in grips with actual conditions
the straightening was only partial, and
instead of being as twisted and contorted
as the barbed-wire entanglements at Adrian-
ople, the land laws were made as compara-
tively free from bends as the hind leg of an
octopus or something. Ever since, a cer-
tain amount of tinkering has been going on,
and a moderate-sized political wrangle on
some minor point affecting the flax swamp
of John Smith, or the manuka scrubs occu-
pied by Percy Montmorency, can usually
be got moving when it is thought wise to direct
public attention from the monthly excursion
to Cohen’s pawnshop. Lately there was a
fierce controversy over the right of one
Brighton, of Southland, to acquire under
Massey’s recent legislation £40,000 worth
of coal for the price of a cheap suburban
allotment, and the Law has so far upheld
Massey’s contention that his legislation
didn’t really mean anything, and wouldn’t
have amounted to anything even if it had
been intended to mean something. Now the
Wellington Post has made the discovery
that large estates are being aggregated by
the piecing together of small settlement
areas quite recently purchased from the
Government. Out of the 14,374 acres held
by R. R. and W. A. Burling, at least two
small sections were State leaseholds, granted
within the last year or two, and since pur-
chased by the first occupiers to sell to the
Burling estate. This is tolerably bad busi-
ness. As between leasehold and freehold
tenures of land the argument isn’t worth
the toss of an elephant as long as there is a
land-tax on the premises to gather up occa-
sional bunches of unearned increment and
similar substances, but the aggregation of
big estates is quite another matter. Maori-
land is a small country, and the amount of
soil available to grow things will soon not
be nearly enough to go round. What is
wanted above all things is a limitation of
the areas that may actually be held. All
legislation passed within the last 20 years
recognises that Government must not lease
or sell to any one person more than 640
acres of first-class land, 2000 acres of
second-class land, or 5000 of third-class land.
The only exceptions are the sheep runs,
which are leased for comparatively short
terms, and which consist for the most part
of land which would be about fifteenth-class
if it were any class at all. Everybody knows
that 640 acres of really good Maoriland coun-
try will, if worked up to its reasonable limit,
provide a very fine living for a family.
Often, as in the Ellesmere district, 50 acres
will do so, but 640 acres of the right sort
is a really good estate. Why not get right to
work on that basis, and, by screwing up the
tail of the graduated tax in a whole-hearted
manner, make it absolutely impossible for
anybody to hold more than a reasonable
area? That is what is wanted, and the
people who talk big about reform, and only
mean some more petty tinkering, are fooling
with the whole question. Under the present
system the Government can go on buying
up land in big lumps, and leasing or selling
it in small sections right to the crack of
doom. Men will aggregate large estates as
long as it pays them to do it, and not an
hour longer. The object of any land reform
worth the name ought to be the making of
any big estate a hissing and a by-word and a
loud unseemly jest in the eyes of all rightly-
constituted people who have a due regard
for the value of twenty shillings.

From Westralia comes the allegation that
the local Labor Premier, a couple of State
Labor members, and a son of a Federal
Labor politician have, among them, taken
up about 800,000 acres in mostly first-class
country adjacent to that which is to be
traversed by the Transcontinental railway.
There is a 12in. rainfall in this locality;
also a big store of artesian water. The rent
of the land mentioned in the allegation is
the paltry one of 3s. per acre, and it is
estimated that, once the Transcontinental
gets going, the value of the allegated pro-
perty will be enormous. It is, of course,
possible that these gentlemen (if they did
as alleged) had no inside information as to
the probable route of Australian’s big rail-
way when they applied for the land. But
it will need some proving. When persons
connected with the stable of a Cup favorite
start “laying” the animal furiously on, say, 4
Monday, and it leaks out on the following
Wednesday that the steed is to be scratched
for all engagements owing to one of its legs
having gone hopelessly wrong or its head
having broken down —when that sort of
event happens the public, like the suspicious
and carping hound that it is, is liable to
allege bitterly that the bettors possibly knew
all about the ruined champion’s condition on
the preceding Sabbath or earlier. Similarly,
if the allegations hold water, the electors
will possibly argue that these Westralians
knew something about the prospective value
of land in the Eucla subdivision when they
went into the pastoral business there with
such extraordinary enthusiasm and compre-
hensiveness. There is no ethical distinction
between a Minister for Works, say, who de-
liberately uses his influence to run a tram
or a train through property which he has
acquired, and a Parliamentarian who uses
knowledge that has come his way owing to
his political position, to acquire property
through which or near which a railway is
to be built. Both cases represent the gam-
ing offence known as “betting on a cer-
tainty.” And when some (possibly baseless)
alligator comes along with a plain, straight-
out allegation that certain politicians have
bet on a certainty to the extent of about
800,000 acres the matter ought to be cleared
up-
One reason given by Melb. Argus
(24/4/T3) for refusing to publish two state-
ments from Attorney-General Hughes com-
menting upon some “utterances of Mr.
Cook” and upon the policy of the Liberals
in respect of old age pensions and the mater-
nity bonus, was the omission of Mr.
Hughes to append his true name and
address to his letter. In a dull spirit of
Tory irony, the Argus quoted “for the
Attorney-General’s enlightenment” the full
text of the section of the Electoral Act deal-
ing with the signing of articles, which sec-
tion, by the way, applies only to the printed
matter published in any newspaper, circular,
pamphlet or dodger, and not to the author’s
“copy.” But in alleging the absence of the
writer’s name and address from the foot of
his manuscript as a reason for refusing to
publish it, the fatuous newspaper said, “For
this, apart from other reasons.” The other
reasons were probably genuine enough. Had
Attorney-General Hughes signed his name
in blood taken from the mangled remains of
Modesty, or What?
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Jones has told me oft with pride
How the articles he writes
On its course this nation guide ;
I have listened many nights
While the brainy Jones, half-hidden in the
aromatic murk,
Smoked and told me they would never give
him credit for his work.
I have often pitied Jones,
Writing bravely in the dark;
Long I listened to his moans,
Thought his grudging boss a Nark.
Jones assured me in his leisure, and his
speech was curt and gruff,
He would go ahead like blazes if they’d let
him sign his stuff.
There were times when I had Doubts ;
But I quickly laid them low;
“Jones must have his talking bouts!
He is much-too big to blow!”
So I argued on occasions when the protests
of wild Jones
Had been bellowed at me, chanted, and
encored in minor tones.
Then there came the Signing Law,
And I softly said “Hooray!
This will stop his endless jaw.
Now each Jones must have his day!”
But the rage of Jones and comrades is just
terrible to see;
And I cannot find a reason for this wave of
modesty.
Jones assures me that his pars.
Are the work of many pens.
And his self-respect it jars,
For the others think like hens.
Then he says his paper’s viewpoint is
archaic and decayed,
And he wishes he had never taken to the
scribbling trade.
N.S.W.
E. O’F.

Truly the Freetrade isles of Britain are re-
garded by their own folk as good places to
get away from. Thus in 1911, the last year
for which complete returns are available,
the net exit amounted to 302,722 English
and Welsh, 88,858 Scotch, and 49,288 Irish.
And fragmentary reports of more recent date
suggest that the rush is growing. It is
announced by cable that last Saturday 4230
emigrants left the Clyde (Scotland) on
three ships for Canada and the United
States alone —both highly protected coun-
tries. Ships are constantly leaving the
Clyde and other Scotch ports for all man-
ner of countries, and this outpouring sug-
gests quite a rush. Scotland has a popula-
tion of between four and five millions, which
represents about 160 persons to the square
mile, and that is less than the pressure or
density of humanity in Austria, Belgium,
Denmark. France, Germany, Italy, Holland,
Switzerland, and various other countries.
On the general appearance of things Scot-
land shouldn’t -equire to get rid of its people
at quite such a strenuous rate. When over
4000 inhabitants flee in one day from one
port of one small country, all making for
two. Protectionist lands beyond the sea, things
begin to look busy.
“Southron” ’writes:—
Cabled that Premier Peake, in England, has pre-
sented a gold watch and chain to J. W. Billiet, one
of the four survivors of Stuart’s last expedition.
There might not be much in this, but for a tardy
announcement that the Government will provide
financially for all the survivors. Observe the con-
trast between the Burke and Wills muddle and
Stuart’s management. Stuart’s companions for the
last time (1861) were Auld, S. King, Heath, Nash,
McGorrerry and Billiet. It is remarkable, indeed,
that four survive, and Auld died only last year.
Stuart accompanied Sturt in the expedition of 1844-
5. In 1859 he notified that he was prepared to
undertake an expedition to the North to “open up
a line for rail and telegranhic communication with
England.” This was the third he had led. In
June, 1860, he got past the centre of the continent.
In May, 1861, he was afield for the fourth time.
Reached a point north of the southern extremity of
the Gulf of Carpentaria, where Burke and Wills had
b.een a few months earlier. You may think of
Amundsen and Scott. In this case, Burke, as the
Amundsen, met with the disaster. Stuart arrived
back in Adelaide in September, 1861. In about a
month his last expedition set forth. Reached the
North Coast, July, 1862 ; back in Adelaide, January,
1863.

Howling against the compulsory signing
of political matter during the stress of a
Federal election, the Argus (23/4/T3, the
day before the issue of writs) remarked: —
The correspondence columns of the press form a
medium through which electors may address each
other, often with most important results. But
when “Elector” and “Farmer” may not express
opinions or afford information on political issues
unless they sign their names and give their ad-
dresses, they will not write.
Precisely. It would never do to disclose the
fact that “Mallee Farmer,” “Disgusted
Unionist,” “Poor Typiste” and “Twice
Mother of Twins.” are one and the same
person, to wit a bloke employed on the staff
of the journal that prints the letters.

N.S.W. politician Wade, the hero of Mt.
Kembla. has struck a netv line of opposition
to the Referendum, and begs the people not
to grant the increased powers to the Aus-
tralian Parliament because the Constitu-
tion is essentially rotten. Deakin begs the
people to refuse the powers because they
would destroy the symmetry of the most
perfect Constitution that was ever made,
and it is only 13 years old. Deakin him-
self had it amended long before it was 13
years old; but, of course, that merely in-
creased the already perfect symmetry. Cook
pleads on the same lines as Deakin at the
beginning of his speeches, but generally
admits about the middle, or when entering
the straight for the final peroration, so to
speak, that he is in favor of “reasonable
amendments.” What his reasonable amend-
ments are like no one apparently knows. No
pne has explained them. And as Cook him-
self has not explained them, he, presumably,
doesn’t know what they are like either. Any-
how, some of these gentlemen are wrong.

This show notice is contributed by a party
who is still at large:—
Mr. Bert Bailey, member for Pitt-street (Sydney),
addressed his constituents last night at the Palace
Theatre, and received a cordial hearing. Mr. Jules
Grant occupied the chair in the box office. The
candidate said there was too much water in demo-
cracy’s stock.
A voice: Hear, hear !
Mr. Bailey: A pity you didn’t take a little more
water, old man (loud laughter). He went on to
say that if the proposals of some people were
carried the business in which he was interested
would not be given a say in regulating its own
a flairs. (“Shame!”) A resident of Broken Hill
or Oodnadatta would be able to tell Mr. Duggan
to nationalise his magenta whiskers. (Uproar.) Also,
“Steele Rudd” would have to hand over his percent-
age of the takings of his successful enterprise to a
man who made a pair of boots at Bathurst.
A voice: Rats !
Mr. Bailey: No doubt my friend knows all about
rats, having been associated with some prominent
Liberals. (Cheers.) Continuing, Mr. Bailey said he
would sooner see the Old Age Pensions done away
with than accept milk of an Illawarra dairyman’s
cow in payment for a stalls seat. (Continued
applause.) He did not believe there were any
Trusts in Australia. Since the Bailey party had
been in power on the Palace treasury seats, the
populace had seen the advantages that" had accrued
to the pioneer settlers at Ruddville. He admitted
that money was no easier, but there had been no
fall in prices at the Palace. (Cheers.) No doubt
there had been an increase in the cost of living, and
he regretted this, as it interfered with business in
his electorate ( No, no!”) Let it be remembered
that the citizens had it in their power on election
day to regulate their domestic concerns so that they
might vote for the old ticket, Dad, Dave and Joe.
There was no compensation in surrendering their
(the citizens’) finances to other parties whom they
could not trust. (Cheers.)
On the motion of Mr. .Jules Grant a hearty vote
of four bob tickets was passed to Mr. Bailey.
[J. Beer, Counter Lunch Hotel, George Street,
Sydney. |

The Tivoli has brightened up wonderfully
this week, and the programme shows no
fewer than five new turns. O’Hana San is
undoubtedly the star, not so much for her
acting as for the gorgeous color effects ob-

within the memory of the oldest inhabitant
has come within miles of it. The other new-
comers are the Be Bruns, a pair of very
clever dancers of the drawing-room variety ;
Osborn and Brookes, chiefly noticeable on
account of the male partner’s fine imperson-
ation of a somewhat over-gymnastic drunk ;
Miss Kitty Dale, a pantomime “boy,” suc-
cessfully transplanted on to the vaudeville
stage; and George Whitehead, a baritone of
no despicable grade. Among older
identities the Jackson family of cyclists
is still doing its best to break its
collective neck. Harry Corson Clarke
and Margaret Dale Owen have only

to burst its corset with mirth. Cummin
and Seaham are getting encouraging re-
sponse. to their invitation, and Nella Webb
is again, for the very manytb time, in her
last nights. The loudest noise next week

sea lions.
The Antony-Cleopatra scandal, which
lately shocked Egyptian and Roman society,
is being shown up nightly by the right-
thinking Asche-Brayton co., at Sydney
Royal. The late Mr. William Shakspeare’s
careful shorthand report of the case has been
artistically illustrated in color by Mr. Oscar
Asche, and, though many come just to look
at the pictures, quite a lot of people are not
above taking notice of Mr. Shakspeare’s part
of the work.

Mephistopheles has one more week, wherein
to repeat his familiar contract with Faust
at the King’s. Each time this paper has wit-
nessed the retirement of that rejuvenated
philosopher into the shades and vapors of
Gehenna, something went Wrong with the
workings of perdition. The outer shades got
caught on their rollers, and the curtain
came down in a hurry ere the award of Sin’s
wages board could be fully enforced. But
Marguerite _ started her upward journey
without a hitch. How long she would be in
reaching her destination Heaven only could
say. Probably a billion years, unless the
four angels decide to flap their pinions and
put on a spurt.
Two weeks of “Dr. Wake’s Patient,” at
the Princess’s, will close on Friday. The
comedy seems to have suffered considerably
by its re-setting since it was first produced
on the same stage. The bucolic barbarism
of ()kl Man Wake was always inconsistent
with his. allegation that his farm had been in
the family for nigh on three ’underd year,
but when, of yore, he visited the marine
hotel to confer his rugged blessing on his
son’s future bride, he never got further than
the terrace in his quest for the place where
he could buy a pint o’ ale. In the present
revival the third act scene is an interior,
therefore the fatuous yeoman has to hurl
his comments upon the “swattiness” of the
day right into the centre of the room where
the quality are seated. The falseness of
\\ ake’s vulgarity, and the impossible crude-
ness of his missus, are more apparent than
ever. The art of Titheradge, Mrs. Brough,
Sty an and Sydney Stirling cannot save the
situation. Saturday’s bill should be more
attractive, than the revival of “Wake.”
Plimmer is producing “A Woman of Im-
pulse,” for the first time in Melbourne.

Madge Temple is Melbourne’s latest ex-
cuse for holding down a seat at the
Opera House. Madge is hardly an innova-
tion as a warbler, but with some smart hats
and frocks she succeeds in enhancing the
effect of a personality that is all the better
for them, and might be w T orse without them.
Bar Taylor and Arnold, Henri French, and
the quaint Charles Zoli, the only other popu-
lar figures are Reynard and Rosella, respect-
ively labelled as “dramatic baritone” and
"Italian harpist.” Miss Rosella doesn’t
harp the way this writer harps, nor does
she harp as a lot of his friends harp. But
it must be said for Reynard that, apart from
his expressive singing, his obvious pleasure
in his own singing is a gladdening circum-
stance and an event that can’t be safely
overlooked.
A persistent traveller on the screen at the
P’ran Lyric (Vic.), last week, was “The
Prisoner of War,” a Turko-Bulgarian battle
yarn, strongly suffused with bellowdrama.
and so laden with the dead and dismembered
that the film looked like an impressionist
view of a slaughter-house. “The Devil’s
Daughter,” a three-reel drama, also occupied
the _ attention of Lyric inhabitants. And
comics like “All on Account of a Transfer”
and “Playing His Master Card,” aroused the
loud laugh that sometimes shows the vacant
mind, and sometimes only reveals that sensa-
tion of emptiness in the stomach before
meals. so frequently alluded to in the patent
medicine advertisement.
The burglar-man who is said to have
got away with valuables from 24 Mel-
oourne suburban homes fell into the hands
of the police last week. It happened this
way. A matron, whilst gazing casually at
the weather, chanced to see a strange man
prowl from the front to the back of Mr.
Michaelis’s house, on the opposite side of
the road. Knowing the hou«e to be unpro-
tected at the time, and noting that the
prowler was a long time about his visit, she
mentioned her suspicions to her husband, a
contractor, engaged on a contract near by.
This active and intelligent citizen jumped
into his buggy, called one of his workmen
from the job to bear him company, and
drove away to make an arrest. Having
caught the astonished prowler, he (the con-
tractor) lifted him bodily into the buggy
and sat on him, whilst the workman
whipped up the horse. A policeman was
sighted and the contractor handed his cap-
tive over to the man in blue. The
indignant prowler’s pockets were stuffed
with jewellery. Later, details of two
dozen other robberies were found at
his domicile, for he was a married
man, who always took home his lootings to
his w T ife. Then the police communicated
the result of their remarkable vigilance to
the newspaper, and the contractor returned
to his own private business. Thus far
there has been no talk of presenting him
with a medal or an illuminated address.
Any rewards offered for the restoration of
lost property by the 24 householders afore-
mentioned will presumably be the perquisites
of the constable to w 7 hom the robber was
handed over.
The old gag was bound to turn up again
on the anniversary of the landing of Cook
at Kurnell. So Sydney Telegraph came
out on Saturday with e picture of a
house in London described as “88 Mile End-
road, once the home of the discoverer of
Australia.” Apparently it can’t be driven
into the intelligence of the Sydney daily
press, even with an axe, that Australia was
discovered at least 200 years before .Tames
Cook happened along these shores, and that
its coast was mostly mapped, and most of its
capes and bays were named, and the whole
blessed continent was annexed and called
New Holland. Apparently the ignorance of
the daily publication is incurable.
For the first time in its quaint history, the
Tasmanian Assembly met the other day and
chose its new 7 Speaker without a preliminary
informal meeting of members to do the actual
selecting. On this occasion there was really
no need to invite members of the Opposition
to assist in the selection, as the appointment
of ex-I’remier Evans was fixed up by the
Government caucus tw 7 o months ago. As
no Ministerial follower had died or fallen
downstairs, or ratted from his party since
the caucus meeting, the ignoring of the
ancient custom was quite safe. Octogenarian
Jonathan Best who, The Bulletin is sorry
to hear, is not in the best of health, dropped
into his old billet as chairman of com-
mittees.
The Tokyo Puck (Japan), in a recent
issue, broke forth into a prophetic map of
the world. It showed the British Empire
reduced to the British islands, India and
Canada. All Bull’s African possessions had
become a black republic. Australia, Maori-
land, Fiji, the East Indies, and a great area
in Manchuria were Japanese. Persia had
been annexed by the Russians. A footnote
declared that the world would presently be
divided up thus: (1) Japan; (2) England;
(3) United States; (4) China; (5) Mada-
gascar (set apart as a sanatorium for tuber-
culous patients) ; (6) Black African Re-
public; (7) South American Republic; (8)
France; (9) Austria; (10) Russia; (11)
Italy; (12) Germany. And the name of
all the rest was Mud.
Unless there is a general increase of fares
and freights the N.S.W. railways will ap-
parently be back this year to the old bank-
rupt situation. In 1910 they paid working
expenses and interest on the dreadful lOU,
and supplied £522,000 odd towards wiping
off the dead shortage of past ages. In 1911
the profit was £553,000. In 1912 it was
down to £415,000. In 1013 (the financial
year closes on June 30), the great N.S.W.
railway system is expected to keep out of
the Benevolent Asylum with difficulty. Be-
cause of the foolishness and wickedness of
past and present politicians, who used the
land sale money as revenue instead of apply-
ing it to public works, the Cornstalk’s 4ft.
84in. way totters under an interest bill of
about £2,000.000 per annum. But for that
iniquity every railway servant might get 10
per cent, put on his wages, and the fares
might be reduced about 15 per cent, all
round, and there might even then be enough
left to pay for an heroic burst of turkey and
small beer at Christmas.

A traveller’s note from the sea :
It was a fine misty morning, when the north-
bound inter-State boat approached the harbor that is
watched over by a tall, white lighthouse on a high,
carven cliff. “You could put the biggest navy in
the world in there and lose it,” said the man who
knew 7 , and he indicated the wide, blue entrance and
the misty, mountain-backed harbor vistas. And the
leisurely steamer did not deviate, but churned stead-
ily on • its course, for the grand harbor was not
populous Port Jackson, but merely deserted Jervis
Bay—the poor, grass-green relation of the bloated
capital, where the ends of the political strings are
held and the railways planned, and the rates fixed.
“Only 87 miles from Sydney now,” said the im-
patient passengers, as the fleet-swallowing harbor
and its sentinel lighthouse slowly faded astern. A
stranger looking at that deserted inlet would never
' have guessed that he was within coo-ee of the
alleged commercial centre of the State. The only
visible improvement since Cook blew past is the tall,
white lighthouse, whose principal purpose is to
light the Sydney boats south and the Melbourne
boats north. Now and then it serves to guide a few
war craft that want a nice, deserted stretch of
water for shot practice. Otherwise it just shines on
high and holds its lamp to the coastal procession.
As the inter-State boat approached Sydney, the
smoke of ships began to show up on every hand.
Off the entrance, tugs strained in front of wallowing
barges full of Sydney silt, and a dredge was ob-
served making off for one of the wretched little bar
harbors. Then the liner, with a sharp turn, got two
marks in line, and steamed cautiously in under
North Head. Another quick turn and it zig-zagged
across the entrance towards South Head, and edged in
between the white pile light and the shore. From
that point onward it walked carefully up the narrow
harbor, passing so close to shore that, here and
there, anyone on board could have thrown a biscuit
or a loaf of bread ashore, or heaved the ship’s cat
into a tree. Extreme care was needed to avoid ram-
ming the wooden ferries about the Quay, while a
great amount of caution had to be observed at the
crowded Darling Harbor corner. (Darling Harbor
is kept crowded by the ships that have to wait in
that marine blind ‘ alley for the cargoes thov really
should pick up in the deserted ports.) While the
tugs took hold fore and aft, and began the weari-
some evolutions necessary for getting the newly-
arrived ship to its dusty berth, one remembered sadly
the beautifully spacious harbor sleeping in the morn-
ing sun 87 miles to the southward. I don’t know
which is the more doleful sight—deserted Jervis
Bay, or the hideously crowded Waverley graveyard
on the sloping ocean cliff. Both mistakes are worthy
of the Ma State.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Y.Z.”: Fred B. Smith, Raymond Robins and
party have crashed through Melbourne with their
“Men and Religion Forward Movement,” and the
echoes of their onward rush are now dying down in
the distance. Curiosity drove me into one of their
“men’s meetings” in a Collins-street church, and I
found the edifice packed with parsons. Every imag-
inable kind of parson was represented, from the
tomtit curate to the sonorous, reverend brahmapoo-
tra, but common clay was scarce. You could only
pick out a small, cheap sheep here and there, dotting
the great waste of pasturage. I understood now how
Fred B. Smith, Raymond Robins and party pulled
their audiences. They have a reputation as great
movers of men and expeditious sin-shifters, and all
the pastors and spiritual masters dropped in on them
to see how it was done, and to pick up points. The
attraction was purely a shop interest, such as axe-
men display when a champion chopper is lowering a
record, or the kind that draws swarms of bricklayers
to see an expert brickie slapping mortor against time.
The plate was full of threepenny-bits.
“One Who Pays”: “Dr. Wake’s Patient,” in Mel-
bourne, is decent debility, made tolerable by good
people. Just a Brough is needed. Otherwise—but
v hat an otherwise! —the company is better than
Brough ever had. He never had such a good jeune
premier as Roberts, who really has charm. Maybe
as much as anyone on the stage, now that Alexander
is a trifle in the sere and yellow. Roberts was nearly
as good as Brough as the Jonathan Wild of “Little
Jack Sheppard,” and what a capital Svengali he
was in the burlesque “Trilby.” Cecil Ward was
nowhere beside him for leading juvenile, yet he was
prettv good in such diverse things as Napoleon, Tom
Dossiter, in “Harbor Lights,” and the Little Minis-
ter. Brough’s company was a gifted family. There
ivere Mr. and Mrs. Bob, Bob’s mother (though no-
body would believe it), Mrs. Bob’s sister (the ever-
excellent Emma), Bob’s half-sister, and also Miss
Major of the clan. It pleased me to notice that Mrs.
Brough got the biggest receotion in “Dr. Wake's
Patient,” though the public was also genial to Beatrice
Day, Titheradge, Roberts, Plimmer, Styan and Lizette
Pa rises.
“Harrison O.” : Can any Abo. naturalist or Poverty
Point physiologist inform me of what use .wings are
to angels ? I am induced to ask the question
through having just seen four of these interesting
birds, in “Faust,” who move upward through the
ether without so much as twitching a wing. I have
met. many stage angels in my time —black-haired,
gold-haired and red-haired, single and married (at
any rate, they wore wedding rings), slim and embon-
point—but not one moved her wings when flying.
I regard my inquiry as of profound scientific and
spiritual importance, and would give at least tup-
pence for a satisfactory answer.
“Harrison O.” : Since Celia Ghiloni apparently has
not got a kind friend willing to mention the matter,
I fain would whisper to her that Art, even in mus : Qal
comedy, should rank before grease paint. Celia’s
persistent refusal to bring her good looks into line
with her embonpoint when playing elderly or un-
beautiful characters, has marred several of her re-
cent performances. In the “Sinbad” panto, she made
the shrewish old maid, Princess Fatima, a beauteous
houri. As Aurelia, the “flirtatious old dame” of
“The Chocolate Soldier,” Celia was 30 years too
young and 45 years too lovely. Now, in “The
Count of Luxembourg,” as an amorous countess who
pursues an elderly grand duke with her unwelcome
affection, the actress once again presents a heavenly
creature, from whom no disrespectable grand duke
would dream of flying; although it is obvious that
the author intends the part to be played on the lines
of Katisha, the old Kat in “The Mikado.”
The theatre position in Adelaide now works put
thus: The Royal keeps a monopoly of drama, Wil-
liamson having given up his scheme for a great build-
ing on North Terrace, though the land was actually
bought, and secured first claim on the Royal itself.
And the big, unfinished Princess, in Grote-street —
a central but hitherto quite unfashionable location —
has been secured by Hugh Mclntosh, so that Hie old
Rickards’ business will presently be removed there
from the Tivoli. (It will take the old iivon name
with it.) Meanwhile, Ben Fuller, who runs the
Brennan circuit at the new King’s, has secured a
long lease of the Tivoli itself when Mclntosh gives it
up, and promises to rebuild it, so that it wil be
available for drama if required.
“Locust”: Re the Smythe birthday. R. S.
Much-Travelled Smythe, now 80, was the most adroit
press agent that ever travelled Australia. Being
originally a pressman, he probably had the publicity
instinct. As an example of his methods: I was
“printer” of a small daily paper in North Queens-
land 20 years ago. One night, about 11 p.m. when
I was “locking up” the early pages, and was vali-
antly hammering quoins into the chases before send-
ing the early formes to press, I was confronted by
a charming gentleman, immaculately dressed, who
inquired if I was Mr. Locust. With my apron I
wiped away the sweat which obscured my vision, and
admitted I was. He gave me his card, and, of
course, I knew him right away, and was greatly
honored. He said, “Have you been to my show
yet?” (It was Miss Finkelstein lecturing about
Jerusalem to the early Wowsers of Australia.) I
confessed I hadn’t. Saturday was my only spare
evening, and I had a permanent engagement for that
night. Being a deep man of the world, he asked
would I accept a pass for two, and that, I con-
fessed, overcame my difficulty about the engagement.
Before going, I was able to assure him (1) that
all the paragraphs sent in had been scattered in
advantageous positions in the literary columns; (2)
that his advt. would appear right at the top of
the front page with white space above and below
(I felt sorry I could not stick it in the middle of
the leading article for his sake) ; and (3) that I
would confer the honor upon him (that was the
impression he gave to the transaction) of coming
on Saturday night. That was the beginning of a
friendship which has continued ever since; for R. S.
Smythe never forgot a man, unlike most showmen,
whose peculiar memories only allow 7 them to recol-
lect men when they arrive at the town where such
men are influential, or likely to be useful. Carlyle,
the Not-Quite-So-Much-Travelled, has inherited his
father’s admirable qualities, and, in addition, thanks
to his training for the special task, has a handy
knowledge of foreign languages, and can write. His
articles on Germany in the S. M. Herald recently
were extremely interesting and finely written, and his
reminiscences of Mark Twain in Life were the real
goods.
How has the Incorporated Stage Society succeeded
in persuading the British censor to license Schnitzler’s
plays? Two Schnitzler plays have been presented at
the Aldwych Theatre, London. Schnitzler is an Aus-
trian, and is the dramatist of the tart. He favors
the one-act play, and it is always blue. In “The
Comtesse Mizzi” the whole atmosphere is one of
decadence, and every character is thoroughly if ami-
ably immoral, and airs his sins as a Wowser adver-
tises bis virtue. Schnitzler w'orks the demi-monde
for all it is w 7 orth, and the censor who has permitted
“Comtesse Mizzi” cannot reasonably buck at any-
thing an Englishman is likely to do.
“8.J.”: Gilbert Parker dramatised “Faust” some
time before he allowed a knighthood to be foisted
upon him, and he makes his devil claim to have
invented titles. “I make more money by titles now
than I used to,” says Mephistopheles, “and I make
them cheaper, too.”
Ellen Terry, Henry Irving’s long-time leading lauy,
having nothing to do in the acting line nowadays,
has written —or, at least, signed—a preface to a
book on “The Russian Ballet.” Russian dancers have
been the rage in London during the last few years,
and they have drawn huge crow T ds at Covent Gar-
dens, the Coliseum, the Palace, the Hippodrome and
elsewhere. The article with Ellen’s name appended
deplores that “the dance should have almost every-
where degenerated into something base and trivial,
while its children Music and Poetrv have preserved
their dignity and beauty.” It considers this strange,
having regard for the fact that dancing apparently
had its origin in religion. We know from the Bible
that it entered largely into the pious ceremonies, of
the Jews, and there is evidence that the practice Was
continued ampmr the early Christians. Ellen alleges
that “on feast davs, the honor of leading the dancers
was reserved for the Bishop.” A Bishop caught
dancing in church at the present day would be torn
to fragments by the churchwardens and his remains
would he scoffed at by pale curates.
(Continued on page 30.)
Those State politicians who call themselves
“Liberal” are so in at least one respect—as
regards the burden they reckon their fellow
man should carry. The Australian Parlia-
ment did its best when it prohibited the im-
portation of anything bigger than the 2001 b.
“Chapman sack”—Chapman, by the way,
called himself a Protectionist, not a “Lib-
eral.” But for want of sufficient power it
couldn’t prevent the local manufacture of the
sack which is like the great cave of Jenolan,
and some of the State Governments and State
railway commissioners have given it very
little assistance. In Victoria especially, the
ex-Tait railways still drag 3001 b. and 3501 b.
bags of barley down from the Western Dis-
trict. And when a man starts to carry a
3501 b. bag. the weight of it is liberal enough
for anybody. There is no Protection to the
lumper’s back about that.
A MID-YEAR RESOLUTION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I was uplifted by the glad New Year,
And all the kindly wishes of my friends;
I felt as some young god who turns his clear
Unwrinkled forehead to the sky that bends
Above him, full of planets he may wear,
Or stars that he may use to comb his hair.
Such exaltation was a little due
To liquid extracts from tall bottles drawn,
And small peculiar dreams that two and two
Walked through my pleasant slumbers at the
dawn ;
I pause not to explain, but simply state
The New Year started, and I felt elate.
It was the time, as all good Christians know,
When pagan persons should attempt to turn
A brand new leaf, and resolutely show
What kindling ardor in their breasts can burn
By making resolutions large and firm
Whose aftermath may set their souls a-squirm.
I had already walked the bitter way
That erring human kind perforce must choose;
My heart had known of faithless vows that lay
Like broken glass up-ended in my shoes,
And yet I made some new resolves and vain—
Made them, and let them go to smash again.
Now, the new year has grown to middle age,
The times of riot past, the wishes few,
I view the situation as a sage,
And know exactly what I mean to do ;
Rich with much sad experience I am bold
To make one resolution as of old.
I will not pledge myself to never talk
To men of Belial in the pleasant way
(Alas, their conversation and their walk
Are godless, but such people often pay
For drinks. And why should such as I eschew
Their observations when my coins are few ?)
I will not say that, should the time agree,
Some evening when the warm sun westward dips,
If some nice girl is sitting on my knee,
I will not kiss the redness of her lips ;
For who may hope forgiveness for his past
Who proves a swine when gods their girl-pearls cast ?
I will not swear to always pay each debt
Exactly at the moment it is due;
I will not undertake to scorn the wet,
Brown influence of things that brewers brew ;
I will not absolutely undertake
To tell no happy lie for love’s sweet sake.
But one firm resolution I will make
Here at the ripening of another year,
I swear it by the brimstone of that lake
Where falls not any rain or dew or beer,
1 nail not, while the heavens and earth shall stand,
Give one brown copper to the German Band.
N.s.w.
PAT O’MAORI.

where it was otherwise?” Counsel said Chia
acted under mad impulse. Hood: “Are,

“0.K.”: Methinks I have discovered the
original author of all the In Memoriam
verses that have appeared with varying al-
terations and combinations in the Australian
press for the past 30 years and more. My
suspicion falls on George C. Beattie, whose
Moral and Religious Poems were published
(clothed boards and gilt edges) by Sands
and McDougall, Melb., in 1889. On page 7
the poet mourns the loss of his mother: —
Shall I my mother never again see?
Who in infancy fondly cared for me,
And strove tenderly to please my fancy;
Who in life’s young days taught me to be good ;
And after rose the bright morn of manhood.
The thought we’ll meet in Heaven shall not beguile,
In Heaven what glories around us shall smile!
Page 77 is sacred to the memory of little
Hannah :
My beloved child, youngest of all
Of my daughters, eight in number ;
You came to us, but soon left us all
For the land where there’s no night, no slumber ;
You remained only a few years,
Then left us suddenly in tears.
On page 20 I find: —
She left this world to reign above
In her Redeemer’s Kingdom of love,
Where no impurity is found,
Where all’s that pure and holy abound.
On page 00 the bard remembers his deceased
wife: —
Oh, how often I look back into the past,
And think of her who was my wife and friend,
Who with me left her own dear, native land,
And brother and sister and parents’ home.
The foregoing quotations from George Craw-
ford Beattie seem to bear the hall-mark of
what is called “obituary verse,” therefore I
reckon that the old man was the legitimate
parent of the school of poetry which is paid
for by the inch under “Deaths” and “In
Memoriam” headings. I say old man ad-
visedly, because a photo of G.C.B. as a long-
bearded ancient is gummed on the back of the
title page, facing the index. The titles of
the poems are indexed alphabetically, and
there are about 200 in all. Although the first
one is entitled “The Christian Race” and the
opening lines are
O why! O why not run the Christian race?
In it can you nothing attractive trace?
the book is absolutely destitute of sporting
spirit, or even sportive mood. In rhyme or
in blank verse the author was equally
serious. He joked not, neither did he smile.
And when he had a point to make he drove it
into the reader’s understanding with relent-
less tautology. As, for instance, in “The
Lonely Cave” (page 186) :
This lonely cave doth with its excellent water
Supply all those who come to it for water,
All those who come to it for water can be
Supplied, for it has always enough for all
In all seasons. Our lonely home in this lone bush
Is supplied with its pure cold water.
You’ll highly value excellent water
Such as the'water of this lonely cave.
Among the amazing nroducts of Australia
the hook of Beattie, published by Sands and
McDougall (Melb.) only 24 years ago, has a
place of its own. The old man had been do-
ing this sort of thing for at least 40 years
before he collected his stuff, and it was prob-
ably his habit of supplying bereaved friends
and acquaintances with metrical expressions
of grief that set the fashion in newspaper
advt. columns.
“Moa”: I believe there is a
society in Sydney for the Pre-
vention of Cruelty to Animals,
but wouldn’t bet on it, because
want of funds or native mod-
esty or something makes it so
persistently hide its light under
a bushel that no one seems to
know, with any certainty,
whether it exists or not. As-
suming that it does, why doesn’t
it send an official to make the
round of the shops where live
birds are on sale? I pass every
morning one of these shops, and
have often noticed dozens of
wretched fowls cooped up in
small cages, each cage contain-
ing about three times as many
chooks as it ought to hold.
During the sweltering days of
summer it was pitiable to see
the miserable creatures packed
like figs in a box. and almost
unable to turn their heads. The
weather is cooler now, but a
good deal of wholly unneces-
sary suffering continues to be
inflicted. And the same state
of things exists all over Syd-
ney. Imagine two or three
days, or a week, of this kind of tor-
ture, and then say whether the people who
send hymn-books and moral pockey-hankies
to the heathen in his blindness might not be
better employed.

“Uomo Delinquente”:—
The N. S. Wales Justice Department is investigat-
ing a row that occurred in Darlinghurst (Sydney)
Gaol the other day. It was the aftermath of a
concert given by the Prisoners’ Aid Society. When
the entertainment ended, the prisoners, according to
the officials, “went quite mad.” They screamed,
stamped and used language that would have startled
the Legislative Ass. itself. Why investigate ? Pris-
oners have an ineradicable tendency to act in this
A MATTER OF ETIQUETTE.
MARTHA JANE: “This it the third time e’s parsed me. I suppose 'c don't like to speak
wiyout a hintroduction."
wav. It is most noticeable in women’s gaols. In
such institutions any deviation from the normal
routine is practically certain to produce a riot.ij I
heard of a London gaol where everything went on
oiled wheels for weeks. Then some carol-singers got
to work outside, and the music penetrated to the
confinees. A riot started that transformed the place
into the semblance of a mad-house. The prisoners
howled wildly within their several cells, beat their
tin cans together, and cursed for hours at a time.
A number of them had fits ultimately. Scores, were
found on the floors of their cells, semi-unconscious
from fatigue. In most intelligently-conducted men’s
gaols the practice of showing women over the build-
ings has been discontinued. Five times out of «ix—
more especially in the prisons which boast a goodly
collection of long sentencers —the after effect is
disastrous. The men begin by being sullen and
defiant, and end by going demented. The desire to
make a loud, purposeless noise runs through the
gaol like a bush fire, on these occasions. The most
stolid and well-behaved are affected, and nothing can
be done to stop them. The psychology of it has
never been satisfactorily demonstrated. Broadly
speaking, it is assumed that the solitude and the
silence get on the nerves of those concerned to such
an extent that, on the least provocation, they are
ready to scream. But certain orders of nuns live all
their lives under even more trying conditions, and
never make a fuss about it. Possibly the criminal
temperament is exceptionally neurotie and high-
strung. Anyhow, concerts and such-like are bad for
it. These well-intentioned entertainments don’t even
add to the general happiness of the prisoners—-any-
how, not half so much as they add to the general
feeling of desperation and utter hump.

A Thoughtful Citizen speaks:—
It is time someone jumped firmly on the Wages
Board system, as carried out at the present time
in N. S. Wales. The interest of the party which
is opposing the increase of wages or lessening of
hours is to prolong the case as long as possible for
two reasons. Delay wears the other chap out finan-
cially, and makes him disinclined to have any more
trouble before a board. Also, it postpones the day
of the increase indefinitely. One way of obviating
this would be to amend the Act so that all awards
should be retrospective to the date of the issue of the
original claim. Also, the system of payment to
chairmen badly requires alteration. At present they
are paid so much a sitting. The longer the case
goes the more money they draw—and although
a man may not be corrupt and deliberately prolong
a case in order to make more money out of it, it
stands to reason that he has an unconscious bias
against anything like red-hot speed. Furthermore,
he is mostly a lawyer, and often one of those whose
incomes can easily stand any side profits that
unexpectedly blow along. A lawyer, of any sort,
by his very training, must become incapable of going
straight to the point. For years and years he has,
in the sacred interests of Holy Costs, gone the
furthest way round to arrive at the point, and when
he becomes chairman of a Wages Board the habit
does not depart from him. So the chairmen should
be paid on some other system. Probably the best
would be that of a descending 'scale. The usual
“sitting” is generally a matter of a couple of hours
at most in the evening, whereas it should be the
same as the average daily hours worked in the
trade or business under review. That would be a
great help to the chairman in his struggle to get a
proper appreciation of what sort of day it really was.
Then there is a doubt if it is wise to allow either
side to have the services of lawyers. Some of the
lawyers behave decently, but of late there has been
a tendency to resort to police-court methods and to
ask witnesses how many times they have been in
gaol. All of which is- foreign to the question of
wages, prolongs the case, wastes time and public
money, and causes needless fury.

POET’S FEET.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“You are a niglitwatchman ?” —“Yes, I watch the
Gas Company’s jobs in the streets.”
“You have time to ruminate. You’re a bit of a
poet, aren’t you ?”—-“Perhaps I am. At my job
I must do something or go mad.”
—Evidence before Gas Makers’ Wages Board.
The busy brain that’s given to us
In turn must have its task,
Lest like a mad dinosaurus
It stares through midnight’s mask.
The watchman paces to and fro ;
At every stride, the rhythm
Is pacing too, and, soft and slow,
A million feet go with ’im.
The feet of pomes, the feet of man
Are things he can control —
The feet are his, the metres scan
In his immortal scroll.
But what of feet we know abound
In meters measuring gas,
Whose busy hands go re ud and round
Nor heed what gas-feet pass?
Perchance, since poets are employed,
’Twill be correct if we
Assume a poet’s hands have toyed
With meters on the spree.
N.S.W.

In advocating the arming of the Weary
State police with revolvers, Melb. Herald
makes the unexpectedly sensible suggestion
that all such weapons should be
stamned with a number and that a
record should be kept by the police, who
would get their first information from the
retail seller. After that, the man who sold
privately would have to notify the law as
to the person who got his second-hand gun.
In a murder case an identifiable number on
a revolver would be a valuable clue. To the
plea that too many of these weapons are in
use it may be said that it is doubtful if there
are 11,000 of them in Melbourne; yet there
is that number of motor vehicles, all num-
bered and traceable.
Melbourne’s city councillors are in trouble,
and if anybody will give them the price of
the Flinders-street market building, and cart
it away, they will feel obliged. Recently the
machinery broke down at the cool stores and
caused considerable loss. Then the Railway
Commissioners told them they would have to
remove or otherwise obliterate a slice of the
building, as it was necessary to widen the
viaduct. The cool stores threaten to
be tenantlefes when the Government
stores at West Melbourne are opened.
On top of these troubles the founda-
tions have given way; the eastern part of
the building—where the subsoil is alluvial silt
-—has dropped about 12 inches ( !), and the
whole blessed contraption threatens to dash
itself into the bowels of the Yarra. Still,
even if the building is swallowed up utterly
the loan with which it was built will remain.
That is permanent, all right.

“Melb ”: Scott’s Hotel in Collins-street,
Melbourne, is being dragged down with a
view to an entire and magnificent resurrec-
tion. At one time there was an idea of
buying the whole corner, and bursting
through into William-street, and making
about the biggest and finest bed-and-drink
emporium south of the line. But, appar-
ently, some of the people interested won’t
sell, so there is nothing for it but to build
a towering place on the old site, the
chimneys of which will warm the feet
of the angels. The Scott after whom the
place was named belonged to the generation
before last. When he was in his early
stages—his pub-infancy, so to speak—the
establishment was a mild, retiring little beer-
house called the Clarendon. It grew, in
competition with Menzies’. The Port Phillip
Club in Flinders-street makes a third in the
trio. It acquired the squatting business of
the deceased Prince of Wales, in Flinders-
lane.
THE OTHER ANIMAL.
PORTER (to himself): “And they say I lead a
dog's life!"
“Inked”: A sadder evening than that
which I spent in company with Rider Hag-
gard at the Sydney Institute of Journalists,
I never expect to endure this side of Perdi-
tion. Rider is a tall, powerfully-built man,
with a small head and a worried face. He
rose to reply to an absolutely funereal ora-
tion from Heney, of the Herald, with a
look on his countenance as though all was
lost. Then came the bitter, hot, blinding
tears —bottles of them. He explained that
he was humble, very humble, and constantly
becoming more so. He spoke in no spirit of
self-praise—God forbid it! —but he was con-
strained to mention that he had won success
in what was a heart-breaking profession.
The only comfort he got was when someone
on a couch of pain and sickness wrote and
told him how his works been enjoyed.
Then he temporarily wiped the tears away.
But not for long. Presently would come the
carking recollection that the modern writer
was like a butterfly. Here to-day, gone to-
morrow. And the grief that overcame him
was almost more than he could bear. A
few sad words followed on the iniquity of
the woman writer. He deplored the doings
of such females :
I would undertake, to-morrow—any man of any
experience of the world, and sufficient gift of the
pen could undertake —to write a book that would
sell by the hundred thousand, and yet avoid the law.
Not for a million pounds would I do it. (Applause.)
At the finish of what had been intended for
a festive gathering, most of us felt as though
a near relative had just fallen dead, leaving
us unremembered in the will.
“Rosna” (in London) : A new sort of
malefactor was discovered here the other
day. He was found sitting meekly beside
the band in Sir Charles Jessel’s ball-room
at Berkeley Square. There wasn’t so much
as a spoon about him. All he wanted was
the society of the nobility and
gentry—he being a middle-aged
shop-assistant, which is the
type that most loves a lord, so
they say. He was arrested and
charged with harmless be-
havior,. or innocuous language,
or. glaring at the aristocracy, or
being found alive on the pre-
mises, or something similar. It
came out in evidence that he
had previously written to an-
other social beacon or lighthouse as follows:
The Hon. Mrs. Massey-Beresford,
I should be pleased to attend your dance, and am
holding the day open. Kindly not overlook my name
in sending out invitations.
With kind regards, yours sincerely,
Kemo L. Sanders.
He hasn’t been sentenced yet, but it is
thought that he will get five years and a
flogging.

Ex-Judge Casey has left £lOOO
towards building St. Mary’s Col-

The site is waiting—a piece of

From time to time Catholicism
turns and asks: “Why haven’t we
a college, like the Anglicans, Pres-
byterians and Wesleens?” There
used to be an idea that the Metho-
dist one would go bung, and
Archbishop Carr would be the
highest bidder. But that idea
has to be given up, for the wily
Sugden has saved “Wesley.”
The Casey benefaction caused the
Newman Society of young Romans
at the University to clamor again
for an Alma Mater. They clam-
ored last year, but Archbishop
Carr and Dean Phelan firmly re-
sisted. All the money that could
be clutched was needed, they said,
for primary education and for
bringing forward the nuns as secu-
lar teachers. The case is exactly
like the agitation to persuade
Cardinal Manning to build West-
minster Cathedral. “No,” said
he, “I want the money for the
poor.” Yet Cardinal Vaughan
built it. The hopes of the Mel-
bourne Newmans rest with Coad-
jutor-Archbishop Mannix, the
schoolmaster and ex-Maynooth
Principal, and they have been at
him. Of course he is ultra-
cautious, and will not go a step
beyond Archbishop Carr’s prompt-
ing. Yet Casey’s £lOOO is a
good nest-egg.
“Kiki”: Liverpool, Maoriland’s recently-
imported vice-regal gaud, has been putting
a little salt int*> the raw places left on Soci-
ety by his predecessor’s missus. Mrs. Isling-
ton. In celebration of the arrival of the

week, a citizens’ ball had been arranged as
part of the festivities, and the committee in-
vited George V.’s unemployed delegate to
attend. But apparently the “invite” was
not couched in terms befitting Liverpool s
size in dignity, or something like that, be-
cause a bald reply was forwarded that there
was no ball on the week’s programme, and
that his Worship was going to Auckland the
day after the ship arrived. This was a bad
enough snub, but when it was learned that
the Heaven-born was putting on a hop and
two other shivoos for the northern city, the
outraged feelings of the inhabitants of the
capital city ran hot and smoked publicly.

“0.K.” writes: —
An outcry from Trafalgar (Vic.) calls attention
to the Law’s incapacity for dealing rationally with
such citizens as F. B. Whiskie, of Footscray (Melb.),
Whiskie, who had presumably left Footscray for the
district’s good, and gone to Trafalgar to get a job,
is a frequent and versatile offender. A long series
of convictions for horse-stealing, larceny, assault and
battery, etc., were admitted by him when he was
brought to account for his latest indiscretion. He
had savagely assaulted a harmless “drunk,” and
when a lady expostulated with him he knocked her
down also, and kicked her likewise. He fought with
the policeman who arrested him, screaming for a
revolver and behaving like a dangerous lunatic. For
all of which the Court —one Harris, P.M. —fined
him £6 or five weeks. Hence the shriek of indig-
nation from Trafalgar. The beak’s view of the case
was not more unsatisfactory than the views of pre-
vious beaks who had punished the same prisoner
more severely for previous misdeeds. They sent him
to prison for a short term, or a longer term, with
the invariable result that he came forth looking for
more trouble. Harris, P.M., accepted Whiskie’s
allegation that his mind was weak, and had not re-
tained smy recollection of his misconduct. Rather
than deal with the evilly-disposed crank as though
he were quite sane, this particular magistrate gave
him the softest sentence permitted by the Law. And
the Law knows that the hardest sentence in its book
of rules would have been less fair to the prisoner
though it were less unjust to the community. No
proper place of restraint is provided for the Whiskies
of this country. No attempt is made to test their
possibilities of improvement. Habitual offenders are
left to make themselves a nuisance in gaol and a
pest to society out of it. Their habitual offensive-
ness argues mental disease, but the Law’s earnest en-
deavor is to aggravate the complaint. Being un-
reasonable, they are unreasonably irritated by a
“discipline” of close confinement, interrupted by
periods of unproductive toil. The gaoling of such
persons is a wicked waste of time and money. The
non-gaoling of them is futile “leniency,” which sets
the public shrieking. What they need is a chance to
do good open-air wprk for a good wage on Federal
Government property in the Empty North, where they
would be considered as penaL*§anatorium patients
for (say) two years. Anv patient declared in-
curable at the end of two years could be fairly
gazetted as a noxious weed and exterminated. The
other day a prisoner at Ballarat Supreme 'Court was
declared a “habitual criminal,” and told by the Judge
that he might be detained in a “reformatory” for 20
years. He replied, “I will be just as well satisfied
there.” The fellow was practically admitting him-
self a fit subject for the grave in his present condi-
tion. Nevertheless, he deserves a reasonable oppor-
tunity to change his views. If the experiment of
putting him on the land were tried and found use-
less, it would then be time to put him under the
land.
“Melb ’’: Principal Beeper, of Trinity
College, inaugurated the Classical Associa-
tion with a discourse on the superior virtue
of Greek and Latin over everything else.
Greek is a positive sin nowadays to anyone
but an enthusiast, who goes in for it as for
music. And our new English Professor.
Wallace, says Latin is no good as an aid to
learning English. No, indeed! Have we
not Shakspeare, Milton, Dickens, Scott,
Dryden, Macaulay, Ruskin? Yet Latin is
comparatively easy stuff, while Greek is the
Devil! It cuts at least two years out of the
best part of your life. So it’s going, going,
and will soon be gone out of the curriculum.
Beeper weeps because Adelaide will presently
be the only place left for compulsory Greek.

SUGGESTIONS TO WOMEN AND GIRLS TRAVEL-
LING ALONE.
Never start anywhere without knowing the exact
name and address of the person to whom you are
going. And be sure you notify them of the time
and dav of your arrival. 1
Never start anywhere unless you have sufficient
money, in addition to your railway or boat ticket,
to provide for your living for three days in case you
are delayed or meet with an accident on the way.
Do not talk to strangers or take advice from any-
one but an official.
If applied to by letter, or in person, all Y.W.C.A.’s
will gladly welcome any woman or girl who is un-
expectedly stranded or knows no place of safety in
the city.
No beauteous maiden need further tremble
at the thought of being Alone in Holy Dune-
din. All she has to do now is to get two
policemen to escort her to the moated fort-
ress of the Y.W.C.A.

CRAWLERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Creeping exercise is the newest cure. Dr. Klotz,
a conspicuous German, is the chief expounder of the
gospel of health that is to out all the simple-life
creeds that have jumped up of late years. The ad-
vantage of the crawl is due to the fact that man
was designed and constructed to go on all-fours,
and in getting proud and stuck-up he has disarranged
the hang of all his internal traps and ware. Only
by getting down to it, and pounding round on hand
and hoof can we hope to get thoroughly fit and well
again.
The upstart praneing on his heels
May carry high his haughty head,
But when internal pangs he feels,
And o’er his being sickness steals,
He realises that instead
Of walking on his hinder end
He should be going on a bend.
The portly cit. who’s full of grief
And ind'gestion’s grisly woes,
Is told he’s carrying his beef
All wrong, and to obtain relief
He should get down on hands and toes,
And must, his valued life to save,
Go with his vest along the pave.
Blackmailers all are hale and strong,
The waiter’s liver is not slewed.
The new position for the throng
That Koltz advises has been long
Their customary attitude.
And toadies crawl no more in stealth —
They say they do it for their health !
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“Montalex”: Have myself seen some un-
canny deep-sea denizens that had “fallen up” to the
surface (“Umfaan,” B. 17/4/’l3). These abyssal
fishes, as they are called, are provided with an in-
ternal gas-producing plant to enable them to stand
the enormous water-pressure of their native depths—
approximately one ton per square inch per mile.
As they are all piscivorous, pursuit of prey very
often brings them up into a pressure so much dimin-
ished that their internal gas, expanding more and
more, forces them faster and faster to the surface.
Before they get there distension reaches bursting-
point.
