“Masareus”: This is my entry for the
Mixed Relationship Stakes. The mother of a recent
Gingin (Westralia) bride died some years ago, and
her father married the daughter of the father of the
present bridegroom. As a result of the recent wed-
ding, the bridegroom’s father-in-law is his own
brother-in-law, and the bride’s father-in-law is her
own father’s father-in-law; the bridegrom is step-
uncle to the bride, who therefore becomes her hus-
band’s step-niece ; the bride’s stepmother is her sister
in-law; her own father is her brother-in-law; and,
finally, the bride’s father-in-law is her own step-
grandfather. But over here, we are still in doubt as
to (1) what relation the bride’s stepfather is to the
cousin of the bridegroom’s fairy god-niece; (2) what
relation the eldest child (if any) will be to the step-
uncle of the newly-wed husband’s bride. If you don’t
know, please don’t worry about it. It is far more
important to kill McGowen, or somebody.
“L’Aiglon”: Bill’s got another grievance.
Last night he cursed his way into the taproom and
pitched a new straw hat on the table.. “’Struth!”
he snarled, “them blighters at the store ’ud do a
bloke for his beer. See that (rancid) hat?” I
had a look. “Seems all right,” I told him. “Right,
be dashed” (or something), he said. “Lor’blime,
I give seven and a kick for that, reckonin’ it was
brand noo, and the (flyblown) thing’s second-hand.
’Ere, read this.” He straightened out the leathers
inside, and this is what I read: “Bon-ton; 6J;
worn by George V.” It was rough on Bill. He had
got the King’s hat, and George V. was presumably
going round bare-headed.

Tiaki : In the 1912 M.L*. Parliamentary
report pertaining to the Cook (Rarotongan) and
other islands, M. Percival, chief medical health
officer, says: “The health of the islands as a whole
was in a most deplorable condition, syphilis pre-
dominant, working havoc with the constitutions of
the natives and materially affecting the birth-rate.
....The diseases I found most prevalent were syphi-
lis-secondary, tertiary and constitutional (few
primary cases are seen, as the natives treat the sores
themselves, with baneful results) ; intermittent fever
of an irregular type, gonorrhoea, uterine diseases,
phthisis, rheumatism, skin diseases peculiar to the
tropics, leprosy, beri-beri (a form of which is
peculiar to and endemic in the northern group),
and other diseases which are met with among white
laces as well as colored. .. .Leprosy is rife on every
island except Rarotonga I would strongly
advise that, in the interests of the patients,
in the interests of the inhabitants, and having re-
gard to the fruit trade, all these eases be collected
by a Government vessel and isolated at Penrhyn
As the leper patients are at present situated, the
situation is distressing in the extreme. No fresh
water for washing, no soap, no change of garments
—filth predominated.” The glad results of the
noble missionary also get a ray of limelight in the
report. The Resident Commissioner (J. Eman
Smith), in his remarks on “bush beer,” says: “I
would draw your attention to the fact that the Lon-
don Missionary Society has been working on these
islands for 80 years, and although they possessed
during that period very great influence over the
natives, they failed absolutely to suppress bush-beer
drinking.... It may seem strange to place in writing
the fact that after 80 years’ Christian teaching by
the London Missionary Society, 90 per cent, of the
crimG is committed on Sunday, and the sworn evi-
dence in the Noo-ongo case will show that 80 per
cent, of the witnesses left church for the bush-beer
tub.”
“Bloggs”: Thus Melbourne Argus
(8/4/ 13), at its old pastime of trying to prove
that North Australia isn’t fit for white folks to live
in:—
The white man has adapted himself to life in most
parts ot the earth. . . .But the white woman has not
yet proved her fitness to live long periods in countries
hot and humid.
I was talking, the other day, to an edu-
cated Englishman who had recently ac-
complished the feat of riding from the
Queensland border to Darwin. He had
no prejudices as regards local politics,
one way or the other. What appeared to
have impressed him most about the Ter-
ritory were the white women he met
there. Some of them had been in these
allegedly pestilential parts for half a life-
time. They were as hard as nails. The
lady of one house he stayed at did the
whole of her husband’s work —mustering
cattle, managing the niggers, branding,
etc., during the six months per annum
that the latter was obliged to be away
droving, buying and selling stock and so
forth —this on the authority of the gen-
tleman himself. This observer summed
up the whole case against the Territory,
so far as he could estimate it from a
careful personal investigation which the
Argus (for instance) has never given that
region, in the words: “Insufficient soci-
ety.” The life there is healthy enough,
he said, for people of either sex, but
most infernally lonely—a disability
which is felt severely by women, who are
gregarious souls for the most part. That
is a trouble which is going to be cured
with increase of settlement; and increase
of settlement will never take place on
any large scale till unpatriotic rags of the
Argus stamp cease representing the place
as a miasmatic and fever-smitten swamp.
* * * *
“Montalex”: Although guaiacum (lignum
vitce) is of much medicinal value —hence the name,
“wood of life”—the bark renders water narcotically
poisonous to fish, and is much used for this purpose
in Borneo and Celebes. I remember going with a
Minnahassa village headman to “cash mush feesh”;
but, as he was equipped with nothing but a long-
handled landing-net, I wondered how he was to do
it. The selected spot was a deep hole in the bend
of a “crick.” From a tree near at hand the chief
chopped a lot of bark, which he pounded to shreds
between two stones. Then he threw it in, and in a
few minutes the water looked as if milk had been
mixed with it. Pop ! —a big red fish shot up as from
a gun, floundered for a few moments, and lay motion-
less on the surface. Then came another and an-
other, faster almost than the old chap was able to
dip them out with his net. As the water cleared
and the supply began to slacken, I examined the tree
and found that it was Guaiacum sanctum, a species
closely allied to the Australian representative of the
genius. Old-time bushranger Cash’s statement, that
Australian natives adopted a plan of narcotically
poisoning fish with lignum vitce bark, was probably,
therefore, quite correct.

“Rangan”: At Ilelidon (Q.) the other
day various representatives of the Department of
Agriculture and of the local shire council took a
solemn oath to exterminate a few million of the
flying foxes that camped near the town, and, inci-
dentally, gave visitors the idea that there was a
bad leakage in the sanitary system. Armed with
enough dynamite to blow the top off Australia, the
experimenters waded through the stench to a tree
black with the inverted stink pots. A wire * alive
with plugs of dynamite was entangled amongst the
branches and electrically fired. Back came the ex-
pert to view the slaughter. Not one fox was in-
jured. Some even did not trouble to fly away ; others,
annoyed at having their daily nap disturbed, leisurely
flew off to resume same on the adjoining trees.
When you burst dynamite in the air it doesn’t make
enough trouble to disturb your eldest unmarried
aunt. The best idea is .to get a cannon, load it
with swan shot, point it at the fox-roost, close
your eyes, pray hard, and then fire. After that, it
is advisable to run lest the drizzle of dead foxes
should cave your head in.
“McNorth”: Touching “Mallarapoonie’s”
belief that the camel is the only dumb brute that
can count (B. 20/2/T3), let me cite the case of
Billy. Billy was an ancient paekhorse, scion of
generations of mokes which had humped man’s goods
about for a crust. When I was tin-scratching back
o’ Cairns (N.Q.) Billy was my onlv horse, and every
Saturday I took him to the township, 10 miles dis-
tant, for stores, etc. The rest of the week he had
nothing to do ; yet he was not satisfied. He would
hang round the camp till Friday night, and then
disappear till Sunday. Several weeks I had to walk
to the township and hump the flour' and beef on my
back, pausing a while to make remarks re the curse
of education in horses. Then a brilliant idea struck
me. I would alter the day for going to the town-
ship to Wednesday. I did it, and thereafter for two
weeks I had no trouble in catching Billy. But he
was very thoughtful and somewhat aggrieved. He
seemed to think he had made an error in his calcu-
lations. When lie found out how he had been de-
ceived, I had to alter the day again. Billy was very
much surprised when I caught him on a Monday.
He nearly wept. All his careful figuring had been
in vain. When I let him go that night he eyed me
with scorn, and, turning slowly, galloped into the
bush, wearing his tail in an attitude of defiance.
He hasn't been seen since.

“Weka”: It was the last race of the day
at the Maori meeting, and Tamati Piripi, the judge,
could hardly balance on the barrel which did duty
as his official stand. The mokes tore up the straight
in a bunch, and a white, a bay and a black flew
past the post locked together. As Tamati made no
sign of giving his decision, the crowd became ini-
patient, and demanded as one cannibal to be told
what horse had won. “By cripe,” exclaimed the
judge in a burst of inspiration, “I tink te piepald
every time.”
UNIMPRESSED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Northern Territory native, brought down South
on a visit and taken to see the sights, remains
stoically unimpresed.— ltem from Melbourne Herald.
We whirl him round the stately town
To see the sights the South contains;
Calm, stoical, the man of brown
Yawns at our hoardings, shops and trains,
And City Council, pipes and drains—
A dreary trail, a futile quest.
For all our lighted parks and lanes,
The Child of Nature’s unimpressed.
The heroes rant, the comics clown.
Alas! no mummer entertains
(E’en Julius, of high renown)
This nigger from the empty plains.
Though J. C. Williamson maintains
That he alone provides the best
Imported stars, all else disdains—
The Heathen blind is unimpressed.
Without a smile, without a f-own,
He weighs our heavy Culture’s gains,
And turns our heavy Culture down ;
A scornful critic he remains.
With leaders grave the Argus strains
To educate this blighted guest,
And Joe Cook racks his Liberal brains—
The savage youth is unimpressed.
Envoy.
Oh. lily roots and sugar canes!
The Tories' rage like men possessed
In borrow, boom and bust campaign—-
The Neolith is unimpressed.
VICTORIA.
GANESHA.

On August 5, 1858, Mr. Cyrus Field announced by
telegram from Trinity Bay in America that it and
V alentia in Ireland, 2134 miles apart, had been
connected by wires. The great Atlantic cable was
no longer a dream. Her first message was despatched
by Queen Victoria to the President of the United
States “hoping that the electric cable will prove an
additional link between the nations whose friendship
is founded upon common interest and reciprocal
esteem.” Phillips Ormonde & Co., patent attorneys,
17 Queen St,, Melbourne.*

“A. Kito”: As I am going ranching in
the Dalby district (Q.), where I understand the
prickly-pear is. a calamity only equalled by State
Frights, I want to know something about the fol-
lowing, from a newspaper that I have hitherto
trusted:—
On many ranches on the Hawaiian Islands there are
cattle that have never known what it means to take a
drink of water. The cactus supplies them with both
food and drink ; the great prickly leaves, with spikes
an inch long, and the tender young shoots supply all
the nourishment required. The cattle have tongues
as hard as leather, and make short work of the
treacherous-looking slabs of green—another pro-
vision of Nature. Curiously enough, this cactus
land, which the traveller usually learns to scorn, is
worth .-£ 3 an acre in Hawaii. The cactus pear, too,
is a splendid food for pigs, and is one of the assets
of a ranch which has great stretches of lava land,
where the cactus holds sway.
I’d like to get some of those cattle, but I suspect
they would be difficult to locate.
ON THE TRAIL.
Entomologist W. W. Froggart, F.L.S.,
has been burrowing after Australia’s first rabbit.
Records show that a pair was liberated by a sealer
on an island near Wilson’s Promontory, about 70
years ago. A big landholder is blamed for originat-
ing the curse, so far as the mainland is concerned,
by unloosing the forbears of those we now have with
us on his run between Geelong and Colac (Vic.), in
the late ’6o’s. Brother Bunny was declared a pest
and a curse within the meaning of the Act, in Vic-
toria, in 1880, and about that time he arrived in
N.S.W. by way of Murray Downs station. He opened
his first branch business in Queensland in 1887.
Froggart’s investigations brought Captain Watson on
deck to remark that the N.S.W. stock returns for
1788 showed five bunnies —-first-fleeters. Among
others who unsuccessfully tried their hands at accli-
matisation was a Mr. Holt, of Marrickville (Sydney).
Captain Watson remembers that Thomas Austin, of
Barwon Park, Winchelsea, near Geelong (Vic.), let
loose several rabbits, as well as sundry hares,
pheasants and partridges, in 1864, and thereby
assisted many brother squatters on the cheerful way
to the Bankruptcy Court.

“Tom Cooper” : There’s a mystery about
the Arrino (W.A.) district that requires explanation.
Years ago it was a land of full and
plenty and a happy hunting-ground
for black brother. Wallaby, bandi-
coot, kangaroo-rat, ’possum, mallee
hens and such-like teemed; yet in a
season they, with the dingo, aban-
doned the place as if it had suddenly
become haunted by Beelzebub’s provi-
dore. Nor have they ever returned,
and to-day the only game thereabouts
consists of the kangaroo, wild turkey
and an odd lame ’possum or two.
Last year two mallee hens were found
nesting for the first time for 30 years,
but the vandals couldn’t leave them
alone and give them a chance to re-
stock the district. Local observers
can advance no theory that will satis-
factorily account for the phenomenon.
It cannot be set down to the crowd-
ing-out influence of the white man,
because it is only during the last six
years that any settlement has taken
place, and then only in small patches.
Presumably Providence has gone on
strike in the Arrino country.
“Ivory”: How are the Abo. records on
calf-branding ? At the Ord River cattle station in
W.A., seven men branded 40 calves in 20 minutes,
50 calves in 28 minutes, and 176 in one hour 28
minutes. All hands were Queenslanders, barring a
couple of W.A. blacks. On another occasion a sport
at Millungera station, on the Flinders, put up a
tenner to say that the six station stockmen could not
brand 60 calves in the hour. Right joyfully was
his 10 jim covered, and when he saw the boys put
through 18 calves in 15 minutes by his own stop-
watch, he bade farewell to his tenner like a man.
“Frae” : Here’s my one and only experi-
ence of black-tracking. I spent three days and
nights in the bush near Yea (Vic.), in company
with two trackers, in charge of Constable Wright,
then at Tallarook, looking for a lost child. Although
there was any amount of soft ground near the creeks,
and although the blacks circled round and round the
homestead in widening circles like lost homers, they
never picked up a track of any description. The
child, less than three years old, was found by her
brother, after a spell of over 100 hours of capricious
Easter weather, in the bush. Incidentally, how about
that for an endurance stunt.
“Tamumu” : This is the story of how the
cow-cocky beat me. I was living where I had to
board the train at a lonely flag-station, in order to
get to town. On my second morning I was peram-
bulating the platform, when what was obviously a
dairy farmer’s cart came along the road and pulled
up opposite the station. The driver, on spotting
me, commenced doing the semaphore act, so I
strolled across to him, wondering whose Strawberry
was dead, or what other cow-country calamity had
happened. In the cart the cocky had a can with
801 b. of cream in it, and he clamored for my assist-
ance to carry it across the paddock. I gave him the
“hand.” On questioning him, I discovered that he
brought 801 b. of cream every second morning, and
that he depended on casual travellers for assistance to
carry it from cart to station. The can with its con-
tents weighed close on a hundredweight. On my
suggesting that he should bring someone with him
to help, he remarked that there were only the
missus and the boy, and he couldn’t afford to take
the latter away from his work. This from a man
who was sending 401 b. of cream to the factory daily.
No more can-carrying for mine, I decided. Two
mornings afterwards the cocky reappeared and sema-
phored as before, but I wasn’t having any. Some-
how or other he got the can of cream to the plat-
form. As he staggered past me he growled, “You
might have given a bloke a hand.” “Nevermore,”
quoth I. “We’ll see,” he muttered. I spent the
time during the journey to town wondering if the
man was mad. He must be if he imagined there
was any way in which he could coerce me into hump-
ing his beastly cream. Two mornings lyter I was
alone on the platform. Presently the familiar "chicle
appeared. A glance showed me that I was routed.
A lonely female and the accursed can were the sole
occupants of the cart. The cocky had sent his
missur! Distress signals were soon hoisted, the jack
half-masted. One couldn’t leave a woman to struggle
with the baleful can, so Mrs. Cocky and I lugged the
flaming thing to the platform. After that I walked
a couple of miles daily to the next station, to avoid
becoming a cow-cocky’s unpaid laborer.
“A.L.8.” : Wasn’t it “Mallarapoonie” who
a while ago, said either that no large and dangerous
alligators are found in Australia, except in the salt-
water mouths of rivers, etc., or that only small and
harmless ’gators are met with in fresh-water creeks
and lagoons? Well, at Plantation Creek (Ayr
N.Q.), the other day, a man lay down on the bank
while his horse drank. A snap like the discharge of
a dingo-trap caused him to spring to his feet in
time to see his horse’s head fall one way and' its
body the other. The neck had disappeared. If the
alligators of fresh-water creeks are only little fel-
lows and harmless, what took that horse’s neck?
And here’s another case. Scene: Deep, long, wide
lagoon, with precipitous banks, on the Lower Bur-
dekin (N.Q.). Enter a huge bullock. Carefully it
made down the steep bank towards the cool water
(it was a blistering mid-summer day). Half-way
down the bank the animal seemed inclined to retrace
its steps; but it sniffed the water and went on. I
on the opposite bank, was about to let my horse
have a drink ; but when I saw two huge greenish
jaws grab that bullock by the snout, I changed mv
mind. For, I reckon, 20 solid minutes the ’gator
tried to pull the bullock into the water. Sometimes,
one would gain a foot, sometimes the other. On
level ground I’d have liked the bullock’s chance. But
presently the bullock’s strength left its knees.
Slowly the animal gave ground and then splashed
into the water, disappearing instantaneously. All
through the struggle hardly a sound had been made,
save the gasping of the victim in its effort to breathe!
“Loo Lear”: Fitzelarence went out with
his new gun, the other evening, rabbit shooting, and
returned with an empty bag and a strange story. He
said that on his side of the river the bunnies were
so accelerated that he couldn’t get a shot at them
but on the opposite bank he saw a dozen or so that
stood up and looked down the barrel of the artillery.
He shot the lot, but his difficulty was to get
across the river and collect them. Half an hour
later Bill brought in the mangled remains. “Next
time ’y get shootin’ into my traps,” he said to
Clarence, “I’ll dashed well bite you.”
* * * *
“M’poonie”: One more example of how
the white man, bred and born in a cool climate,
can’t live, let alone work, Distralia’s tropic north.
In Central Queensland, a wune back, I ran across
a tall, fair, clean-shaven foreigner, who had the
blue of a Norse parentage in his eyes, and that air
of boundless energy which has carried the white
man into every corner of the world, and made him
boss of most of it. The man was known as “George,
the Swede,” and he looked only for the toughest
and best-paid kinds of contract work —and mostly
found as much of it as he could cope with. A
better toiler never stripped under a vertical sun.
Later on it turned out that George wasn’t George
at all —that he was Gustav, and a Dane. One day
I asked him what part of Denmark he came from.
“No part,” said he, “I natif off Iceland.” Nov, a
person who claimed the Arctic Circle for an old
friend had interest for me, and I pursued further
inquiries. Ultimately, after hearing of dull and
dreadfully protracted winters and many tales of
much snow and ice, I asked Gustav if he ever,
thought of going back to his native land. Gustav
just dwelt long enough in his work to hook a stream
of sweat off his forehead with a bent forefinger;
then he snapped out, “Pi shingo ! No.” There was
a world of emphasis in those words, and if Thb
Bulletin wants any private information about Ice-
land, it can have Gustav’s other name and address
for the asking.

“Snell” : The splendid river boat Widgee,
with a great glory of flags, scenting the woodland
with radiant blue paint, and vociferous with whis-
tles, was making its first flight up the Murray,
gorged with groceries and fencing wire, and seeking
a return cargo of wheat and wool. Just beyond
Goode’s bend, a small row-boat was seen to put
off from the wooded north bank. The propelling
force was a wire-haired Scotch terrier of a man
who rowed with such frantic efforts, and threw out
so many signals between jerks, that the whole
crew of the Widgee gathered for’ard to witness the
demonstration. As the steamer drew near, the man,
generally suspected of being Scotch, arose in his
punt, and shook a warning oar at the leviathan.
Then he whirled his hat as a danger signal, and
fluttered his crimson whiskers in the breeze. “Stop •
he howled. “Dammit all, dinna ye hear me.
Stop!” The Widgee made no pretence of stopping,
and the wild Caledonian grew more frenzied. “Pull
up!” he squealed. “Woa, hack! ITa’ ye man-
ner iv sense, veh dommed heathen ye? Stop!’ The
captain of the Widgee hung his head over the side.
“And why should I stop ?” bellowed he. The man
in the boat danced up and down. “Why for? h e
screamed. “Why for?” and he pointed tragical y
up stream. “In the name o’ Chreestian manners
and common decency, that’s why for. The wife s tn
swimmiri'!” The captain of the Widgee went on,
and chanced it.
IN A LACHLAN BEND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Out at the back of the last lone station,
Down in a Lachlan Bend,
Where the white cranes flock to their recreation
And the black swans nightly wend;
Down on the beach where the fish play nightly,
Down by the stream where tfie stars shine brightly,
Up with the tent! I will take life lightly
Down in a Lachlan Bend.
Heaven-made spot for a dreamer lonely,
Down in a Lachlan Bend ;
Silence stirred by the song-birds only,
Harmonies soft that blend.
Here’s where the magpie’s carol’s sweeter,
Song of the thrush hath a ’witching metre,
Smile of the dawn, as the songsters greet her,
Gladdens the Lachlan Bend.
Far from the glamor of Fame or Fashion,
Down in a Lachlan Bend;
Out of the World, with its pain and passion,
Joys that in sorrow end.
Free from the spur of a mad ambition,
Dead-Sea fruit of a dream’s fruition,
Living a life that has no contrition,
Down in a Lachlan Bend.
Here’s where a man may with Masters mingle,
Down in a Lachlan Bend ;
Here with the Lords of Australian jingle %
May he a season spend.
“Banjo” bringeth his ballads ringing,
Ogilvie carols his sunnier singing,
Pulsing verses is Lawson bringing,
Down to the Lachlan Bend.
Close to the breast of the rippling river,
Down in a Lachlan Bend ;
Near to the heart of the Mighty Giver,
Here would I wait the end.
Fires of life are the long years damping—
Lord, I am sick of the Plains, and tramping.
Fain would I bide till the last long camping,
Down in a Lachlan Bend.
N.S.W.
BLACKBOY.
THE OLD LAND IN THE SOUTH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I can feel the green flax calling
From the swamp-lands and the creeks;
I can see the tree-ferns sprawling
Where the under-tangle reeks;
I can hear the bell-bird donging—
Oh. my heart is cold with longing
For the land I left behind me in the South.
Where the toi-toi’s whiteness
Traces where the rivers run ;
Where the range’s infiniteness
Faints in blue beyond the sun ;
Where the red, red rata’s glowing,
And the white clematis snowing
In the land I left behind me in the South.
I am back on virgin beaches
When the sunrise spreads the sea
With a lilac path that reaches
To the end of Ecstasy;
And I dream by waves that wander
From the mystic Over-yonder
To the land now left behind me in the South.
Oh, the farthest hills look greenest
In the hour of youth’s desire,
And the unknown ways serenest
When impatient spirits fire....
Ah, those barren hills and dreary,
And the spirits dulled and weary—
Head me homeward for the old land in the
South.
N.S.W.

“F.C.” : I would like to know wherein this
country appears so dreadfully sinful. All the
missionaries on earth seem to be dying to
“save us.” We have had visits from Booth,
Dowie, Voliva, Ditchfield, Chapzander and a
host of others, and now Smith and Robins
leap on our chest like two holy devils. Why?
We have no uproarious Suffragettes to be
soothed, not much white-slave traffic to be
squelched, very little municipal or political
“graft” to be dealt with, no floating or sta-
tionary gambling casinos, not even the dread-
ful “Continental Sabbath.” * It can’t be that
these people rush us because we are compara-
tively generous in the matter of the “plate,”
for they all protest it is not the “plate” they
hanker after. Then what is it? Why is it?
Where is it?
"DRTBKIWER" NURPHY, Journalist.
Parh (W.A.).
A strenuous citizen went out of action
when Samuel Vaile died at Auckland (M.L.)
last week. He first came to light in London
85 years ago, and reached Auckland with his
father in 1843. In 1850 he departed for
San Francisco in a sailing vessel, but was
left on Pitcairn Island, and had to work
his passage back to Auckland by way of
Honolulu. A year or so later he founded a
solid business, and started to look round for
elbow-room for his energies. Auckland not
fulfilling requirements, he rushed back to
London, joined the Inventors’ Institute, be-
came a Fellow of the Royal Horticultural
Society and a member of the Society of
Arts. Then he hustled back to Maoriiand,
and put in most of the subsequent years in-
venting a new system of railway administra-
tion. In between times he contested Parlia-
mentary seats (always unsuccessfully), lec-
tured, elaborated a new system of land set-
tlement, was chairman of Auckland Cham-
ber of Commerce, and wrote several books.
He was a whale for keeping up with the
procession. No whale was ever more con-
spicuously in the front rank of things.

By The Bulletin’s Oldest Inhabitant:
Apropos Coadjutor-Archbishop Mannix (B.
24/4/’l3). A Coadjutor-Archbishop or Bishop is
always appointed Vicar-General of the Diocese. The
first of the kind was Dr. Charles Henry Davis, who
was titular Bishop of Maitland and coadjutor to Dr.
Holding, but there is no mention of “right of succes-
sion.” Dr. Davis’s “see” was thg little town of East
Maitland, and he never set foot in it. He was con-
secrated in 1847, and died in 1854, The next was
Roger Bede Vaughan, who had the right Of succes-
sion, and was appointed Vicar-General on his arrival.
An Assistant-Bishop is of different standing. Dr.
Higgins was Assistant to Cardinal Moran, without
succession rights. He became an independent Bishop
at Rockhampton, and is now at Ballarat.
The late John J. Donovan, K.C., LL.D.,
was the son of a wealthy George-street
(Sydney) ironmonger. Fie was originally
an attorney and a member of the firm of
McCarthy, Son and Donovan, successors to
the business of Holden and McCarthy. In
1873 he had his name erased from the roll
of solicitors, and was admitted to the Bar.
He quickly achieved a big position on the
Equity side. A lot of his wealth was lav-
ished on charities and on the R.C. Church.
He was a bachelor, one of the oldest mem-
bers of the Australian Club, and as cour-
teous and kindly an old citizen as any in
N. S. Wales. His years were 75 when he
Hew aloft.
There are some good (but
not very new) stories in “Anec-
dotes of Bench and Bar,” i
new book just out in London.
One of them concerns the burly
Wapping and Wagga butcher
who alleged that he was the
long-lost Roger Tichborne and
got 14 years for it. One of the
counsel opposed to him was
Hawkins, afterwards a Judge.
As he entered the court one
morning a lady handed the
Murrumbidgee liar a tract en-
titled “Sinner, Repent.” He
looked at it and wrote upon
the cover, “This must have been
meant for Hawkins,” and passed
it along the barristers’ table.
In England a policeman has
always to be on duty outside
the hotel where a Judge is stay-
ing while he is on circuit. Once
Justice Grantham came down
late at night to post some let-
ters in a neighboring pillar box.
He was wearing a tweed cap
well down over his head and
ears, and the drowsy bobby, not
recognising him, inquired, “I
say, has the old gone to
bed yet?” “No,” was the re-
ply. A quarter of an hour
later a window was raised, a head was pro-
truded. and a judicial voice cheerily ex-
claimed, “Constable, the old is going to
bed now.” And the Judge used the same
word as the bobby—the word defined by
Johnson’s Dictionary as “a term of endear-
ment among sailors.”

A yarn from The Bulletin’s Old Sol-
dier :
Someone in the March Fortnightly writes about
“Archdale Wilson, the Captor of Delhi.” It is one
of the usual attempts to set on a pedestal a highly
respectable, but very ordinary man who had the
misfortune to be placed in an exceptionally difficult
position. Colonel Archdale Wilson, of the Bengal
Artillery, was in command of the Meerut Brigade
when the mutiny broke out among the native troops
there one Sunday night in May, 1857. The muti-
neers—two infantry and one cavalry regiment—
broke away, and marched to Delhi—nearly 40 miles
—the same night. Wilson still had at his disposal
two very strong European regiments—one cavalry
and one infantry—and three batteries of artillery.
He never made the least attempt to pursue the run-
aways beyond sending out a party of horse-artillery
recruits to follow and watch them, and they picked
up quite a number of stragglers as prisoners. When
it is remembered that the Delhi Brigade, which was
all natives, did not mutiny till the Meerut Brigade
arrived on that Monday morning the significance of
the failure to pursue will be understood. Brigadier
Wilson said his first duty was to protect his own
station. Some weeks later Wilson was ordered to
march to Delhi with all his available men, and join
the Commander-in-Chief, who was collecting a force
to take the place. ' By the 18th July, through the
deaths of two generals, and the invaliding of a third,
he became Commander-in-Chief himself. Here is the
situation as he himself described it in a letter writ-
ten on the same date to John Lawrence:—
“The force consists at present of 2200 Europeans
and 1500 natives—or a total of 3700 bayonets, while
the insurgents are numberless, having been reinforced
by the mutinous regiments from every quarter.”
That was the simple truth as Wilson saw it at the
time. I was there, and though I was not in the
know any more than any other subaltern, I could
hear what was said, and see what was done by those
who knew. The Fortnightly writer wants to prove
that Wilson never proposed to retire from his posi-
tion before Delhi, and quotes the above letter as evi-
dence :—•
“I have determined to hold the position we now
have to the last, as I consider it of the utmost im-
portance to keep the Insurgents now in Delhi from
overrunning the country, or turning their arms
against the reinforcements coming up from below.”
That was a highly exaggerated view of the capa-
city and enterprise of the sepoys and their leaders.
Wilson had determined to hold his position to the
last, but only conditionally on his getting rein-
forcements, as he states near"the end of the letter:—
“I candidly tell you that unless speedily rein-
forced this force will soon be so reduced by casual-
ties and sickness that nothing will be left but a re-
treat to Kurnal. The disasters attending such an un-
fortunate proceeding I cannot calculate.”
Certainly he could not calculate the disasters that
would follow an attempt to retreat to Kurnal, a
place about 40 miles north of Delhi; it would have
been impossible for the simple reason that there was
no transport. Still, the danger was very great and
very near, as everyone in the camp on the ridge well
knew. Wilson was an old man, and was no hero.
It is not to be wondered at if his nerve sometimes
gave way. I was there, and I know. Wilson was
more than sufficiently abused at the time for his
weakness, but he managed to hold on, and finally
pulled the place out of the fire—otherwise he took
Delhi. He might have taken it sooner, as some
people said, and he might have broken down utterly
but he didn’t.

“Vic” writes:-
Ballarat’s best citizen, William Scott, has gone
mto the Benevolent Asylum. Over 50 years a lead-
ing man. A founder of Ballarat East; several times
mayor, and entertained the Duke of Edinburgh in
that capacity. A founder of the very Benevolent
Asylum to which the citizens lumber him. Once
had a great boot business. Like poor Graham
Berry and his grocery, his ruin came to pass through
devotion to the public interest. Member of munici-
pal council for 38 years. Did much service to the
hospital and orphanage. Senior Ballarat magistrate
prominent Mason (is Masonry dead?), pioneer of
Oddfellows (and hows this?); also, of fire brigade
and Water Commission, Government representative
on the Water Trust, Parliamentary returning-officer
for over 30 years. Stick all that up on the poor-
house wall. 1

EDITOR
TRITCHEIT
Launceston
Examiner
(Tas.).
John Gabriel O’Ryan, who died in Syd-
ney the other day, was one of the few
juniors at the N.S.W., or any other Bar,
who knew the startling experience of hav-
ing his leader fall dead in his arms while a
case was in progress. Edward Butler, Q.C.,
with whom O’Ryan often appeared, expired,
as stated above, in the Supreme Court before
Judge Faueett. John Gabriel was from Tip-
perary. He was called to the N.S.W. Bar
in 1876, and had himself dis-barred in 1894.
Thereafter he practised as a Gent. One.
From The Bulletin’s Oldest Inhabitant:
Two old Bathurst (N.S.W.) families have been
connected by marriage recently. At All Saints’,
Bathurst, on April 22, Horace Lee, second son of
the late Henry Lee and Mrs.. Lee, of Wallaroy, on
the Plains, was wedded to Miss Linda Rutherford,
fourth daughter of the deceased James Rutherford,
of Hereford, Kelso. The bridegroom is a grandson
of William Lee, who settled at Bathurst soon after
Macquarie crossed over the new-found track or road
devised by Captain Cox. Cox got Hereford, an estate
of 2000 acres, as his reward, and it passed to Ruth-
erford later on. William Lee represented Roxburgh
in the Legislative Assembly (1856-1859). James
Rutherford, an enterprising Amer can, settled in
Australia some 60 years ago and became associated
with Walter Hall and others in the big coaching
business of Cobb and Co.
THE COMPLEAT “LIBERAL."
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There is not much to say about Mr. J. F. Hannan,
the Labor candidate, who is opposing Mr. Fair-
bairn. His one claim for votes seems to be the
fact that he is the Labor party nominee.
The old member for Fawkner has a quite different
claim upon many residents for such suburbs as
Albert Park, Middle Park, South Yarra, Queen’s,
Prahran and South Melbourne. He has been a fine
athlete, and even now, with the cares of a busy life
upon him and the weight of many more years than
he appears to carry—he is 58 —few men would care
to tackle him with the gloves. He was a member of
the first Victorian intercolonial eight—a fact for
the rowing men of Albert Park and South Melbourne
to remember—and he was always a good hand at
other games, such as cricket and tennis. He •still
plays a good game of tennis on a Saturday afternoon.
—Argus. 25/4/T3.
My free and independent friends,
Ere you record your vote,
Each candidate cross-question, and
His answers duly note.
Be satisfied, beyond a doubt,
That he will give his best
Attention (and subscriptions) to
The sporting interest!
His views upon the empty North,
’ Defence, and things like that.
Need not concern us, if we know
He bowls well and can bat.
What gain to us though he should be
A sound Protectionist?
South Yarra will not stand him if
He proves a mug at whist!
His Referenda attitude
We need not seek to know;
What Albert Park’s in need of is
An M.P. who can row.
’Tis said this fellow, Hannan, is
An earnest candidate;
But, look at Fairbairn’s tennis, though
He’s over fifty-eight!
No, no, my friend, it will not do ;
The Labor man is “o8f.”
Why, hang it, I have eve> heard
The blighter does not “goff”!
VICTORIA.
HARRISON O.
DRAWING THE LINE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. Young (to female witness): “Did you ever
see any sandbagging going on?”—“No; there was
no sandbagging in my house!”
—Divorce Court evidence.
A little screamin’ now an’ then,
Some thuddin’ on th’ floor,
Would not disturb my gentlemen
Or start a rude uproar.
They wouldn't jump an’ run or swear,
If they ’eard fightin’ on th’ stair.
They’ve all been well brought up, I guess,
An’ wouldn’t interfere
In family unpleasantness
Over the supper beer.
A little fightin’ now an’ then
Don’t worry my young gentlemen!
You can’t expec' to keep an’ ’ouse
As quiet as th’ grave ;
My lodger gentlemen would rouse
If I just tried ter save
Th’ carpets an’ th’ walls. Good lor'!
Wot else are they intended for?
Mind yer! I wouldn’t let them do
A murder on th' mat,
Or kick each other black an’ blue,
Or do garrottin’ ! That
Is much too low an' under'and!
Sandbaggin’, too, I will not stand !
I am not narrer-minded. But
I like a ’ome that’s free
From p’leece an’ gents, that always cut
An’ run from ev’ry D.
I tells my gents, straight to their face
I don't like p’leece about ther place.
Good manners is a sort o’ gift
That very few ’ave got;
There’s very few I've ’ad ter shift —
But mine's a real picked lot,
An’ I’m partic’lar, too, yer know!
If they’ve got sandbags, out they go!
N.s.W.

Very few, if any, Australian banks show a more rapid
growth than the Royal of Melbourne. Ten years ago
its deposits totalled £690,303. Exclusive of Gov-
ernment money they now amount to £1,687,458.
Its current account credits now exceed the total of
the deposits of 1903, and that is a pretty good in-
dication of the sprint of the bank’s general business.
Partly by doubling its paid capital, which now stands
at £300,000, and partly by making liberal ad-
ditions to its reserves, its own funds have kept up
fairly well with the growth of the public’s money
in the business —and that is a most important thing
from the depositors’ point of view. They want their
money protected, and they get the best protection
from the bank which has the greatest proportion of
share capital and reserve funds, represented, of
course, by good assets. In this case the paid capital
and reserves amount to slightly over £1 for every
£6 of the public’s money, and that is a very solid
guarantee fund, and quite sufficient under all ordin-
ary circumstances. Further, 44 p.c. of the public’s
money is represented by liquid assets. In one way
and another, the Royal owes £2,231,084, and it has
£991,359 liquid; this leaves £1,239,725 of other
people’s money to be guaranteed by £1,747,184 of
non-liquid assets (advances, etc.). In other words,
there could be a slump of about ss. 6d. in the £
before the depositors, etc., would be in danger of
losing any of their money. There is no sure way of
telling what kind of assets the bank holds ; but from
the size of the interest return one wouldn’t suspect
that there were many unprofitable lame ducks in
the flock. Here are some figures:—
Profits as Compared with Sharf,holders’ Funds
Sept., 1909 £8 11 4 p.c. Sept.. 1911 £8 17 10 p.c.
Mar., 1910 8 13 9 „ Mar., 1912 9 16 8 „
Sept., 1910 8 13 3 „ Sept., 1912 9 9 7 „
Mar., 1911 9 5 2 „ Mar., 1913 9 10 4 „
Admittedly, this alone isn t much of a test of the
real quality of the assets, because a money-lender
might charge a high rate of interest to compensate
for the doubtful character of the security; still,
cutting out that possibility, the figures are worth
something.
The business has shown considerable expansion
during the year:—
Mar., 1912. Mar., 1913.
Notes in circulation.. £1,649 £1,256 Dec. £393
Bills in circulation .. 115,271 66,278 Dec. 48,993
Deposits at interest.. 1,337,018 1,398,474 Inc. 61,456
Free deposits.. .. 782,363 765,076 Dec. 17,287
Liquid assets .. ..1,094,389 991,359 Dec. 103,030
Advances .. .. 1,570,155 1,690,189 Inc. 120,034
Premises .. .. 57,700 56,995 Dec. 706
The Royal has thus had to draw on its liquid
assets to the extent of £103,030, in order to make
advances of £120,034, which means that little or
no public money has been flowing in. Fortunately,
this bank held a very large amount in liquid shapes
12 months ago, and has thus been able to accom-
modate its customers during the year to some ex-
tent ; but there are plenty of other banks which
were lending up to the limit of their capacity in the
boom times, and, therefore, have found it simply
impossible to do anything for even their best cus-
tomers during the recent period of scarce money.
And that is the sort of bank that the wise man will
fight shy of—-provided, of course, he is the average
man who is liable to want accommodation from his
banker now and then. There are banks which have
had to refuse temporary advances to good cus-
tomers, who have had fixed deposits of a larger
amount than the loans they have sought, to enable
them to complete advantageous business ; and banks
that put themselves in such a position that thev
are compelled to shake their heads and turn old
and presumably valued clients away at the very
start of a period of scarce money, are worth watch-
ing. The fact that the Royal was last year able
to increase its advances substantially, though there
was nothing coming in from the public, is first-
rate proof that it didn’t put itself in the afore-
mentioned corner. And, better still, it has 44 p.c.
of the deposits and other public money still re-
presented by cash and other liquid assets, so that it
still has some distance to go before it reaches the
danger line.

over an old-established, but not well-organised music
business in Sydney about 4J years ago, and has since
been living the strenuous life: —

This co. may be doing better than the figures—and
especially the dividend figures— suggest. It plunged
into the time-payment business on a comparatively
small capital ; and its turnover increased very rap-
idly. Unfortunately, money then became scarce;
hence the management has found it necessary to
hang on to more of the profits than it m'ght other-
wise have done. It takes a heap of capital to run
a time-payment concern; already there is £151,517
on the books to represent the unpaid balances under
hire-purchase agreements. And though the share
capital is £140,233, or more than twice as much as
the co. started with four years ago, an overdraft of
£44,728 has been accumulated, and trade and other
debts amount to £12,933. Six months ago, in view
of the rapid increase of the book debts under the
head of hire-purchase balances, the management en-
gaged the auditors (Yarwood, Vane and Co.) to
make a special report on this asset. On the whole,
vvnat they had to say was cheerful. Arrears were
found to be small—under 6 p.c.—and where for-
feitures had been made, the losses on re-sales were
not great, and “easily borne by the profits. - ’ Never-
theless, a tew changes were made in the direction
of requiring a 10 p.c. deposit and limiting the term
over which payments extend to three years: and
these changes must reduce the risks. The results,
certainly, have been disappointing so far, but no
doubt valuable experience has been gained. A life-
time of ordinary commercial training would leave a
man with a lot of things to find out about this par-
ticular kind of business; and the management has
been finding out a few of those things. There ought
, a i ot of raoney in presently. The co. has
got the turnover, which is some sort of proof that
its name is already widely known; it appreciates
the risks (there is good proof of that in its de-
clared policy to pay no more than 7$ p.c. p.a. until
it has accumulated £50,000 of reserves) : and it
has a big stock ( £60,116) out of which to make
future .Profits A further point in the shareholders’
favor is that not a penny of the capital and re-
serves is represented by goodwill or other paper
assets. Liven good management, there seems to be
every reason to expect satisfactory things. The
business is waiting to be done, and there are exitra-
Pr ° fitS though they will no doubt be
erratic—-especially until Australia gets out of the
vicious habit of promoting booms by borrowing in
furious spasms, and then plunging itself into gloom
by swearmg-off piously when it can’t continue the
jag any longer.

For its size, S. Aus. has a remarkable lot of rich
people—not millionaires, but sol d five- and six-
figure men, who have got their fat out of one sort
of farming or another. Among that class this bank
is It. There is no other S. Aus. bank left; there
were a couple, but the locusts have eaten them.
And though a man may be nothing of a State
Brighter, and may put Australia first and any small
section of Australia second, he is quite likely to be
able to see some advantage in having a head office
Board and a general manager on the spot. There
may come a time when he may have to appeal to
C*sar, and he naturally sees some possible advan-
tage in being able to whisper into Caesar’s ear per-
sonally. So, one way and another, though this
bank has no branches outside its own State, apart
from the one in London, it has accumulated a fine
business; and no doubt because it can watch that
business well, the profits are very fine, too. Which-
ever way you look at them the figures are good.
Take a few to go on with:—
Liabilities.
Mar., 1912. Mar., 1913.
Notes in circulation.. £28,049 £1(5,801 Dec.£ll,B4B
Bills in circulation .. 192,890 275,358 Inc. 82,468
Deposits, etc. ..4,591,125 4,155,320 Dec. 435,805
Assets.
Liquid assets.. ..2,199,087 2,393,505 Inc. 194,418
Advances .. ..3,536,154 2,986,570 Dec. 549,584
B remises .., .. 84,506 105,627 Inc. 21,121
Liabilities thus amount to £4,447,479; and
£2,393,505, or no less than 54 p.c. of the total
is represented by liquid assets. That indicates an
extraordmar.lv strong position. But the real posi-
tion, though no doubt still strong, is hardly likely
to be so good. The fact is, this bank is banker to
the S. Aus. State Government, and, especially in
a time of furious borrowing, the State balances are
liable to seriously affect the posit.on. A truer esti-
mate may, perhaps, be got from the quarterly aver-
ages (March quarter)
~ 1912. 1913.

This covers S. Australian business onlv. Which-
ever figures you take, the nroportion of liquid re-
serves is high, and the proportion of capital and
accumulated profits, compared with deposits and
other liabilities, is large; and those two facts,
coupled w.th the excellent rate of earnings and the
large amount of profit laid by, brand the Bank of
Adelaide as thoroughly deserving the pride of the
solid little city which has reared and cultivated it.

The Farmers’ Co-operative Auctioneer-
ing Co. (Waikato, M.L.) ;
Bonus
Dividends and Written
Profits. (8 p.c.). Rebate. Off. Reserves.
1910 . . .££ 492
1911 .. £10,470 £4538 £2534 £750 8M39
1912 .. 14,696 7336 2965 1000 11,534
Premiums on shares .. £459 11 993
1913 .. 18,922 9832 4326 16,757
This co. was formed in Waikato (M.L.), with head-
office at Hamilton, six years ago, to carry on an
auctioneering and general commission business among
farmers, ihe district is fertile and has developed
rapidly, and partly through purchases of other
business the turnover has taken long and vigorous
leaps. But though other businesses have been incor-
porated, no goodwill item appears among the assets,
[here may be something hidden in the property
account, £16,508; but if so it can be only a trifle,
and this is an excellent feature of the proposition.
A more doubtful one is the very rapid growth of
the book debts and the consequent increase of the
co. s own indebtedness. Here are a few compara-
tive figures:—
„ .. , J 1911. 1912. 1913.
Capital and reserves .. £86,338 £139,969 £161,107
Liabilities .. .. 72,872 75,285 93,173
I roperty accounts . . 19,702 18,879 17,625
Book debts . . . . 136,688 195,839 236,838
stoc k . . . . 5,092 6,922 8,794
Cash 231 114 24
These figures are as at Jan. 31 in each case, and
before the annual appropriations of profit are made.
Thus for the past year the co. divides £14,158-
and a cash balance of £24 isn’t much to face the
payment with. The co. is plainly trading to the
utmost of its fair capacity, if not beyond it. Cer-
tainly, besides the reserves shown in the first table
there is now a reserve of £11,500 for bad and doubt-
ful debts. This amount has been accumulated out of
earnings not shown above as profits. It may be a
very good reserve; anyway, the management in
accumulating such a reserve at this rate seems alive
to the possibility of a considerable slump in values
in the districts in which the co. trades. And when
the co. piles up its book debts so fast it is more
than a hint that credit is free in the Waikato; and
whenever and wherever credit is free it is pretty safe
to look for inflated values; and after that it’s only
a question of time for the bump to arrive. Apart
from that, things look all right. Especially for a
co-operative co., reserves have been permitted to
grow rapidly, which is another way of saying that
dividends and bonuses have been kept within reason-
able bounds. And that policy ought to continue.
If the co. had any large proportion of its capital in
well-bought freeholds or stock, or in gilt-edged securi-
ties there would of course be less cause for anxiety.
But all its own money and £60,000 besides is repre-
sented by outstanding accounts, including p.n.’s, etc.,
and the chances are that a whole lot of those debtors
are people who have bought land on terms at high
rates. It’s a case where careful management is im-
peratively called for —with very solid reserves to back
that management up.
* * * *
“Shareholder”: Re F. W. Hughes, Ltd.
(B. 24/4/M3). The Bulletin evidently isn’t ac-
quainted with the full facts of the case in connection
with that 6 pc. dividend. True, the director’s re-
port declared it; but a week after the meet'ng, the
shareholders each got_ a circular to the effect that
“the directors, in view of the present stringent
financial situation and of the commitments of the
company in the near future, decided to defer, for a
time, the distribution of the dividend;' Meantime
vour account will be credited with the amount which
will be due to you.” And you can take mv word
for it, as one who knows a little of the inside run-
ning, that the shareholders’ chances of getting that
6 p.c. between now and to-morrow morning are ex-
ceedingly remote.

out for yourself what allowance has been made for
depreciation. At June, 1912, “12 tugs and
launches” stood in the books at £23,210. At
December 31 the item had dropped to £22,630, so
that in the six months the book value has shrunk
£5BO, which is just on 5 p.c. p.a. The other ship-
property item at June was “17 hulks and barges as

an increase of one lighter and £1666. This writer
isn t an expert valuator of hulks and barges, still

Though Brisbane had one little co-operative store-
keeping business the promoters of this mn
thought the city was due for something Lore pre"
tentious A co. was therefore projected in 100 00ft
shares, but only 32,720 of them had been traded off
by the end of the first year. Then a struggle to “t
in calls commenced; and it has evidently been a
tough one. “Of the 2800 shareholders on the regis
9000 says . the last directors’ report, “no less than
reaching ° f tbair indebtedness
reaching £8536. Naturally enough the directors
are annoyed about it, and are taking “stringent
measures to enforce payment of all overdue cads
including interest.” It doesn’t sound promising’
The directors may get a few shillings of capital
from the erring shareholder in that way but
it isn t likely to turn him into a customer
—and contented custom is what this co. wants
just as much as capital. And perhaps the
erring shareholder has cause to be annoyed as
well as the directors. Anyway, the directors them-
selves admit that things were badly run—they had
to make a complete re-organisation of the staff and
as ‘the manager has also improved the tone of the
store, shareholders may now depend upon receiving
that civility and attention to which they are en-
titled.” Theoretically co-operation in trading is the
only sensible thing. The 5000 housekeepers who can
easily keep a big store going, and by paying cash
can get the benefit of trade discounts, are fools if
they don’t want to save the fat profits that half a
dozen middlemen would make out of the job of sup-
plying them. But it isn’t so easy as tripping over
tlie mat at 11.55 p.m. The men who promote co-
operative businesses are not, as a rule, men who are
running successful trading businesses; and prettv
often they don’t see the need to pay the price of "a
man who has proved that he can run a successful
business. This niggardliness, coupled with too much
amateur control, is the cause of most co-operative
failures. It has brought the Melbourne Stores into
shallow water ; and if this co.’s Board doesn’t look
out it will get into the same awkward place. The
next couple of years will be a critical time. The
d rectors, of course, promise great changes. For in-
stance, “the excessive stock hitherto accumulated
will shortly become a thing of the past”; also “a
property has been purchased in South Brisbane, on
which a bulk store and stabling have been erected.
The property also comprises two cottages.” The paid
capital, by the way, is £10,158; and, as shown
above, a good deal of it only exists now on paper.
It may be a good thing to reduce stock in order to
attract custom ; but this paper has its doubts. Also
it has its doubts whether the co. is in a position to
buy property which “also comprises two cottages.”
It will be a tragedy if this effort to extend co-
operative trading fails, and those Brisbane people
who believe in co-operation are under some sort of
obligation to give it a hand ; but they will have to
give the management an eye at the same time.

The actually-paid capital is £15,000, and the
£15,000 transferred a few years back brings it to
the present £30,000, making the dividend really
15 p.c. Some idea of the gamble the co. is in can
be got from the first column in the table which
heads these belated remarks. Though Australia has
had an unbroken run of six or seven good seasons,
this co., through no fault of the management beyond
its inability to prophesy with accuracy, showed a
loss of £2308 in 1910 and a further loss of £629
in 1911. Last year it got back to a profit, and no
one would put his hand on his vest and swear that
the co. will repeat the performance next year. The
back-country miller has to get in early, or else
stand the extra cost of cartage back from Sydney;
and when a co. buys wheat forward it runs a lot
of risks. Say the co. buys in August in expectation
of a bad crop in December, and pays a high price.
Crops in other countries show up unexpectedly, and
down comes the price of wheat, and along with it
the price of flour. And that, of course, means heavy
losses for the miller. But, on the other hand, things
might be the reverse, and the figures for 1909 show
the result. The Young co. is in a strong position
financially. Liabilities stand for only £374, while
assets, other than plant, land and buildings, stand
at a very respectable £25,763. So unless there hap-
pens along a bad year equal in badness to the bad-
ness of all the bad years so far rolled into one, the
to be able to stand the strain.

A great mistake, when erecting new works or re-
arranging old ones, is often made in constructing the
building and installing machinery ere looking round
for means of transport of the raw material, half-
manufactured and finished goods. Messrs. Adolf
Bleichert and Co., of London, have issued various
catalogues and leaflets, illustrating and describing a
few of the many transport systems and plants which
they have supplied, and which show the solution of
the transport question and the advantages of em-
ploying such appliances. W. and J. Lempriere,
Melbourne and Sydney, are the Australian representa-
tives, and would be pleased to hear from those in-
terested. A specially trained engineer is retained to
consult and advise.*

From June to September the Bacchus Marsh Shire
Council (Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yds. of
road metal, at an average cost of 2s. 6d. per cub.
yd., and the cost of the spalls per cub. yd. averaged
2s. 4d., making a total cost of 4s. lOd. per cub. yd.,
broken and spread. Their last contract under the
hand-breaking system amounted to 6s. Sd. per cub.
yd., stacked on the roadside, and spreading Is. ;
total 7s. 3d., as against 4s. lOd.— a saving of 2s. sd.
per cub. yd. This result was obtained by using a
Bigelow 16in. x 9in. Patent Stonecrusher, and the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
manufacturers of these breakers.* [3

* * * *
Of course, some of the Ardlethan shows
<N.S.W.) are rich at and near the surface. Also,
it is quite possible—in fact, probable—that a few
•of them will turn out to be mines. But, giving all
that in, there is need for an extra amount of caution
in touching this field. The old Cornish miner’s say-
ing about tin is always worth remembering, and
specially well worth remembering just now: “Where
”tis, there ’tis.” It is the trickiest of all the com-
mon industrial metals. And the lode tin mine is
particularly tricky. After J. S. Reid and his friends
had made a pot of money out of Broken Hill, thev
sailed in gaily to make another pot out of Euri-
owie and Waukaroo. There were fine lodes on the
surface of both of these fields on the outskirts of
the Barrier. Tens of thousands were spent on the
properties; and batteries and dressing plants were
put up. The reports that have been written about
Ardlethan are poor things compared with those that
gladdened the hearts of the Euriowie speculators.
But those speculators never got a dividend. Over
50 companies and syndicates were formed in Ade-
laide, Melbourne and Broken Hill, and every single
one of them petered out ignominiously. Some of the
men who had got their experience at those fields
were next sent on up to North Queensland to battle
•with Stannary Hills. That was a great property—
or group of properties. The prospects were so fine
that an expensive tramway was built to carry away
the profits. Profits were made—they rolled out for
a time—but the mines sucked them all up again.
A quarter of a million was dropped over that group.
But there is always the Vulcan to throw at the men
who don’t trust tin lodes. Certainly the Vulcan has
a good, long record. But it was capitalised at a
figure that the Ardlethan boomsters would laugh at;
and so with all the other North Queensland lode tin
mines that have been gouged at a profit. This
merely repeated the history of Tingha (N.S.W.).
On all Australian experience', lode tin mining is a
game for the gambler, and if a man isn’t prepared
to take big chances in return for prizes that don’t
promise to be colossal in any case, he had much
letter stand out.
• * * *
The prospectus of the Southern Cross
Tin Mining Co., Ardlethan, leaves a bad taste in
the mouth. The capital is £50,000 in 100,000
10s. shares; the vendors get 50,000 paid and £6OOO
in cash for 22 acres, without, so far, any proof of
payable ore; 50,000 shares are offered at 2s. 6d on
application and 2s. 6d. on allotment, with 6d. calls;
but the co. is a no liability concern, so £12,500
is all that can be depended on. The co. can go to
allotment on 40,000 shares, producing £lO,OOO,
leaving £4OOO after the £6OOO is bitten off. The
alleged mines are to be developed on what is left of
the £4OOO “after payment of brokerage, prelimin-
ary and flotation expenses,” and the balance of the
£4OOO is also “to pay for concentration plant and
water conservation.” The prospectus further states
that capital will be forthcoming from ore to be won
from the mine, but when the present writer was
down the best of its shallow shafts the other day,
the Southern Cross had not developed a hundred-
we:ght of shipping ore, and very little of any other
sort of ore either. Nevertheless, in a burst of
generosity, the vendors agree that all ore won from
the mine is to go to a trust account as from April
1, which is a well-chosen day to start on. There
are two holes down 20ft and 25ft. respectively, and
the prospectus states that “there is every indica-
tion of lodes being developed in these shafts.” Mr.
W. J. Young, M A., “certified geolog’st, Science
and Art Department, London.” is a director, and
provides the only report on the property. “I have
again visited the claims,” he says, “and examined
a new formation just discovered in the 25ft. shaft.
This consists of the capping of an outcrop 14ft.
wide of decomposed porphyry and kaol-'n, contain-
ing tin.” The “capping” and the “outcrop” at a
depth of 25ft., and the 14ft. of width in a shaft
4ft. wide, will be hard to beat. By the wav, Mr.
Young, as mentioned just now, who is a director,
also provides the report on the property. Who is
the vendor ?

Tongkah Harbor, with five dredges work-
ing, shows a satisfactory record for the half-year to
March 31:—

Taking a six months’ average on the year’s figures
in the first line, it will be seen that while the dredges
worked more hours this half-year and treated more
ground, the total production of tin was lower; while,
on account of the verv high price of tin, the value
per cubic yard has advanced slightly. The co. has
let tenders for a sixth dredge, which should begin
building some time in June. So that although the
current half-year won’t be much affected by the new
dredge, next six months should feel the benefit.

Miner: (1) Co. is in 1,750.000 2s. shares.

Mount Morgan (Q.) shares put on 10s. (or
a little rise of half a million for the lot) in a
month. Speculators in Sydney caught on because a
large block of shares, originally owned by the Hall
family, have been taken off the market by a strong
financial group in London and Melbourne. The
monthly returns are likely to improve when the
new smelter is put in commission. Revenue for the
first quarter of this year was £252,844. As costs
have aggregated over £700,000 a year (half-year to
May 31, £387,586) for the last two years, it isn’t
safe to expect that more than 30 p.c. of the gross
revenue will be profit. The official reports say re-
markably little about development work, although
it is known that the diamond drill has been boring
west of the winze at the 1050 ft. level. Mount
Morgan is a difficult mine to handle because the
ground is heavy, and square set timbering and close
filling, don’t altogether obviate the danger of another
creep. Still the workings are comparatively shallow
compared with many other copper mines. So
there is plenty of room for development below
1050 ft.
Charters Towers (Q.) continues very dull.
Crushings have been disappointing. Its pet propo-
sition, the Brilliant Deeps, was boomed to 30s. a
while back. Its crushing of 718 tons returned the
lowest average (£3 9s. per ton) since the deep
shoot of stone was met with. With so much de-
velopment work in hand, dividends seem to be out
of the question for some time to come. New Queen
Cross has also failed to hold its position in the
share market. The Papuan holding the next lease on
the Moonstone line crushed 411 tons for an average
yield of £6 19s. Id. The Papuan was unfortunate
in having its engine shed destroyed by fire, and has
had to install a new plant. Levels are being ex-
tended to the north-east in the hope of meeting with
another shoot of payable stone. The reef is at its
best to the south-west of the shaft, particularly the
hanging-wall shoot, which made good in the New
Queen Cross. The latter is now well equipped to
raise stone at a moderate cost, but the tonnage as
yet proved in the mine is very limited.
An inspection f the North Mount Lyell
(Tas.) is to be m ...e by Alexander Montgomery, a
well-known mining man from Westralia. At the
smelter, 13,001 tons were treated for 162 tons of
blister, containing 160 tons of copper, 17,7700 z.
silver and 4140 z. gold. The average pans out 1.23
p.c. copper with ss. lOd. worth of precious metals.
Metal-bearing fluxes from the Thassis being silicious,
the furnaces were able to treat a comparatively large
quantity of Mount Lyell pyritic ore. Only 1025
tons from the North Lyell, and 1641 tons from the
Comstock were smelted. If the North Lyell ore
can be depended on to give an average extraction of
5 p.c., its value with fine copper at £7O is 705.,
which is a near approach to the average value of
the ore smelted at Mount Morgan. The latter con-
tains the largest reserves, and its smelting extrac-
tion is calculated on a mixture of Mount Morgan
and Many Peaks ore. The advantage enjoyed by
Mount Lyell is the huge deposit of pyritic ore which
cheapens smelting. The hydro-electric power scheme
is designed to cheapen the supply of electricity by
substituting water power for steam.
* * * *
Mount Bischoff (Tas.) is content to pro-
duce and send away 100 tons of concentrates per
month. For March, 19,086 tons of crush dirt were
treated—a moderate quantity considering the big esti-
mate placed on reserves (3,000,000 tons) and the
favorable tin market. Dividends now total
£2,386,500, or £lO9 7s. 6d. per share. Something
oyer 101 b. of tin oxide is extracted from a ton of
dirt ; and while working costs are below ss. per ton
of dirt, regular dividends can be paid.
Hamilton, of the Boulder, has had a look
at the Corintlran North (W.A.) on behalf of a
speculatively-inclined syndicate, which is negotiating
for the purchase of the unissued shares. Should
business result, the cash resources of the co. will bene-
fit to the extent of £31,000. This show was bought
about two years ago by D. L. Doolette from the
Oroya Exploration, which had previously acquired it
from the prospectors. Development was continued
by Doolette, and in April last he succeeded in float-
ing the affair into a co. of £lOO,OOO, in 200,000
10s. shares. More work has been done since then,
and a 20-head mill is now in operation. This plant
is designed to treat 3000 tons a month, and a profit
of between £l5OO and £2OOO is expected from this
tonnage, with an extra £lOOO when the cyanide
plant, now in course of erection, is working. At the
time of flotation, ore reserves were estimated by
Fred Morgan, of Bullfinch, to be 158,625 tons, of
a gross value of £195,448, and the profit at £52,685.
Developments below the 100 ft. level, where the sul-
phides come in, are reported to show more extensive
and more valuable ore deposits than in the oxidised
zone.

Gold dividends totalling £162.603 were
paid by Westralian cos. for the quarter ended March.
Great Boulder’s contribution of £65,626 was the
biggest, and then followed Ivanhoe’s £45,000, Kal-
gurli’s £24.000, Sons of Gwalia’s £16,250, Mar-
aroa’s £5OOO, Sand Queen’s £4500 and Golden
Ridge’s £2318. Dividends amounting to £87,986
have been declared payable in April.

* * * *
Langi Logan South (Ararat, Vic.) hasn’t
matured as fast as its neighbor, the New Langi
Logan, which isn’t saying much. In seven dreary
years it has eaten up £25.652, and all it shows is
a heap of plant, a shaft 340 ft. deep and a sprinkling
of bores. The legacy the claim was to inherit was
(1) the extension of the Cathcart gutter after that
had welled its way through the Cathcart Central,
and (2) the continuation of the main Langi gutter.
But there doesn’t seem to be much doubt now that
the Garfish Central lease gets the Cathcart gutter.
As to No. 2 expectation, the results in deep ground
being obtained by the Government drill on the ex-
treme east of the co.’s lease, convey a hint that the
gutter may be hugging the Silurian on the next
man’s block, who, if he means business, will fire in
an application to the Mines Department to get his
area bored. The other hope is of deep ground away
to the east. There would be no need for all this
surmising if the co. had put down a few more
bores, nor would there be the problem staring a
capable Board in the face as to whether the shaft is
just where it should be. Of course, the co. must get
a huge slice of the gutter, but things would be a
lot clearer if the lie of the lead could be shown in
the northern section of the property.
* * * *
Mount Elliott begins to smell like a dead
shark. When the show closed down for an alleged
“creep” and a well-advertised “fire,” the directors
stated that the closing down would not affect the
dividends. Then uncertain persons began to sell, and
presently the same directors remarked that they “had
decided not to pay a dividend, owing to the smelt-
ers being closed down,” and the Melbourne market
broke away to £4 10s. It looks as though some-
body wants Elliott shares, and wants them cheap.
Dave Murray, of the Duke and Main
Leads, Maryborough, Vic., knows how to turn out
a mining statement better than most of his con-
temporaries. Consequently shareholders don't have
to work out for themselves the hard fact that costs
last half-year went to 435. 3d. per square fathom
as against 38s. Bd. for the preceding half. The 9i
dwt. mark then touched was clearly too good to
hold. If costs could have been kept to that level
dividends would have come regularly out of the
dirt mined. As it was, the margin per square
fathom was only ss. Bd., a difference that would
vanish if the co. had a 25 p.c. increase in wages
forced upon it. Wages are now responsible for 641
p.c. of the total expenditure; timber, etc., calls for
13 p.c. and fuel 9 p.c. of costs. Shareholders got a
solitary sixpence in the term, and were glad to
escape calls, due to the poor ground off No. 15 rise
in the west end, where a blank of nearly 500 ft. has
forced the panelling line to within 700 ft. of the
faces. Co. has 10,000 ft. of gutter remaining to
panel, so the end is still pretty far off, provided the
wash keeps about the lldwt. mark. The healthiest
sign is that the lead in the north end is turning
north, thus getting parallel to the quartz forma-
tions. While this is so, and while the bores at both
ends give better results, there ought not to be undue
haste to shell out what little profits are being got.
* * * *
Events at North Britain, Blackwood
(Vic.), have not yet stirred that derelict centre.
The return for the £B6OO spent on the property is
gold worth £4OO. There isn’t much fat in that
sort of thing: The co. got some gold off a cross-
course at 400 ft., and it is on the hunt after Wright’s
shoot, which, according to the palmist, gave splendid
things above. What the co. is finding out is that
the lode stuff is more like jasper than friable quartz,
and that the gold is so fine that it almost requires
to be slimed. What such a claim looks for is more
stone and more gold and the best of treatment.
Queen of the Hills, at Meekatharra
(W.A.), had its first clean-up, the other day, when
3436 tons were treated during a 40-days’ run for
£5241, of which £lB6 is stated to be profit. This
performance falls far short of what the public was
led to expect, but in extenuation of the plant’s in-
ability to crush up to official estimates, it is pointed
out that a number of adjustments had to be made,
and heavy rain flooded the works and necessitated a
cessation of treatment for some days. The return
averages 30s. 6d. per ton, but the difference between
that amount and 505., which was the grade of the
ore treated, is stated to be locked up in the various
mill processes. High costs are attributed to the
small tonnage treated and to the stoppages afore-
mentioned. Management reckons that its estimate
of the mine will be fully realised when the troubles
usually associated with the starting of a new plant
have been overcome, and that the initial mining
and treatment costs of 245. sd. can easily be brought
down to 16s. The show is owned by the Lake View
and Oroya Exploration, and despite aspersions cast
upon its future by unfriendly critics, it is billed as
one of that concern’s most valuable assets.
* * * *
Bullfinch Prop. (W.A.) has got its main
northern series lode at No. 3 level just about where
this paper reckoned it would. Bulk sampling shows
values for the first 4ft. exposed to range from 625.
to 86s. per ton, wlrle the average worth of the face is
said to be £7 2s. 6d. per ton. A good development.
Central Red, White and Blue, Bendigo’s
biggest to-day, has still yards to go to catch up to
the records of properties like Garden Gully, John-
son’s or New Chum Railway. Still, it isn’t doing
badly for a mine on a side-line that was hawked
about at 6d. a share. To date, dividends total 70s.
per share, and as the cash has come without any
serious break, save stoppage for the new plant, this
company licks the rest of the field in the matter of
recent records. On what small things Bendigo moves
is shown by last year’s figures of the Central Blue.
In the first six months 15,471 tons were crushed:
in the second, 13,216 tons —a total for one year of
28,687 tons, or just about two months’ work for
one of the Kalgoorlie giants. It took from Septem-
ber 1910. to March, 1913, to crush 50,000 tons and
get’ out 45,5530 z. gold. Billie Cundy regards pro-
spects as “particularly bright.” What he is build-
ing on is that the downward continuation of the Cen-
tral Blue shoot of gold will be picked up to the
north on its pitch down to 400 ft. The company,
too, has the deeper formations to open up, and im-
provement in these would mean a hustle on the
market.
* ♦ * *
Mount Oxide (Q.). 160 miles from the
railway, has to class 21 p.c. copper as seconds. In
Ernest Henry’s time*it used to cost from £7 to £8
to carry the ore to Cloncurry, so it is easy to under-
stand whv the big heap of seconds, now amountmg
to over 4 000 tons, accumulated at the mine. The
deepest level is 208 ft., but three winzes have proved
rich ore below the level, and an intermediate level
off No 2 winze towards Henry’s old workings (ad-
jacent to No. 1 winze) also proves the continuity of
the ore channel. No. 3 winze is 265 ft. north of the
adit crosscut, and it is safe to assume that the whole
of the distance is in highly payable ore, the richest
streak of glance being on the hanging-wall side.
Such an extensive enrichment of copper glance is
very rare, although it does occur in large quantities
in some of the Arizona (U.S.A.) mines. Perhaps
the most heartening prospect ahead of Mount Oxide
is the approach of the railway towards Mount Cuth-
bert, for the cost of handling the ore under present
conditions is almost prohibitive.
* * * *
The blight known as suspension of labor
conditions appears to be spreading over Hill End
(NSW.). The fabulously rich gold that painted
gorgeous prospectuses doesn’t seem to have shown up
in the work so far done by most of the new com-
panies. Golden Gully Sluicing has been granted two
months’ suspension, on showing an expenditure of
over £lO,OOO for a return of £l4O. New schemes
for treating the unwilling material are being
hatched, so it is said. Amalgamated Hill End, in
atmlving for partial suspension, evidenced the tact
that £l6 000 had been spent, of which £3OOO had
been eaten up in the search for ore the rest going
in plant and other indirect ways. The advice upon
which the co. has worked is now admitted to have
been unsatisfactory. The intention was declared of
exploring and developing the deeper and new ground
by a tunnel, which will be 300 ft. deeper than the
existing workings. The application was refused.
« * * *
It hasn't taken long for the Great Luck-
now Consols (N.S.W.) to land itself into difficulties
again. The reconstruction took place in London
only a few months ago. At that time, some uncom-
plimentary remarks were passed by shareholders,
who, however, didn’t do any better for themselves
than to re-elect the old crowd, on the ground that
as this crowd had created the mess it should know
best how to clean it up. Apparently it was so bad
that no one else cared for the job. The idea was to
get down with a new shaft below the old workings
and start afresh. The main shaft has somehow
managed to get down as far as 30ft., at a cost of
£2700, including plant erection'. Then the mine
was closed down. Later on, an anplieation for sus-
pension of labor conditions for three months was
made. The warden evidently felt sore about the
matter. However, one month was granted. The
underwriters are said to have failed to take up their
allotment, and London shareholders are expected to
meet shortly to deal with the unissued shares. The
early fate that has overtaken the co. does not appear
to be unexpected.
