A
WOMAN’S
LETTER
Sydney, April 28, 1913.
My Dear Arini, —
On April 26, 1770, the little HALS. En-
deavor, 368 tans —a mere bath tub compared
to the Australian warship Melbourne —put
her inquiring nose round Botany Heads.
Her commander. Lieutenant James Cook,
R.N., was a navigator we have several mil-
lion miles of reasons to remember.
On Saturday afternoon hundreds of citi-
zens made a voyage—more or less of dis-
covery—to Ivurnell, near the historic land-
ing place of Navigator Cook. The knoll
where the flagstaff stands was emerald green
after the late rains. The waters of Botany
Bay, sapphire blue, under a cloudless sky,
lapped the headland with a long, thin line of
white foam, like the picturesque grey hair
of The Bulletin’s best poet. Off the shore
H.M.A.S. Melbourne, our first pallid hull
to fly the White Ensign, banged its guns as
Governor Strickland tugged a rope and
tossed the Union Jack aloft. His Ex.
addressed the invited guests, who were
ranged like sheep near the flagpost. The
general public, carefully fenced off by a
team of bluejackets, the Police Band, and a
small regiment of Senior Cadets, heard
nothing.
Admiral was the only speaker at the
Ivurnell show who realised that two or three
thousand uninvited guests had taken the
liberty of crossing Botany Bay to be present
at a gathering in which they had every right
to take a part. Throwing his voice out to
its highest stormy-weather pitch, and cast-
ing his mighty chest to the four winds, the
Admirable King-Hall turned on the pivot of
his heels and declaimed his sea-breezy re-
marks to the general public. “He’s real
’earty—so was Sir Harry Rawson —ain’t
above talkin’ to the crowd,” said Mater-
familias, clasping the baby, the billy, a
pound or two of sandwiches, and the family
shoes —the family having just taken its feet
out of Botany Bay.
Minister Carmichael, at Ivurnell, ad-
dressed the invited guests and the Police
Band. He was inaudible to the crowd, till,
by one of those odd freaks that wafts one
solitary sentence over the waves of sound,
he seemed to say, “That great Australian
genius, Norman Lindsay, made a model of
the Endeavor.” Probably it w T as an aural
illusion. The daily papers are unanimously
dumb. No reporter mentioned any allusion
to the artist whose mechanical genius is
only second to his draughtsmanship. Yet
the Lindsay model of the Endeavor is recog-
nised by antiquarians, who are steeped in
historic lore, as a unique piece of ancient
story, accurate to its smallest detail, and
worthy of a niche in some national treasure
house.
St. George and his mate the Dragon were
f§ted in the Town Hall on April 23. The
banquet this year was thrown open to a
limited number of our sex, who go meekly
to such extensive feeds, being unaccustomed
to devour the festive boiled ox in municipal
marble halls. On the word of a dame who
risked her best frock at the feast, no vassal
spilt a drop of gravy down her shoulder
blades, though Baron Loin was hauled
round the premises prior to the great knife
and fork display given by the. guests. J.
Lethbridge King. S.M., was in charge of
George’s beefsteak party. The Dragon was
too dead to be present.
The garden party at State Government
House was like the understudy of a resurrec-
tion. Several thousand persons —officially re-
corded as 2500—were drawn out in a sinuous
human worm that slowly wriggled, inch by
inch, from the gate to the vice-regal Hand.
The middle of the many-legged beast writhed
with impatience as it lifted its damp feet off
the sodden lawn. Far in front, the head of
the long procession could be seen shaking the
Hand. Away in the grey distance the tail
of the animal, clothed in top hats, frock
coats, autumn millinery, umbrellas (the sky
being in a weepy mood) and wrath, moved
on at the rate of half a mile a week.
Governor Strickland, defended by an
armed battalion of Gentlemen Helps, met the
impact of several thousand paws, and passed
them on to Lady Edeline, who was seated on
the verandah. With her, the Cullen dame,
very smartly clad, and Mrs. Premier
McGowen, were firmly anchored outside the
track of the Society Caterpillar. Some-
times, when rain threatened to wash it off
the grass, the Many-legged made a rush, four
abreast, at the plaid legs of A.D.C. Forbes,
who was armed with a sword, and able to
defend himself. By the time the head of the
procession was looking for its gamp and
gathering its tram-fares for home, the tail
was stamping over the feet of Private Secre-
tary Hood, who called aloud the names of
those present.
The event of the garden party frocking was
the appearance of Mrs. Ronald Forbes. She
looked like a sporting edition of one of the
beautiful Miss Gunnings. Her round hat,
with its waving blue plume, being of the
latest fashion, harked back to the Gunning
period. The gown was chiefly black with a
dash of blue and a panel of black and white.
A discreet inch or two of black silk stocking
was visible where the skirt had an artful slit
at one side. After this Vision, the average
matron loomed solid and heavily upholstered
in silk and velvet, as mere clay.
Mrs. Learoyd was submerged in the new
brocaded pile (once so popular for covering
sofas), in a violent shade of amethyst. Mrs.
Acheson, who loses her only treasure, the
youthful Mrs. Lionel Robinson, in a week or
so, lingered on the verandah in reminiscent
The chick’s departure in the Orsova
for England is imminent. Mrs. Lipscombe
hurried for shelter in a pearl-grey garment
tipped with a cosy fluff of black and white
fur. Pretty Ena Davies, one of last season’s
buds, was a Millais study in brown, with the
correct “Bubbles” expression about her brown
velvet eyes. The Arthur Towers had climbed
down from their up country roost for the
occasion. Sydney’s Mayoress, very correct
in blue, made a dignified progress to the seats
of the mighty, while Mr. Mayor gently sub-
sided into a verandah chair. The Admiralty
lady glided through the heavyweight conting-
ent, a slender figure clothed in twilight grey.
All the Strickland girls, except the baby, who
was cutting her teeth elsewhere, hung round
the parental chair, and about 2500 persons
said that the weather looked like more rain.
Lady Denman is off to England in a few
weeks, for a flying visit to the parental
nest. The wealthy Cow T drays were coming
out to Australia to stay with son-in-law
Denman. Whether the silly “eviction”
business has altered their plans this deponent
knoweth not. Anyhow, they have weakened
in their former yearning to come this way.
So the daughter goes home to have a glimpse
of mamma.
Mrs. Elliot, wife of Norfolk Island’s Resi-
dent Magistrate, is hurrying back to the
water-lapped Paradise of Pines, after a stay
in this town. Two or three tall sons are
the magnets that draw their parent to this
settlement.
The unlucky youth Quintal, who was
fatally injured in the surf at Newcastle the
other week, was a Norfolk Islander. About
a year ago he cast off the lotus-eating garb
of island life, to step into the hubbub of
work on this continent. One of the finest
swimmers on his native shore, and as expert
as a porpoise in deep seas, the irony of fate
dashed him to death in the sport he loved
best.
Norfolk Island, having suddenly leapt into
the public eye, clamors for attention in its
uncommercial methods of dealing with the
fruits of its soil. Generally they rot on the
ground. Its luscious oranges, if undamaged,
command high prices here or in Auckland.
But they are often so badly packed that they
don’t command anything but the garbage tip.
“If the new Administration wants to help
us.” says a Norfolker, “let it begin by show-
ing us how to help ourselves. We want an
expert to spend three or four months on the
island. Our people need teaching the busi-
ness of picking and packing fruit. Some
may be lazy, but many are only disheartened.
Instead of picking the oranges, which are
admitted to be almost unrivalled in quality,
a Norfolker will shake the tree. This begins
a large crop. of bruises. Careless packing
ends with prices that don’t pay for transport
to Sydney. Norfolk Island grows everything
except business habits. There’s a cry from
a small Macedonia near your eastern gates:
‘Come and help us !’ ”
The Morea is bringing to these shores,
amongst other glittering luggage, a new Sec.
for G.-G. Denman. He will step into the
gulf left by the departing Giant Quilter, who
will return no more. The new man is not
fresh at the work, for he once controlled the
household of Millionaire Astor in London. This
ought to have been a good experience in cut-
ting down expenses, for your Dives is gener-
ally thrifty in the matter of candle-ends and
bits of soap. Later on, he was P.S. to
Minto and Grey in succession, when they
Governor-Generalled in Canada, and after-
wards got a whiff of sunshine on the staff
of Islington in Maoriland. His most attrac-
tive possession is a very pretty French-
Canadian wife.
A hurried rumor states that the Gee-Gee
proposes to buy a reliable flying machine as
the only hope of getting in and out of this
State without camping in the park at night.
On Saturday he makes a breathless rush at
the arms and legs of our senior cadets in the
streets of the city. Having reviewed the
said arms, the legs will probably be still on
the march when the exile, bound for Mel-
bourne, is plunging on the bounding main
outside South Head.
The British Empire Leaguers recently
gave the glad hand to Canada’s voice on the
Dominion’s Commission. The Voice was the
eloquent G. E. Foster. In a fortunately
obscure advt., the League mentioned that
the public were cordially invited. Luckily
only one per 5000 of the city’s population
could have seen this invitation, and about
50 p.c. must have had previous engage-
ments, or no tram fares, or a cold in its
head, for the King’s Hall is a place of mod-
erate dimensions. Also, it was packed like
a herring-barrel with leaguers in various
degrees of evening undress. Any space left
over was allotted to crockery and the bun
of peace for the Empire pillars and their
friends. When a sprinkling of the public
did worm its way into the back of this
gathering, it went through a mild edition of
Rugby football before it could find breath-
ing space.
In the midst of the scrimmage for floor
space, while forwards and half-backs were
diving for extra chairs. Canada’s G. K
Foster began his address. President Wil-
liam McMillan, supported in the rear bv
two large Union Jacks, was in the chair. '
The Canadian’s homely David Harum
manner is flavored with a few Amurkan ex-
pressions. He starts at half-speed. There
are no flowers of oratory in his vocabulary.
Not one superfluous adjective weakened his
powerful speech. For its broad, human sym-
pathy and tense grip of the higher Imperial-
ism (a thing as remote from the Jingo flam-
boyance of our local Empire shrieks as the
magnetic Pole from an escape of suburban
gas), G. E. Foster’s great address ought to
have been printed in full for circulation in
our State schools. On Empire Day the
infant mind will be stuffed with sickly plat-
itudes by perambulating politicians. Here
was a firm insistence on our individual
rights as citizens, and our claim to have a
v«ice in the affairs of Empire. Various
other suggestions infinitely more useful to
the old Mother beyond the seas than yelling
“God Save” (since the Lord saves those who
help themselves) were included in these
Imperial pearls of wisdom.
t For 100 years the Benevolent Society of
N.S.W. has been filling as many hungry
stomachs as funds permitted. To keep its
birthday on May 8 the centenarian will stoke
any vacant cracks under the ribs of the
Rich, the Not-So-Rich and the Affluent Poor,
at a party in the Town Hall. President
Neville Mayman and the ancient society’s
committee have issued placards inviting
official Pooh Bahs of every size to come
along and wish them many happy returns
of—well, you know how many plates of soup
there ought to be in 100 years of Benevo-
lence.
At the Girls’ Realm Zoo party (in the
Moore Park menagerie) an able-bodied mon-
key, whose name may be Borneo Fagan,
made a smart dash for loot. After accepting a
tribute of peanuts in a suspiciously friendly
manner, our poor relation threw himself
headlong, with all his feet and his tail,
at the nut lady’s purse, which was held near
the monkey cage. Borneo tore it to tatters,
examined the contents, and filled his mouth
with small cash. When last seen by the
owner of the coins, he was spitting out six-
pences—as too valuable to swallow —and in-
dustriously chewing a penny.
Mrs. Hankey, a daughter of the Mount
Morgan millionaire D’Arcy, died suddenly
three or four months ago. A London cor-
respondent writes that Midas, who adored
this daughter, is still inconsolable, and finds
no comfort in his millions. Wasn’t Mrs.
D’Arcy a Queenslander?
F. M. B. Fisher, a Maoriland politician
of more or less tonnage, has been delayed in
Melbourne. The reciprocity chat with Com-
monwealth Fisher dragged its interminable
length over more time than either of the
Fishermen at first anticipated. Seddon
was the first to dream of closer trade rela-
tions between the Island Dominion and the
Island Continent. (At least he was the first
in recent days, for there was a period when
N. S. Wales owned Maoriland and all its
cannibals, and the business was all one
show, and Maoriland’s dried shark was our
shark, and its “long pig” was also our “long
pig,” and it had an interest in our kangaroo,
and we were all brothers.) Seddon’s death
snapped a link in the chain of commercial
continuity. Fisher, of Maoriland, has been
repairing the breach. He passes through
Sydney, on his way home, any day this
week or thereabouts. With him is a retinue
consisting of sister Mrs. Mac Ewa n, W. B.
Montgomery (who is something in the Cus-
toms), and his daughter, Private Sec. Hall
and a pyramid or two of luggage.
Ex-High Commissioner Hall-Jones, of
Maoriland, steps ashore in this port from
the incoming P. and O. boat. He brings
two daughters back from the Thames vil-
lage, where the H. Jones family camped in
pleasant places. The cares of office pressed
too heavily on the stooping shoulders of the
ex-Commissioner for Maoriland, whose
capacity for work is much greater than his
physical strength. So he gave up the billet.
. A gentle-mannered, kindly man, Hall-Jones
is an epitome of the success that dogs the
slogger who leaves the grey Old Mother in
the North Sea for the bright youth of Aus-
tralasia. He left England a carpenter, and
he carped at the old job for quite a while.
Then he was floated into politics, and
despite his lack of aggressiveness he made a
very respectable record in his new capacity.
Some of the measures which he hewed out
were remarkably sensible, and when he got
warmed up and made the chips fly in dead
earnest, he showed an occasional streak of
real genius. Also, he was of great value in
planing down reckless or irresponsible col-
leagues. In the Public Works Department
he found a wide field for his energies, so
many of Maoriland’s public buildings being
made of wood. An unusually truthful in-
formant tells me there is no fact in the
statement that Hall-Jones will return to
politics. He has a _ satisfactory nest-egg,
and means to live quietly on its nourishing
properties.
• Frederick whose death
m England is recorded in the daily press,
probably crowded more adventures into his
60-odd years on this planet than Methuse-
lah scraped together in the long term of his
unnatural life. _ Leaving the British navy
as a lieutenant, in 1877, he pursued the god
of chance in the Chilian service, and after-
wards, I. think, was a captain in the mercan-
tile marine. He sold a property in Britain
to put the proceeds into cocoanuts. Event-
ually, after seeing the ups and downs of a
roving career, he made his home on a small
isle in the Fijian archipelago, where Cako-
bau Rex used to make history of a
poor sort. Mrs. Langdale was the daughte o'f
a former American Consul in tho*P t! - f
parts. With her, the strenuous LanediT

An incurable optimist, he stood beside t-h!
post and smiled into the camera. For had
luck there was never a man to beat him in
these seas save “Jonah” Perkins, of Queen*
land. But while Perkins was being wrecked
which was his speciality, his brewery
brewing money all the time. He didn’t take
his beer-factory to sea with him, so he and
his fortunes never sank together. Captain
Langdale’s disaster was that he did take his
brewery to sea with him —in a manner of
speaking.
The once dashing Sydney girl, Nellie Hill
who married one of the Rundles, dropped
dead in a London street the other week
The Willie Cooper dame, now unpacking her
stupendous wardrobe at Fiona, is a sister
of Mrs. Wentworth and a daughter of the
late George Hill, of this city. The late
Nellie was a cousin. The Hill family is in-
digenous of the soil, and with the Coopers
and Rundles counts amongst the earliest of
the N.S.W. nobility. Unlike some of their
kind, the rapid rise to affluence found George
and Richard Hill unchanged. They wore
no frills, and. on the word of an ancient in-
habitant, offered old-fashioned Australian
hospitality to all and sundry.
With patriarchal generosity, Richard
Hill, M.L.C., fed the dejected aboriginal.
“When his home was in Bent-street
in the comparatively early days,” says
a venerable pilgrim whose beard is
covered with the frost of time, “it was
quite common to see 40 or 50 blacks squat-
ting about with a family of mongrel dogs
waiting for their tucker from the Hill
larder.” The Willie Coopers are also bitten
with a desire to give their innumerable
acquaintances (the friends of the rich being
as the sands of the sea) a mighty good time.
A Victorian black-and-white artist named
Laurie Taylor, writes that he is doing a
good deal of work for London magazines
since he settled in the Great Fog. He
digressed as a designer of two wallpaper
friezes. Otherwise he is as keen as ever
about the illustrative art.
Mrs. Nisbet Smith, the youthful bride who
used to be Vea Thiel, is showing her squirrel
furs and trousseau finery in town.
Manly golf carnival has been mixed up
with wet feet, fine growing showers and
mushrooms. Mixed foursomes occurred in
the middle of last week, with Miss Duret and
Dr. C. N. Smith as victors. Mrs. Percy
Thiel, a keen goffer, played with W. Doug-
lass, and the redoubtable Miss Trevor Jones
was coupled with H. Robertson in the battle
of the foursomes, which waged without
quarter amongst 70 or 80 competitors.
The National Gallery trustees have deli-
berately pounced on a Frances Hodgkins
water-color, “The Window Seat.” This is
a more articulate expression of the Hodg-
kins brush, than some of her daring impres-
sionist sketches. It snatched first prize in
the Australian women artists’ section,
Franco-British Exhibition. In spite of this
tribute to its technique, the ladies in the
window seat are less alluring than “La
femme se chaufe.” To the ordinary eye this
is the better of the two pictures, which both
deal with the eternal petticoat. A remuner-
ative crop of red blobs marks the “sold.”
Buyers have shied at the hazy modernist
infant, and mostly bought more beefy speci-
mens of flapperhood, such as “Venetian
Bead Threaders.”
Oscar Asche gave the Shakspeare Tercen-
tenary hobby a massive push into the public
eye on April 23. The Bard’s birthday was
kept up with every known frenzy of recita-
tion, and many slices from Will’s works in
our State schools. Actor Asche dropped
in at the Girls’ High School and other sem-
inaries and held forth in a voice of thunder
on the extreme eminence of the deceased
writer, which we cannot regard as fresh
news.
The non-appearance of the mighty Oscar
at Newtown State School on April 23 dis-
appointed the youngsters, but failed to break
up the harmony of the proceedings. The
Headmaster worked in the sweat of his
brow, the afternoon being warm, to fill the
youthful mind with Shakspeare, and Dominie
H. W. Moffitt added a lightning sketch of
Portia in her legal frills. Newtown’s Mayor
flung a few cheerful remarks at a dense mass
of bays and girls, and the President of the
Shakspeare Society failed to shake the boys
in their unswerving devotion to Juliet as
their favorite heroine.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, April 28, 1913.
My Dear Yariue, —
Though the rain and the sun competed des-
perately on Saturday, there were a lot of
smart gowns gathered on the Caulfield lawns.
Governor 1 ulier, back from Dookie the pre-
™°u.s night > ar rived early on the scene.
White spats of the well-known immaculate
brand kept his feet warm, while scrambled-
egg gloves kept his hands from getting frost-
bitten. llis face was worn bare. Help
Curwen, in attendance, bore, as his wand of
office, a neatly-folded umbrella. He seemed
to be awaiting the word to cast loose the
black lashing of the thing. Also, his tail
pockets bulged as if he were concealing a
pair of bed socks. Perhaps, though, it was
only a hot-water bottle. Mrs. George
CUirnsitle, with daughter Audrey and a
nephew in tow, were invited into the vice-
regal pen for a chat. Both the visiting
ladies were heavily upholstered, and a small
feather duster shot up from each top-dress-
ing. The last shriek of fashion was dis-
played by the newly-rt\irned Mrs. Willie
iobin and her daughter, both of whom par-
aded in care of brother-in-law Ullathorne.
Patches of geranium red showed up on her
bright blue furnishings, and a whole sheaf
of roses was packed aboard a very small
toque. Norah Field (the Tasmanian heiress
w pO is engaged to young Daintree) was
with Muftie Huon. Major Lilley promen-
aded with his wife, and Chamberlain Nevill
exchanged remarks with Meta Power, got
up like a sweet chocolate and cream confec-
By the way, Herbert Power’s horse
carried off the race of the day. The split
skirt has become popular, and glimpses of
silk-clad leg were to be had everywhere.
Only the fire of enthusiasm will keep us warm
this winter, if the split skirt and the trans-
parent neck remain fashionable. Pneumonia
and chilblains are to be the smart com-
plaints.
The Melbourne Medical Association is a
busy body. About 50 of the members meet
once a month to go studying industrial con-
ditions. This means a pleasure jaunt away
from professional work to inspect a local
factory or workshop. The proprietor is
warned of the impending visit, and meets
them on the doorstep, with, I trust, a hand-
ful of cigars and a bottle. This month, a
piano factory was subjected to the profes-
sional examination. Exactly what medicine
men have to do with pianos remains a
baffling professional secret, like the system
of fee-scales. Perhaps they were bent on
finding the germ of the concert epidemic now
raging in Melbourne.
c< “Weenie” Ffrench (sister to Nellie Melba’s
Sylvia), who has been in N. S. Wales with
mamma and stepfather De Boulay, was lately
restored to Melbourne .by her pleasant-
looking bridegroom. Her new name is Aber-
crombie. The knot-tying happened at Rose
Bay, Sydney. “Weenie,” otherwise Irene,
thumped a typewriter in Dalgety’s, till a
serious illness terminated her commercial
career. When she lay ill in one of the big
hospitals, Melba and Auntie Bob Power
brought her flowers fit for a star actress.
It was something to be a star invalid, any-
how.
Mrs. Rubber Edwards has sent out cards
for a farewell frivol of the tea and scone
variety, to be handed round and poured at
her Cliveden flat. She and her husband
intend to thoroughly overhaul the U.S. before
steaming across to Europe. During the
parental 12-months’ trip, the small family
will inhabit the flat, and the fleet or herd
of motors will be left at its disposal.
Two militant sunragettes are menacing
the peace of this city. They are Margaret
Hodges and Harriet Newcomb, fervent l'ank-
hurst enthusiasts, and, of course, they find
the cowlike calm of the enfranchised Mel-
bourne woman rather irritating. Margaret
considers that a great opportunity to adver-
tise woman’s needs was thrown away in the
recent Eight Hours’ procession. These two
mature females are clothed in deception,
after the manner of their kind. Their mild
tepid manners give no idea of the extent to
which they revel in cable news of bombs in
empty houses, bonfires in pillar-boxes, and
lumps of blue metal in muffs and handbags.
Ao one would suspect them of being capable
ot tearful explosions when roused. They are
revisiting Australia, after years of absence
(they once ran a training school in Sydney),
looking for Australian recruits for the
Womens International Suffrage Alliance
whmh is billed for tall talk at Budapest.’
lhey have secured one private, but 12 more
are required. W’hat a golden opportunity
tor earnest souls tormented with a mis-
sion !
The Pinschofs are preening themselves on
the engagement of daughter Elizabeth to
Walter Moore Finley, an American now
doing business in Sydney. The knot will be
tied towards the end of this vear Sister
Garmen, who has just been loaded with com-
phments and other inedible substances at
Wellington (M.u.) for her performance of her
own name-part in Bizet’s opera, is not going
to London with operatic intentions. On the
contrary, she is going with the very ordinary
idea of keeping husband Harris from feeling
lonely during the long trip. Her lyric out-
bursts will, as of yore, be strictly amateur.
'»Tr T i le eid -Osmont knot was duly tied on
vyednesday. Bride Muriel is one of our
nicest girls, and the bridegroom, ’tis alleged
in a stage whisper, has money to incinerate.
Ihe ceremony, which was performed in Aunt
Christie Jones’s best parlor, at Camberwell,
was shorn of all expensive trimmings, a
travelling suit taking the place of the regu-
lation bridal yards of satin and lace. Dad
iteid, a pillar of the Stock Exchange, gave
away his only child, and wore a subdued ex-
pression the while. But as she has boarded
with Auntie of late years, his home won’t be
depleted by her change of residence. After
a Sydney honeymoon, the young couple will
start housekeeping in a specially decorated
Cliveden flat.
Dr. Norris, who has just moved on to
Condon to inspect the would-be Australian
immigrant, was (with Mrs. Norris) the
guest of honor at Dr. and Mrs. Cherry’s tea
party, in the University Club room. Nor-
ris in his day carried off enough scholarship
way through the ’Varsity,
and his two boys have been left behind in
order that they may try and break Dad’s
tine record. Norris seemed quite satisfied
with his prospects on the other side, though
he turned up a more lucrative post to go.
Amongst the polite tea jugglers present
were Professors Berry and Woodruff, Dr.
Constance Ellis, the Tate couple, and Miss
Beilis. The Norris cot at South Yarra now
shelters the Hemmingtons, who also belong
to the healing profession.
East Ihursday afternoon, Dot Heath was
converted into a man’s permanent Darling.
1 astor McCrea, of Toorak, went out to
Malvern to do the joyful job. Papa Fred
Death gave his girl away in his own front
room, which was expensively decorated for
the occasioh. Bridegroom Harold Darling
brought along a sister from Adelaide to
bridesmaid, shoulder to shoulder with
Myrtle Heath. The guest list included only
near friends and relations. Later in the
afternoon, the newly-wedded pair left on a
honeymoon which is to terminate in the holy
dty ot Adelaide, where the home will be set
up for keeps.

Maggie Mather, a favorite piano-wrangler,
got off tickets amongst her best friends, and
ran a concert at the Masonic Hall last
Thursday. Her confederates included violin-
ist Ernest Toy and mezzo-something Mar-
garet Murdoch, with accompanist Harold
Elvins to back things up. Maggie in real
life is “Madame” Beutler. (She wears the
French prefix because she is married to a
German.) She served up her husband’s own
compositions on the piano, and violinist Toy
also gave some selections from the same
source. The feverish applause still con-
tinued after tributes of tired chrysanthe-
mums. done up in bilious neck ribbons, had
been handed over the platform edge. This
uproar was intended to call up the com-
poser, and Maggie, garbed in old gold satin
with beaded edges, duly trotted him out. He
wears a Beethoven expression, helped out
with long hair and a rugged countenance.
Margaret Murdoch, frocked in mauve, also
carolled a little Beutler. She did it in pier-
cing tremolo’d German, with a severe ex-
pression, as though she meant every
“Ach !” and worlds more. Both ladies were
rather distant and unbending on the plat-
form ; but for this perhaps the.dressmaker is
to be blamed.
Artists Douglas Richardson and Margaret
Baskerville close their picture exhibition
partnership at the Athenamm, this week.
Their friends rose nobly to the occasion and
brought along all their artistic acquaint-
ances. Some came up to scratch and
bought; others just stood off and admired till
they nearly burst. Margaret shouldn’t do
portraits not pale blue pastels, at any
rate. Most of her models suffer from the
trimmings they felt it their duty to wear.
One of them seems to be in fearful agony with
cornflowers tickling her left ear. Douglas’s
water-colors show fine bits of seascape and
country corners, whereas his allegories and
subject spasms in oils are not quite up to
Watt or Leighton, perhaps. In fact, it is
hard to say what some of them are up to.
The Horticultural Society started its
autumn carnival of fruit and flowers, in the
I own Hall, on Wednesday of last week. The
big, bare building blazed with enormous
chrysanthemums, and a good many pounds of
eating and cooking apples were also on view.
John Madden, C.J., having grouped himself
carefully behind the rampart of vegetation
lining the edge of the platform, said things
far more flowery than the decorations. Gaud
Mayor Hennessy, wearing his brightest
smile, escorted his Gaudess, who was bur-
dened with a pink bouquet. She blushed
on the platform in shot bronze blue, and
showed a top-dressing of sables and the best
tail feathers of a sky-blue ostrich. Actor
George Titheradge—always a keen flower-
raiser—-looked in and had a chat with Mad-
mul, rF k e, 7 k. not met for a grood 20 years.
Ihe Gaud-Mayor lig-htheartedlv broke the
regulation which forbids the distribution of
exhibits amongst the public in the hall by
persuading his Gaudess to wrestle with her
tightly-bandaged veil and bite a giant grape
from a prize bunch. The show, which re-
mained open until Thursday, was well at-
tended by all the Very Best people who take
their gardens seriously. The number of
such is growing unbeautifullv less, as the
flatting habit spreads. Soon there will be no
aristocrats of floriculture. The old rosebilitv
will be extinct.

POLITICAL
POINTS
The bitterest election scuffle in Victoria is
in the Bourke electorate, where ex-Presby-
terian parson Robb is trying to oust Anstey.
Robb has left the pulpit, but he hasn’t yet
dropped one of its unpleasant little ways—
the habit of making large, irresponsible state-
ments. And, having been a parson, he is
unused to being contradicted. Thus, w 7 hen he
animadverted on the Labor pledge. Anstey
retaliated by quoting the pledge taken by
every parsonical follower of John Knox, and
asked, if that were proper, why should not he
take the pledge of his gospel likewise. Robb
said this was an attack on his private cha-
racter, which was a perished sort of howl,
and drifted into the old, w T orn-out parables
about fracturing the marriage-tie, destroying
religion, nationalising babies, and all that
thomas cat. Anstey has fought many elec-
tions, but reckons this the most unpleasant
of the lot.
[F. C. Jones, Elgar-road, Burwood, Victoria.]
Having to comply with the Electoral Act
or cease drawing its salary, the Tas. Legis-
lature met on April 22, passed a resolution
giving the Complex Ores Co. a year’s grace
to complete its works and prevent its valu-
able concession reverting to the State,_ ob-
tained four months’ Supply, and on April 24
closed the political front door with a bang
until August 26. It was quick work, but a
Government with a majority of two cannot
afford to take unnecessary risks. Still the
session isn’t a record for the island.
a two-days’ meeting of Parliament in ’58 —
opened to-day, as it were, and shut up to-
morrow, so to speak.
Sydney Telegraph broke out last week
with an article on “Cotton-Growing in Aus-
tralia,” in the course of which it expounded
that cotton couldn’t possibly be grown at
white man’s wages. It would be interest-
ing to learn why. Supposing nigger cotton
is shut out by a sufficient tariff, this coun-
try could supply itself with white-labor
cotton and white-labor cotton goods just as
easily as it can furnish itself with white-
labor meat or wheat or potatoes. Every
community which isn’t utterly rotten can
afford to nay itself for supplying its own
requirements, provided it has the soil and
climate to produce the goods. The idea that
the white man is so expensive that he can’t
afford to be alive, and so costly that he can’t
afford to work for himself, and must have a
twopenny nigger to do his toil, is a purely
slave-labor idea. And no country ever pros-
pered for any length of time on slave-labor
ideas.
THE CRY OF THE PIG MOTHER.
The Labor Government took credit for everything.
It was even said the pigs had bred faster since they
assumed control. — Joke Cook.
THE PIG : " Shure , Cooksey , darlin , there's
divll a worrud of a loie in ’t. Tell me
now , wasn't it the Labor Govermin' in-
vinted the Maternity Bonus?"
From the “campaign points” of a Sydney
Tory daily:—
Women, ask yourselves how it is that while wages
have increased in recent years, the task of making
both ends meet is as difficult as ever.
But seeing that it plainly and obviously
isn’t, why should any woman deafen herself
bv shouting such an absurd question in her
own ear? Also why should she dislocate her-
self by trying to get her mouth round to her
ear to ask the question? The working
classes of Australia are, and have been for
the last few years, better off than at any
time in the previous century. As an evi-
dence, the people of N.S.W. have accumu-
lated as much spare cash in the Savings
Banks since 1900 as they did in all the pre-
vious days since Captain Cook. Therefore,
it is absurd that a foolish paper should ask
foolish conundrums about the difficulty of
making both ends meet. In any case, who
expected women to make one end meet?
THE INEVITABLE RECOMMENDATION .
Mr Bruce Smith pointed out that Federation was no experiment, having been successfully adopted
as a solution of the complex political affairs of many ancient middle ages... .The federation of the Aus-
tralian colonies had been under consideration by English statesmen and by the greatest Australian
political authorities, for over half a century prior to the adoption of the existing Constitution.... The
framers of the Constitution had ransacked the Federal Constitutions of past times and selected a
feature here and a feature there, in order to construct a scheme that would be acceptable.

[T. COLLES, THE BULLETIN OFFCE, Sydney.]
“Jones” appears for the defence: —
Melbourne Argus lately printed a huge plan, show-
ing “blocks of land along the Transcontinental rail-
way route taken up by members of the Westralian
Labor party,” and invited the public to consider
how wicked a thing this was. As a matter of cold
fact (not cold feet), three Labor members held areas
among some 60 shown on the plan ; the blocks are
all outside the Federal reserve, and mostly nearei
the ocean than the line ; they are State lands, taken up
under Liberal-made State laws; they were taken up
in the same way and on the same terms as the land of
dozens of other citizens; they are by no means the
largest holdings; and, anyhow, it isn t a r ederaf
matter at all. But while it was on the scent of
railway land dealings, the Argus need not have gone so
far from home. A couple of far more interesting
maps would be those of the land along the routes
of the unprofitable Fern Tree Gully and Outer Circle
lines, just outside Melbourne. And the plans need
not show the blocks adjacent to the lines, but only
those through which the lines were run, and
prices paid for the strips taken. There wouldn tbe
any Labor politicians’ names there, but Liberal
M’s.L.A. would show up largely, some of them men
whom the Argus ever spoke of with respect.
The tail-end or final fragment of the
N.S.W. railway strike was the usual com-
plaint that strikers were being “victimised”
—in other words sacked —because of their
share in the worry. The “victimisation”
problem is always a difficult one. If an
ex-striker does anything short of rushing at
the rear of Johnson and kicking him real
hard when he isn’t looking, he can’t be sacked
without a complaint that he is being "vic-
timised” for his share in the recent indus-
trial upheaval, which complaint is liable to
lead to new trouble. In other words, the
man who has been on strike may consider his
position thereafter impregnable, just because
he has been on strike. And on the other
hand the management may start running
the strike leaders out of the service out of
pure cussedness, knowing that it can always
plead that they began to get unbearable en-
largement of the head by reason of their
fancied impunity. It is a false position on
both sides.
For haughty contempt of the public intel-
lect it would be hard to beat a suggestion in
a leading article in the Age (19/4/ lo) that
at the last election the Victorian Labor
Senators were returned as the result of a
split vote. The Labor three polled 648,884
votes, and the Fused trio 620,511 ; but to
these the Age adds the votes secured by
Parson lionald and 1 ida Goldstein, bring-
ing the alleged pro-Fusion vote up to
692,474. As a matter of fact, it was the
Labor, and not the Fusion, vote that was
split in Victoria in 1910. Vida Goldstein, a
Laborite in all but name, stood as a demo-
crat, and condemned the Fusion and all its
works. Ronald, who was returned to the
House of Reps, on the Labor ticket in the
first Federal Parliament, also stood as an
independent democrat. The argument that
a large proportion of the votes recorded in
favor of these two would have gone to the
Fusion nominees had the independent brace
stood down is a brazen effort to mislead
those electors who do not know any better.
If the figures published by the Age signify
anything, they show that the Labor Senate
majority in Victoria should have been about
70,000 greater than it was.
[Albert John Owen, 129 Grey-street, East Mel-
bourne. )
That clever Victorian girl, Miss Vida Gold-
stein, is standing as a candidate for the
House of Reps., her constituency being
Koovong. which is Heidelberg, Kew. Haw-
thorn, Camberwell and a heap of Intense
Respectability. She has a better chance at
this game than in her two previous shots at
the Senate (in 1910 she was seventh with
53,583 votes among eight candidates ; Ronald,
with 18,380, being the tail). Robert Best,
who lost his Senatorial job in 1910 by a scrap
of a deficit (he had 213,976 votes against
Blakey’s 215,117), now represents Kooyong.
But he and Miss Goldstein run on very much
the same ticket, save that the lady is the
more progressive of the two, and as Labor
hasn’t put up a candidate of its own, it will,
to a great extent, make her a present of its
vote. Also, a lot of women will vote for her,
and it is a district that is heavily manned
with women. And a lot of men will vote for
Miss Goldstein because of her pluck and
brains, and because she is good to look upon,
and would ornament any stodgy old House.
[F. C. Jones, Elgar-road, Burwood, Victoria.]
The most interesting anti-Defence man up
to date —one J. R. Firth —wrote in a blaze
and howl of glory to Sydney Telegrai j h last
Monday, and wound up with this trumpet
snort: —
If child conscription is not the meanest act ever
perpetrated by a civilised people, let some valiant
defender of the compulsory clauses contradict me.
Firth evidently thinks the Defence Act has
no supporters in Australia, and that, instead
of being passed by popular vote, it was
shoved on to this country by that bloated
autocrat, the Shah of Persia.
The wonderful art of passing on the re-
sponsibility, as exemplified by Adam when
Eve shoved along the apple, is manifested for
the thousand billionth time by the appoint-
ment of a commission to consider the advisa-
bility of establishing a Greater Sydney. And
it was manifested for the thousand billionth
time minus one in the appointment of the
North Shore Bridge Commission. Surely
these things, after being inquired into for 50
years, don’t want further investigation. The
great need is for someone to get a brick and
a move on him, and start doing things. In-
quiry has been pursued till the poor thing is
quite exhausted by hard running.

THE BIRDS OF PREY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Though the Hill of Malabar is rather far from Home
or Paris,
And some distance from Yassberra or from Brisbane
or the Block ;
Still, in calm and thoughtful hours 1 behold its
heathen towers
Where the palm-leaves rustle softly and the flying
sextons flock !
There, ’midst sand and solemn dirges undertakers
leave their perches—
Flying ghouls who wait for business on the Hill
behind Bombay ;
And. . . .for reasons sound and valid, in my mind
those ghouls are allied
With our own peculiar Tories—our Australian Birds
of Prey!
Noble palms there are that bear them, ere they rush
to rend and tear them —
Palms and fresh, sweet-odored gardens where the
Towers of Silence are ;
Fragrant groves and tropic cultures where the corpse-
removing vultures
Drowse in placid expectation on the Hill of Mala-
bar !
There they stand—the Towers of Silence —many a
long and heated mile hence,
Where the boats steam out to Suez from the shores
of old Bombay ;
Yet... .Australia’s Upper Houses, where each vul-
ture-Fat carouses ,
Seem to me like Towers of Silence thronged with
drowsing Birds of Prey!
Hark! They hear the sound of warning—’tis the
Parsee’s chant of mourning—
Every eye is swiftly opened, every vulture heeds the
call;
For their hunger knows no shortage, so they watch
the distant cortege—
Pleasant ghouls who feed and fatten on each Parsee
funeral!
Then, with wings outstretched and rushing, see the
flying sextons crushing—
See them fight upon the Towers which look down on
warm Bombay ;
Thus... .with sharpened beaks and talons, leaving
bones alone for balance,
Prom our Legislative Councils swoop Australia’s
Birds of Prey !
See! They circle round and hover —when the
funeral rites are over,
And the corpse is thrown before them, how unani-
mous they are;
Yea, the dead Parsee will fatten, so the vultures
gorge and batten —
’Tis the pleasant Parsee custom on the Hill of Mala-
bar !
Then the sextons, fat and drowsy, perch like ghouls
obscene and frowsy,
And the bones alone lie gleaming on the Towers of
far Bombay ;
Yet..../ do not blame the native — scan our Cham-
bers Legislative —
On Australia’s Towers of Silence drowse our Tomj
Birds of Prey!
See our Towers!—six or seven—how they lift their
heads to Heaven,
How they stand beside the Yarra, and at Brisbane,
Hobart, Perth;
And, of course, the Holy City has its sample—more’s
the pity,
Also Sydney, where the sextons croak and drowse in
sodden mirth !
There are priests likewise who guard them —Premiers
we fools placard them,
Watt and Denham, James McGowen —all this land’s
one vast Bombay :
And. .. .when vulture-Tories, flapping, at the bones of
Bills are snapping,
I remember Western India and the Parsee s Birds
of Prey !
Hark! They hear the sound of warning—'tis the
plundered people mourning,
Whilst the noble James McGowen staggers up the
Tory stair;
See! Another Bill has perished—Law the people
yearned for, cherished
As a hope and aspiration, made for Fat to rend and
tear!
Thus, the Peakes and Denhams stagger—Watt just
now’s a carpet-bagger,
Who meanders round with Holman by the Thames
in Bull’s Bombay;
And whilst hopeless millions cower, Bills are
flung from House to Tower —-
All the Empire’s darkened over with the rushing
Birds of Prey!
Very far, of course, from Paris, India’s Hill of
Malabar is—
Also distant from the Houses where our sacred
Tories flock ;
Still, in calm and thoughtful hours, I can see our
Silent Towers —
Are we all Parsees and heathens, that these flapping
vultures mock?
For to-day, midst solemn dirges, every stout Bill-
sexton urges
That this hapless land forever must remain one vast
Bombay ;
50.... f0r Reasons sound and valid, all these Tory
ghouls are allied
With those undertaker-creatures—Western India’s
birds of Prey!

SPORTING
NOTIONS
The Rugby League, the Rugger Union,
and the Soccer clubs all ended their practice matches
last Saturday, and next Saturday will see the nomp
and circumstance of the premiership competitions.
The chief event of the afternoon was the League
match between Glebe and Newtown at Sydney spores
Ground. This was a benefit in aid of Cubitt and
Neil, each of whom got a leg broken in play last
season. Newtown went down, blithered to death by
26 points to eight. It was a great game, and
yielded a net profit of ill 19, to be divided between
the two game roosters, as King O’Malley might say.
Brother Larkin, boss of the N.S.W.
Rugby League, sent two teams to do missionary work
at Bathurst last Saturday; and they did yeoman
service in the good cause. Bathurst arose and took
the teams, North and South Sydney, to its bosom,
and nurtured them on fine victuals and rare drinks.
About 1500 people emptied themselves on to the
Sports Ground to watch the, to them, new game,
and saw a fine demonstration out of which South
clawed its way to the top with 23 points as com-
pared with 19. At the subsequent feasting Miller,
M.L.A., asked why footballers should not be allowed
the same privileges as cricketers; but no one could
tell him, or if anybody could that person didn’t.
Micky Meagher, president of the Bathurst League,
arose and said that the talk of professionalism in
connection with League Rugby was just plain
“twaddle” and the public was getting tired of it.
Which is gospel truth. Liberal allowances are made,
but the teams playing do not divide the profits be-
tween them, or anything of the sort; neither are
players paid fixed stipends, as they are in some
forms of purely “amateur” # football in Australia.
While the game is run on its present lines it must
go ahead, because those lines are dictated by com-
mon sense and business instinct.
* * * *
On grounds nicely softened by the first
winter rains, and surrounded by applauding crowds,
varying from 3000 to 20,000, Melbourne’s hoofball
season started on Saturday. The chiefest concussion
was at East Melbourne, where Carlton tackled last
year’s premiers—Essendon. The Old Blues have been
successful only once there in the last eight years,
and they wanted to break the long sequence of
routs. So no points were given away. On the whole,
the game was fast and clean, with good marking and
kicking; but of system there was absolutely none,
the passing being wholly of the casual order. A
stiff breeze blew down the ground, giving each team
the lead alternately, but Don’s majority was never
enough to swear by. In the middle of the last
quarter Carlton drew level, and, keeping up the
pressure, piled on two goals four behinds more, and
at bell-ring was on top with 83 points to 67. Across
the park, on the Metropolitan ground, Melbourne was
expected to demolish Collingwood, but the latter has
abandoned its unseemly custom of losing the earlier
matches, and, having resurrected the beginnings of its
once-famous system, took amole revenge for the un-
holy walloping is sustained in the initial match of
last season. Ultimate scores: ’Wood, 60 points;
Melbourne, 37. The redoubtable battlers of South
Melbourne entertained St. Kilda, but had some diffi-
culty in carrying out their barrackers’ advice to
“make mincemeat of ’em,” and though they man-
aged to squeeze home by nine points—-63 to Saints’
54—there was much luck about the performance.
The old Red-and-Whites have been indulging in argu-
ment with their committee, which body suddenly
dropped five of them the previous week with a dull
thud, and the team, as a whole, has" hardly got over
the shock yet; Richmond went to Geelong and
reaped defeat—37 points to the Seas-'ders’ 55 ; and
’Roy accounted for ’Yars ; ty—79 to 37 —without un-
duly distressing itself. None of the Ass. games gave
much of a shock to anybody, though P’ran was
hardly expected to soifFcate Brighton in the hollow
fashion denoted by the scores—los to Brighton’s 45.
Essendon.polished off Northcote in workmanlike man-
ner—--60 to 35—-and North acquired 75 points to
Melbourne City’s 31. The Brunswick-Port d’sturb-
ance should have been an evenly-balanced contest.
but wasn’t, ’Wick having the "measure of Port
throughout, and piling up 98 noints to 40. During
the riot, Gotz of Port and erst of Carlton, got his
nerson in the place where the ball ought to have
been, and retired badly damaged. Gotz has had poor
luck ; of late years, and this writer would advise him
to adopt a less strenuous —er —recreation —sav, chauf-
fing or election canvassing. ’Scray and ’Town col-
lided in the violent manner customary with neigh-
boring teams, ’Scray emerging topmost with 54
points to 35.
* * * *
The Union brand of Rugby in N.S.W. is
getting more kicks than halfpence this season. At
Bathurst, the stronghold of inland football, the
result of the League s missionary enterprise was that
the City Club, last year’s premier, went over bodily
to the League, and sufficient members of otfier clubs
broke away to make three teams with which to start
a League competition. The Union remnant now
consists of the Bathurst Club, two college teams and
a XV from the Experimental Farm. The college
teams make no secret of the fact that it is only
the fear of being attainted as professionals that pre-
vents them crossing bodily also.
•* * * •
The N.S.W. Rugby Union is bringing to
Australia a team of Maoris, which will figure in
eight matches in the Ma State, starting on June 9.
After that the dark band will make for Brisbane.
Otherwise things in the Union orbit will be quiet,
the only other excitement being home and home
matches with Quensland, at Brisbane on July 5 and
Sydney on August 2.
Horse-owners, trainers and stewards of
race meetings throughout this turfy land will be in-
terested in the publication of a pocket edition of
the “Rules of Racing,” revised up-to-date. It was
compiled for the V.R.C. a while ago, and Secretary
Byron Moore has put it on the market at the modest
price of 18d. a copy (vide advt. in this paper).
Herbert Power’s five-year-old Nari made
some amends for a long series of disappointments, by
winn'ng a decent stakes—the Glen Huntly Cup—at
Caulfield last Saturday. The unlovely gelding pipped
Safrano (the favorite) on the post, some good horses
being behind the pair. Long ago Nari was declared j
to be very fast when in the humor, but this defeat
of Safrano and Mala, with an 181 b. pull in the
weight, doesn’t make the brute look anything to
speak of, even on his day. The most notable event
of the racing at Caulfield was the glaring failure of
a certain Rhombus to win a steeplechase. The
horse, which had lagged behind whilst the favorites
were coming to grief, looked so palpably like being
ridden to lose that jockey and owner were disquali-
fied for two years.
The Victorian Club has had to sky the
towel in the bout with the V.R.C. over the racehorse
Audacity, wh'ch brute, haying been well backed for
the last Newmarket Handicap, was ruled out of the
event, owing to being unregistered, The V.R:C. had
cancelled all bets, while the club had ordered its
members to pay and receive—-mostly receive. Coun-
sel’s opinion on the case having now been obtained,
the decision is against the club both on the law, the
the facts, and the grounds of high policy, Punters
therefore save their money from the paws of Jericho,
and the Book of Lamentations is loud in the land.
* * * *
Tohnny Summers thanks his guardian angels
for helping him to stoush Bob Turner at th
Sydney Stadium.
As Omar Whakem said on the occasion of
the Johnson-Jeffries fight, so it was on Saturday
night at the Sydney Stadium, when Bob Turner
essayed to push his way over Johnny Summers, to
once more share in the loot and glory of the game:
Oh, talk of stoush and threats to pulverise,
One thing is certain, old Time ever flies —
One thing is certain and the rest is lies,
The pug that once retires for ever dies.
Although the scrap lasted till the 11th round, it was
by the grace of Summers. That astute young man is
not out to win his fights in a round or two at the
expense of his hands. He prefers to throw himself
up against his rival’s midriff ; and there it was that
he smote Turner hard and numerous —almost as
numerous as the Bulgarian army outside Adrianople.
Turner made a very gallant attempt, but he was out-
classed. He could not hit nearly as hard as Sum-
mers ; neither could he protect himself to anything
like the same extent; and when one man in a fight
has the margin both of skill and brute force, there
isn’t much in it. Only the lucky blow—-the random
shaft between the joints of the harness —can pull
that sort of proposition out of the fire. But the
lucky blow is the gift of the gods, and the gods were
not fighting for Turner on Saturday night. In the
11th Summers grew weary of restraining himself.
Turner had been badly damaged amidships, and the
time was ripe. Like a flash Summers landed left
and right twice each to the head, and Turner went
down. So dazed was he that he never took the
count, but struggled up and leaned against the
ropes. The first man to see his helpless condition
was Summers, and he looked appealingly at the
referee. The referee signed him to go on. So Sum-
mers smote some more. The ropes held Turner up,
and again Summers looked appealingly to the referee,
and while he looked Turner slipped to the floor. Then
the police intervened and Summers was declared the
victor. What Turner’s seconds were thinking about
not to throw in the towel is a mystery; but the
chances are that from their angle they could not see
how helpless their man was; and they had pro-
bably been deceived by the fact that he rose from
those four severe knocks without taking the count.
Next Saturday night will see another at-
tempt to “come back.” This time it is Bill Lang,
who will endeavor to do the resurrection act, with
the head and bingy of Petty-Officer Curran for his
take-off. The scrap will take place as usual at the
Stadium, and the popularity of William is so great
that it will be necessary to get along early to get
anything like a decent seat. If Billiam fails to
arrive, it will not be for lack of proper preparation,
for he certainly has done all that a man can do to
get fit. He is more anxious to win this fight than
any he ever took part in, as he feels that victory
will remove the stigma of that unfortunate occasion
when he went baresark in London and smote Curran
while the latter was kneeling. Certainly, but for
losing his head, he might have won on that occasion.
On the other hand, Curran is just as anx.ous to prove
that he could win otherwise than on a foul. So,
providing the stamina is there, it will be a battle
great and grim. * #
Hughie Mehegan was welcomed back to
Australia last Friday night at a dinner at Hill’s
Cafe, given by Mr. It. L. Baker —he of the Stadium,
whom perfect strangers coarsely speak of as “Snowy.”
With Mehegan were three visiting boxers, Harry
Mansfield and Alfred Spenceley, lightweights, and
Sydney Stagg, a welterweight. Mehegan is in per-
fect health, and not the least downcast by his re-
verses over the water. He did his best, and he
knows that here in Australia we are quite convinced
that his best is darned fine splendid. Also, we
know he is a game loser, which, though not so
lucrative, is just as honorable as being able to win.
That the decisions of some of the English referees
were hotly questioned by excellent authorities is
well known. Mehegan’s next battle in Australia will
probably be with Picato, at Melbourne.
The news that Matt Wells, Hughie Mehe-
gan’s London opponent, has agreed to come to Aus-
tralia under engagement to R. L. Baker, caused
great rejoicing among the brethren this week. He
will, of course, be pitted against Mehegan here, and
probably, against Summers also.
* * * *
Having broken a thumb, Sydney boxer
Dave Smith will be unable to keep his engagement
to, if possible, put a head on his brother pug, Les
O'Donnell on May 10. However, the brethren will
be solaced with a scrap between Jerry Jerome and
Pat Bradley, which is not the same as the other, but
should be just about as good.
* * * *
Rowing has gone through a strenuous time
in Adelaide of late. The trouble, to an outsider,
would appear to be that the city crews are jealous of
those .of the country—represented mostly by Murray
Bridge. A crew was picked to represent S.A. in the
inter-State race, rowed in Adelaide last Saturday.
Murray Brdge objected, on the very reasonable
ground that the strongest eight had not been selected,
and offered to prove its case by rowing the said
selections, which seemed fair. The picked crew de-
clined, and after much tongue-wagging and ink-
spilling, resigned in a body. Entries for a test race
were called, but Port Adelaide was the only metro-
politan club to come up against the Murray Bridge
gang, and on April 19 the Bridge horde fled away
from the Port multitude, and won by 300yds.
The inter-State Eight-Oar Race at Ade-
laide, last Saturday, was a record and a ring-tailed
roarer. To postpone the race was diffic It, and to
row it looked about impossible. The t de and the
wind were opposed, and angry, foam-crested waves
howled over the course. It was 5.30, with the light
fading fast, when the event, in a sort of way, started
itself. The crews had been told that as soon as they
could get into any sort of line they were to go ; and
in anv sort of way they went, and when they had
been gone about lOsec. the starting gun blew out.
Westral'a got the worst of it, the Sandgroper boat
being almost broadside on when the rest went off.
But the ’Gropers came round and went after the
bunch with a bound The N.S.W. boat’s poppet
string carried away almost immediately the start
was made, and that mammoth liner dropped out. At
half a mile Tasmania was leading South Australia
bv half a length, with Victoria struggling a couple
of lengths further back, and Westralia another four
lengths at the tail end of all things. The Queens-
land boat was not in it, having sunk. South Aus-
tralia (Murray Bridge) overhauled Tasmania, and
took a lead of a couple of lengths, the cox baling with
a billy per medium of one hand and steering with the
other.” Victoria and Tasmania were then together,
and Weatralia eight lengths behind them. Next the
Westralian boat filled and the crew ran it ashore.
Victoria’s boat was a-wash and in a hopeless plight,
land so was Tasmam'a’s, owing to the cox. having lost
his bailer overboard. South Australia, by hard
shovelling out of water, was enabled to skim along,
and won by about 200yds. in semi-darkness. Tas-
mania was second, having managed to struggle over
the line somewhere. Immediatelv afterwards tlrs
boat fell overboard also. The Westralians emptied
their boat and nluckily started again. They scored
third place. Altogether it was the wildest eight-oar
race ever seen in Australia.
* * * *
There were only two contestants for the
Single Sculls Championship at Adelaide on Saturday
—Marr, of Queensland ; and Coverdale, of Tasmania.
Coverdale had the advantage of 31b. weight over
his opponent, who just turned the scale at lOst. It
was a one-man show. Before 100yds. were covered
Coverdale was leading by half a length. This advan-
tage he continued to increase, till he finally won by
10 lengths. The scullers were able to hug the
shore and get comparatively smooth water.
The mystery surrounding the challenge to
Barry from a Maoriland “Unknown ” begins to un-
ravel itself. This joke from Mokua angered a Maori-
land paper, and it let a sleuth-like reporter loose.
He discovered that the challenge evolved in the
fumes of a convivial gathering. The defi. to Barry
was worth about as much as the fumes. Many
people thought it was a trick on the part of Richard
Arnst to lure Barry into another match; but Richard
was entirely innocent.
Glory be to Joss-Confucius, the Sydney
First-Grade Cricket competition ended on Saturday,
and North. Sydney, which slew Sydney by 82 runs
on the. first innings, established itself premier club
with 21 competition points. University, which
dished Paddington by 79 runs on the first innings, is
runner-up with 19, and Redfern is third, being away
on the horizon with 11. It has been a disastrous
year for most of the clubs. Burwood and Gordon
managed to accumulate only seven points each during
the season, and they were rich compared to Glebe,
which did the beggar or Lazarus act with one. But
there is a lower Sheol yet, deep as the above-
mentioned is. Look at what the following clubs owe,
and weep, if your stony eyes may dissolve in tears:
Central Cumberland (Parramatta), two; Balmain,
five; Sydney, five; Waverley, five; Middle Harbor,
six; Petersham, nine; and Paddington, 10. The
defeat of Sydney was not a thrilling episode. North
having banged up 271 the prev'ous Saturday, started
on the job of getting Sydney out for as few as pos-
sible. Long was the most successful bowler, and got
away with four wickets for 63 runs; Gregory got
his single stick for two runs; Punch two for 45,
and Hopkins two for 32. But it wasn’t the bowling
of the Northmen that broke Sydney up ; it was the
weight of the anxiety. A single century-getter would
have changed history. Not a man of them let him-
self go; thev all played cautiouslv—and were dis-
missed for 189. University’s handling of Padding-
ton was very different. The previous Saturday Pad-
dington had left 155 on the board. It was nassed
when onlv two wickets were down. Then ’Varsity
ran on till three were down for 234, and declared.
But the day was far spent, and it, was a first inrnngs
win only. A special interest attached to this season’s
competition: Both North Sydney and University had
won the. Rawson Cup, the outward and visible sign of
the premiership, twice, and a third win would make
it the absolute property of either of them. North
Sydney has it.
* * * *
Brisbane (Q.), like the other State capi-
tals, has put up the cricket shutters, as far as its
First Grade is concerned. The honor and glory
abide with South Brisbane.
* * * *
I Hardly was the N.S.W. cricket season
coinfortabiy closed up when a bombshell was dropped
among the failures of the Grade competition. Certain
of the teams which failed to toe the mark regularly are
to have a recommendation heaved at them by the N.S.W.
Cricket Association next season. Certainly, there is
something very rotten in the state of Sydney cricket,
and the long list of clubs which finished the
season owing points suggests the necessity for the
pruning-knife. Where this deplorable state of affairs
was due to the inabil'tv of the clubs to rake full
teams into the field, the most sensible thing is to
wipe out the helpless institutions and nut thp m „ ,
of their misery. Meanwhile, there is terror m-inw*
ing and gnashing of beer. ’

Unexpectedly large gate receipts were again the
order of the day at yesterday’s practice a.iatches.
At the Sports Ground no less a sum than £'l7l was
received. — Daily -publication.
The starling flutters from the pines;
The butcher-bird withholds his notes;
Some silver-eyes within the vines
Are wheezing in their tiny throats.
Though tired is all the feathered band,
And Nature’s songs are weak and thin,
Chirrup! Chirree! On every hand
The umpire whistles winter in!
What though with sharp and vicious trills
The postman whistles at the door,
To leave a hundred little bills
Which up to now were whistled for?
We go to him with joyous heart;
For, light of foot and bare of shin,
The football umpire’s made a start
To whistle all the dollars in!
Our loafing’s ended, so to speak!
Ten thousand blokes in steady jobs
Will idolise us every week,
And swell our coffers with their bobs !
We players do not care a jot
Whichever side may lose or win,
do long as to the common “pot”
The gate receipts come rolling in!
The whistles shriek on tram and train!
The west wind whistles ’cross the ground !
Each ferry boat takes up the strain
And magnifies the piercing sound!
Yet sweetly' shrill and rising clear.
Above the harsh commercial din,
One blast rings out! From far and near
The paying push comes trooping in!!
N.s.w.

“L.H.P.”: Antony and Cleopatra,” on Sydney
Royal boards, once more recalls ancient memories
of Louise Pomeroy’s production in February of
1882 at the same house. Sydney has had to wait
31 years for another. The American star was Cleo-
patra and J. B. Steele was Antony. Joe Tolano
(now a boniface at Woolloomooloo) was the clown
and Harry Leston played Lepidus. Harry Jerdau,
so long at West’s, appeared as Csesar, and Theo Ham-
ilton stage-managed and did Pompeius in between
times. Pompeius is out in the present version because
Asche drops all the M'senum scenes. In 1882 John
Musgrave was really fine as Enobarbus, and Maggie
Stewart made a shapely Charmian. Clint and Hen-
nings did some fine scenery, and the revel on Pom-
pey’s galley was a feature. Herbert Flemming was
Eros, and Sam Lazar or Alison (I don’t know which)
made the ads. glow with “over a hundred auxiliaries
on the stage.”
Una Bourne’s engagement with Melba at the be-
ginning of the year must have been a valuable intro-
duction to the English provincial musical circles, for
that gifted young woman Una figured as solo pianist
of Melba’s touring concert co., other supporters of
the star be'ng Edmund Burke, the basso whom she
brought to Australia ; a flautist (Moyse) and a piano
accompanist (Lapierre). Writing to her friend, Mr.
Laurie Abrahams, on January 21, Una Bourne said
that the concerts were going splendidly, but the man-
ager was much troubled by the flautist and piano
man, for neither of them could speak English, and
when they went for a walk together they invariably
lost themselves.
Passed to Paradise, England’s oldest clown, James
Doughty, aged 95. He went into the show business
in 1835 as clown and acrobat at fairs and such like
public oc ions. Soon he was promoted to the
theatres, for he was three kinds of clown —singing,
talking and tumbling—a rare combination At
Drury Lane he was engaged by James Anderson,
tragedian and otherwise powerful actor, who will
be remembered by old Australian playgoers. When
under another management Drury Lane, the
“National Theatre,” was transformed into a circus.
Doughty again clowned there, and made a hit by
donning bloomer costume, which had just then been
invented in America. E. T. Smith, father of Mel-
bourne’s Dr. L. L., was another manager who ran
Drurv Lane as a circus. He engaged three clowns,
of whom Doughty was one. During his latter years
Doughty lived on a company of trained dogs, which
beasts he exhibited at seaside resorts and minor
music-halls.
The 70-year-old Sarah Bernhardt has been in a
smash at Los Angeles, California. On her way to the
theatre her motor ran into a heavy truck, and the
fragments of it flew up and hit the stars. Sarah
only flew halfway up, so she returned in time for the
performance with nothing worse than a wrenched
ankle, a bleeding lip and a few bodily bruises. In
spite of the misadventure she insisted on playing as
usual, and was, of course, applauded for her pluck.
“You are too kind, my friends,” she said to the
audience, as she faced it with the wreck of the
vehicle still in her hair. “Your sweet sympathy has
healed all my woes. When the heart is young
bruises don’t last long.”
Ethel Irving was, at latest advices, going into
management on her own account in London. Ethel
has apparently no fear of ridicule, for April l was
billed as the day on which she was to start at the
London Globe. Most manageresses would have care-
fully avoided that date through dread of being
written down an ass. Ethel thinks that she has
spotted a winner in “Vanity,” a new play by Ernest
Dennv, who scored a success some time ago with
“All-of-a-Sudden, Peggy.” An English comedy fresh
and sweet,” is how Ethel describes “Vanity.” Solo-
mon, who ran a show of his own in Jerusalem, called
it “vexation of spirit.” Dion Boucicault is to pro-
duce the piece—Australia’s old friend, Dion.
“Kodak”: My picture-show education is incom-
plete, and I am studiously neglecting it. This is what
i have learnt up to now:—
(1) All elderly men, who wear the variety of
whiskers known ’as “sideboards,” are hard-hearted,
and are doomed to humiliation at the hands of the
angel child. They always perish while saving the
angel child from something or other.
(2) All angel children are d —d fools with fire—
also with railway trains, balloons, rivers, wild
animals and traffic.
(3) Roll-top desks are not intended for writing
on. Thev are used principally to support your head
while you think of What Might Have Been, and weep
bitterly into the ink.
(4) When the agitated wife and mother works
her mouth as if she were chewing a piece of tough
steak, she is merely saying that John should have
been ’home long agoj and that she doesn't know what
can be keeping him. (P.S.—This is what makes
picture-show dialogue so nourishing.)
(5) Even the least emotional men throw their
wives about when the usual domestic crisis arrives.
(P.S.-— If you have hitherto neglected to do this,
please make a note.)
(6) If you don’t gesticulate like the picture
people when you talk, there must be something
wrong with you—or with the picture people. Are
you stiff in the joints, or have they got the jumps?
(7) Fat men are the funniest because they have
more face to express themselves w'th, and are gener-
ally bald in addition. The very best humorists are
shaped like eggs. .
(8) No house can long contain an angel child and
a guilt''’ secret without bursting.
(9) Af-er you have read the screened synopsis of
the coming scene, vou really don’t need to see the
pictures. But the pictures—which the showman in-
s;sts on giving you—are not understandable without
the synopsis. This indicates what a complete sort
of thing the picture-show is. (“John. Brings Home
News of the Tragedy at the Mine”: Victure of John
masticating the atmosphere in front of open-mouthed
audience.) , , .....
(10) If the film drama starts happily, it is going
to end sadlv; if it starts sadly, it is going to end
hapnilv. The glad element of surprise has been
eliminated by the various improvements in the lan-
tern In the cheerful, early days, the operator used
sometimes to get burnt and be heard swearing in the
<^a ('ll) It is as well to know the marks of the angel
child m order that you mav start hating her early.
The chief sign is that all the characters, good, bad
and rotten, grow gloomv at her approach. She also
(a) prances along to indicate a sunnv disposition:
(b) clasps her hands to indicate ingrained piety;
and (c) holds her left chest w’th both hands as a
sign that her heart is full. Any picture child who
has none of the above tricks is a plain kid.
The newest beauty fad is crawling. .Not
the chicken crawl ragtime, nor the habit ot
crawling to persons of title, but actual cavort-
ing on all-fours about the floor or on a lawn.
The thing started in the London hospitals,
where it was prescribed for spinal weak-
ness. “Good for indigestion,” somebody said ;
so the fad got another crop of adherents.
And then the figure! There really does seem
fo be something in it so far a.s indigestion is
concerned. Who knows —if Pierpont Morgan
hadn’t been a slave to habit and Walked
upright, he might have lived another oO
years!
Francie Adler, of Australia, seems to have
made a stirring success in the brand-new
grand opera, “Cyrano,” which was staged
the other day at the Metropolitan Opera
House, New York. Francie, who is now
Madame Alda, created the main role of
Itoxane. New York paper pictures of the
star show her as a very plump lady nowa-
davs, with her black hair a-curling round
her head. She is married to the director of
the opera.
Florence Hamer, the copper-crowned
actress who played here in “The Marriage
of Kitty,” lias just achieved a third husband,
and retired from stage life. She divorced her
first venture, and married a tea-planter
named Masson. This marriage was also a
failure, and the dark-complected gentleman
was kindly but very firmly divorced. Now
it is confidently anticipated that the right
man has transpired. The lady declares it
was worth suffering her two previous trials
to secure her present blessing.
The vast Butt took possession of America
the other day with the emphasis of an ele-
phant attaching a bun to itself. Eulogistic
press cuttings followed her about like faith-
ful dogs. As regards N’York, it may vera-
ciously be said that she came, she saw and
she staggered it. But she is proudest of her
Continental European succesfees. In Berlin
two princesses (Margaretta and Leopold ot
Prussia) came tb hear her sing. As they
neither rose and ostentatiously left the the-
atre, nor threw things at her, the deduction
was drawn that they approved of her efforts.
Hopeful suspicion became joyous certainty
when she and her husband were asked to tea
by the Royal ladies next day. In Germany
a princess would be in danger of losing her
job if she fraternised with an artist who
couldn’t sing, in much the same spirit as a
Jew who can’t make money is often forced
by his co-religionists to re-name himself
O’Flanagan. Vienna and Budanest also
approved of the contralto, and she scored a
huge success in Paris.
Melbourne is having its pier strengthened
and a special gangway prepared, both jobs
to be completed by the end of this month.
By the way, the immortal children are to
be stationed, not here, but in Sydney. Your
Mr. George Innes has found them one ot the
few local edifices, suitable to their needs,
that have not been transformed into hash-
towers. There is room in this mansion ror
16 nurses and nine chauffeurs, not to mention
kennel-maids, dog-watchers, stewards, major-
dbinos. stud-grooms, chefs, under-chefs, keep-
ers of the draw-bridge and other customary
features of a compact villa residence.
The Denman lady will board the Orsova
at Adelaide, on May 15, and travel to
London, with Major Quilter and Help Nut-
tin- in charge of the luggage. Her Ex., who
feels the need of a change, is going to stay
for a few days with mother. The rest of her
few weeks’ sojourn in England will be devoted
to paying overdue visits, and she will return
to Australia early in September. The infants
Thomas and Judith are to stay here and
keep their vice-regal pa from feeling too
lonely. Mrs. Cudworth, a newly-imported
addition to the staff, will keen a trained eye
on their infantile gyrations and chide them
when necessary.
I have just received the following intima-
tion from distant Edinboro’ (N. 8.)
Mr. Charles Skinner and Miss Elsie Skinner an-
nounce the marriage of their sister Elizabeth to Mr.
Allen C. Hinckley on Monday, February 3, at the
United Free High Church, Edinburgh, Scotland.
Hinckley, of Melba’s Grand Opera Com-
pany, brought a batch of introductions
to these parts which franked him into ex-
clusive circles. He proved a great favorite.
In fact, he presently became known affec-
tionately to all hands as “Hinck.” A man
must be popular to achieve that sort of
thing. Did anyone, for instance, ever slap
King Charles the Bad of Navarre on _ the
back and call him “Baddy?” It isn’t
recorded.
At a recent wedding-feast here the man
who sat next the bride was named Mr.
Judge. I don’t think people with painfully
suggestive names like that should be allowed
at weddings, any more than we would, in
these enlightened times, stand, at such a
ceremony, that depressing old w T edding-guest
whom Coleridge mentions as “beating his
breast” because “he heard the loud bassoon.”
For that matter, bassoons at weddings—
however, enough.
A Melbourne girl, who last year went in
for matrimony on an Indian tea plantation,
took exactly four weeks to discover that
marriage was a failure, and another seven
davs to put a stretch of sea between herself
and hubby. He fled imploringlv after his
bride, and was rejected by her in London.
She is now heroically tackling the job of
earning her own bread and butter, hats and
’bus fares. The arrival of a _ lusty infant
threatens to complicate the situation,_ and
mother may have to reconsider her decision.
An Englishman bv the name of Clarke
has just arrived with the intention of breed-
ing cocoanuts by the million in our waste
tropic spaces. A fellow passenger with this
enthusiast was Miss Maisie le Soeur, a much-
talked-of footlights charmer, who went to
London two years ago, pursued by a perfect
maze of thrilling and romantic rumor, bhe
is somewhat slimmer than of yore, but in
other respects looks absolutely scrumptious.
Sincerely yours,
JOHANNA.
“Melb ”: Smith and Robins, Yankee soul-snatchers,
have been and seen and conquered. They were in
Melbourne for exactly four days; and in that brief
space they found out all about the city s Sins and
the best way to deal with them. It reminds me of
Petion, who, during the French Revolution, was sent
to England to obtain a complete knowledge of Eng-
lish law. He did it by sitting for two days in the
Old Bailey.
WANTED A DEVIL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. Robertshaw’s Mephistopheles is rather a con-
ventional devil. . . ,
From a Melbourne criticism of “Faust.
O gifted actor, won’t you please
A brand new devil give to us,
A novel Mephistopheles ?
We’re full up of the crimson cuss
Of “Faust,” whose roasted chestnuts all
Date back beyond his primal fall.
The lank, lean devil who is grim,
A ghastly kind of bore we deem.
We’re weary unto death of him
Who breathes a kind of purple steam,
And devils who are too polite
The popular contempt invite.
We ask the artists to submit
A well-bred devil free of creed,
Philosopher and genial wit,
With human sins. In short, we need
A fiend with whom we all may be
Content to pass eternity.
VICTORIA.
