ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will he
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. Ao
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.
A.A.: Isn’t there something wrong about
that yarn re the utterly miserable wages for which
women toil in the Vic. hop fields? Why do they do
it ? Almost any woman who likes to call herself a
cook or housemaid can get three times the money you
mention. Furthermore, seeing that there are only 121
acres under hops in all Victoria, and they are scat-
tered over five counties, it is hard to understand
how, in one centre, the hoppers live in “rows” of
miserable humpies. Surely one row would hold them
all. Please supply details C. J.T.: Faced with the
problem of why your strong and healthy six months’
old infant’s complexion is snow-white, we are fain to
own ourselves baffled. We have no information to
impart, no advice to offer, no anything. .. .Grad:
Even if it were worth while touching, it is too late
now. . . .Mulga : Not up. .. . J.P.H.: To your know-
ledge the matter forwarded has never appeared in
print? Quite right. To our knowledge it never
will. .. .C.H.M.: Essay on “Some Things About
Women” read to The Bulletin’s tame flapper oyer
a corn-beef supner and repudiated as dull and sin-
less J.W.R.: Pleased to print “The Night Ex-
press” :
The engine-driver stood on the footplate,
While onward swiftly sped the train,
He was leaving his home and mother,
Perhaps never to see her again.
He had sworn he would be true for ever
To the girl he had prom’sed to wed,
And though thousands of miles did them sever,
Still to his stoker mate he huskily said:
“I wonder if she is thinking of me now,
Or if some other chap s arm her waist entwines,
Or if her lips are pressed upon his hard ones,
To be away from her is very hard lines.
“I don’t suppose it matters much, anyway,
I always was a most unfortunate chap,
But mother is a true and constant friend to me
Through good or ill or any other dire mishap. >
“And she would keep my memory ever green,
If I should happen dead to fall to-night,
And hang my photo, on the dining-room wall,
That framed enlargement makes me look all right.
“I remember ’twas that photo, made my darling
love me,
And decided her to link her fate with mine,
And I hope my mother and my sweetheart
Will be thinking of me as I race along the line.
“She often said she wished I could give up this
work,
And come home white at night instead of black,
She didn’t know I wasn’t always like that,
This is dirty work and that’s a fact.,
“Hark! What was that?” he said, with staring
eyes,
That through the darkness peered straight ahead,
What was it made him stagger back in dread?
The head-lights lit the track for miles ahead.
For in the very centre of the track
Stood one who was as dear to him as life,
For whom he would have shed his last drop of blood,
She whom he hoped some day to call his wife.
She stood there while the train sped swiftly onward,
No fear she showed or anyth’ng like fright,
A frightful shudder shook his manly frame,
“My God!” he cried, and leapt into the night.
Henry P.: You ask if “a ‘fair day’s work’ is
one that will earn some profit for the boss” ? Well,
if you don’t contrive to own —either singly or in
a co-operative fashion —your own factory, machinery,
capital, buying organisation, lying organisation,
selling organisation, office, book-keeping system,
and all the rest —in fact, if you hire these
things from another man, a fair day’s work
will probably have to leave some profit for
the boss. What is a fair day’s work for a horse?
Is it enough to feed the animal, or is it supposed also
to cover the driver’s wages and wear and tear of the
cart, harness and whip? Till the co-operative horse
acquires brains enough to drive himself, he will
probablv go on earning oats for somebody. . . .Con-
stant Reader: (1) Can’t tell you what was the big-
gest figure ever attained by the British National
Debt. It reached its apex at the end of the Napo-
leonic wars, but the accounts were in such _con-
fus'on that these estimates were made by eminent
statisticians: —
Doubleday .. .. ..£944,152.000
Whittaker . . • • • • 900,436,000
Porter . • . . . . 885,186.000
McCulloch . . .. .. 840.850.000
(2) Can’t say. Various Chancellors of the Exchequer
fake their figures in various ways. Probably 1888.
(3) At March 31, 1913, £674,744,567 and about an
equal amount of local debt. If the two are added
together there is a steady increase in Britain’s lia-
b lities. (4) Too tired to figure out British debt
per head. Do it yourself. (5) The Commonwealth,
at June 30, 1912, owed £277,124,095, or £5B 10s.
3d. per inhabitant. But remember, that the local
debt here is very small, while in Britain it is enorm-
ous ; that the public debt here includes almost all
the capital of the railway system, wh'le the thirteen
hundred million pounds, which represent the British
railways, are not included in the British debt; and
that th's country gets very wild when local rates
reach 2s. in the' £, while 10s. in the £ is by no
means uncommon in Britain.... Ralph Wood: Only
a lecture, and not a very cheerful one at that
J.D.K.: Britain’s debt is really growing nowadays
about, as fast as any other debt on earth, but much
of it is called local debt, because Britain shoves
almost all its public works expenditure on to local
authorities. Railways are private affairs, and their
debt is private debt, so the so-called National Debt
is almost entirely a war liability. In 1891 the
United Kingdom owed about £927,000,000 of Gov-
ernment and local debt. In 1911 the combined bill
was about £1,315,000.000, an increase of nearly
£400,000.000. The privately-owned railways of the
United Kingdom are in debt (in the way of deben-
tures) to the extent of somewhere between
£400,000,000 and £500,000,000. Haven’t the ex-
act figures at hand Gerald Barken: Once more,

Public Opinion and
Beale HigK-grade Pianos.
The great majority of our Pianos are sold on the personal recommendation of users, and we are constantly receiving letters from our
customers praising the Beale High-Grade Pianos. Our instruments are in use in every part of Australia; in New Zealand, Fiji, Samoa, New
Caledonia, Mauritius, South Africa, India, and on the Continent of Europe; and everywhere they give the same unbounded satisfaction.
Below we give a few (greatly condensed) extracts from unsolicited testimonials from buyers of Beale High-Grade Pianos.

We have literally thousands more
such testimonials, from almost every
part of the world. The immense suc-
cess attained by the Beale Pianos is
due solely to their high-grade quality.
Prom the very first we have gjven our
utmost endeavor to turn out only thor-
oughly reliable, soundly constructed
pianos, embodying the very best musi-
cal qualities. We have created a won-
derful factory organisation, and we
pay the best brains and the best skill,
so as to ensure a perfect result. We
realise that an Australian-made piano
is subjected to much closer scrutiny
in Australia than any imported piano,
and therefore we make-—we are
compelled to make —our pianos much
better than those of our competitors.
At the same time, by making pianos
in Australia, we are in the position to
make them much better suited to Aus-
tralian conditions. Any competent
cabinetmaker will tell you that timber
seasoned in the cold, damp climate of
Europe and America cannot be ex*-
pected to withstand Australian ex-
tremes of dry heat as well as timber
which is seasoned- in this climate. This
is a most important point in piano-
making, and is one good reason why
so many foreign pianos develop cracked
sounding-boards, split tuning-systems
and faulty casework soon after arrival
in this country. But no man can say
he ever saw a Beale piano with a
cracked sounding-board. We abso-
lutely guarantee that the sounding-
boards of Beale Pianos will never
crack in any climate, because our spe-
cial system of construction renders
cracking an impossibility, at the same
time that it greatly enhances the tonal
qualities of the instrument. Beale
Pianos contain the only practical steel
tuning-system in the world—-so there
can be no question of splitting of the
wrest-plank. And then our patented
system of cross-plied woodwork en-
sures permanence for the outside case
of the pianos.
Be sure of this: the better musician you are, the more you will delight in a Begle High-Grade Piano.
Yde have always on hand a number of good second-hand pianos, taken in part payment of Beale Pianos.
Call and inspect the Beale Pianos now. Inspection does not put you under any obligation whatever. You suit yourself entirely as to
whether you purchase.
If you cannot call, write for Catalogue, which we will be pleased to send post free.

THE BIG

BRISBANE.
IN BANANA LAND
A 50-year-old Courier comments on the
great anxiety of the Irish of that day to
come to Australia. One ship, the Queen of
the Colonies, took on 100 emigrants at
Queenstown and subsequently discovered 26
illicit Hibernians stowed away among the
dunnage.
“Meona”: The Scripture moveth us in
sundry places. _At Warwick (Q.) recently
a claim for £5O damages was made for
wrongful conversion of a sheep. Said sheep
had been reared from a pet lamb, and was
exhibited in the local show and won a prike.
Some sheep in an adjoining pen were bought
by a butcher who included the pet sheep
under the impression, he alleged, that he had
bought it also. The pet was slaughtered. In
court the only law quoted was from Samuel
11., ch. 12, v. 1-6, which was solemnly read
aloud by plaintiff’s solicitor: —
And the Lord sent Nathan unto David. And ho
came unto him, and said unto him, “There were two
m.en in one city : the one rich and the other poor.
“The rich man had exceeding many flocks and
herds ;
“But the poor man had nothing, save on 4 little
ewe lamb, which he had bought and nourished up ;
and it grew up together with him, and with his chil-
dren ; it did eat of his own meat, and drank of his
own cup, and lav in his bosom, and was unto him
as a daughter.
“And there came a traveller unto the rich man,
and he spared to take of his own flock and of his
own herd, to dress for the wayfaring man that was
come unto h : m ; but took the poor man’s lamb and
dressed it for the man that was come to him.”
And David’s anger was .greatly kindled against the
man; and he said to Nathan, “As the Lord liveth,
the man that hath done this thing shall surely die.;
“ And he shall restore the lamb fourfold. be°' , ”se
he did this thing, and because he had no pity. ”
In this case, it cost the local butcher only
£5, which, in comparison with what Uriah’s
lamb cost the other butcher, was cheap.
Coo-Coo!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“A.J.8.” writes to the press:—“l heard a noise
recently resembling' the cooing of a bronze-wing
pigeon in the night. On going out I found that two
large brown snakes, seven or eight yards apart,
were uttering this strange ‘coo’ to each other. I
despatched one and the other escaped. Next night
I heard only the one ‘coo.’ ” ~
I heard a sound
That stole around
And minded me of lovers true ;
“I hear" (said I)
“The note a-nigh
Of billing doves that kiss and coo."
The eaves below
They cluster so,
Those gentle doves and pigeons too ;
And each to each
In tender speech
Confesses this: “I love but you.”
But “A.J.8.”
(A doubter he)
Went out to watch that am’rous two ;
When stars were clear
He went to peer
At that sweet pair whose cry was “Coo.”
And when 1 cried.
As back he hied,
“And did you see those turtles sue?”
With loud guffaw
He said “Haw —haw —
Old man, the serpents come to coo.
“With bendings brown
They wander down
From yonder line of ranges blue.
And when they meet
They sing : ‘You sweet!’
And that is how you hear them coo.”
Straightway therewith
To Eden’s myth
My wingbd thoughts instanter flew,
And 1 could see
That Wondrous Tree
And one who twined and murmured “Coo,
“You gentle Eve
Why do you leave
The gifts untouched that are your due?
That fruit so rare
That dangles there
It hath a flavor known to few.
“Its savor fine
Is half divine
And she who plucks will never rue” —
And when in haste
She dared to taste
Away he glode and whispered “Coo !”
And ever since
When Love, the Prince,
His song of glamor sings anew,
Forget not this—
Beneath the Kiss
Perchance the Serpent’s calling “Coo !”
QUEENSLAND.
FURNESS BORN.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, April 26, 1913.
My Dear Tiiargomindah,—
Minister O’Sullivan and his wife gave the
High Court Judges and a few mere mortals
a trip on the aged dug-out Lucinda, to-day,
As usual, the way to the wharf was thick
with dust, and the ozone from the rubbish
carts cried out at the foot of the stars. Still,
the dust and the flavor were nothing com-
pared to some similar goods their Honors
have to breathe occasionally in their own
courts.
Bishop White, of Carpentaria, who intends
to sail for the South on Monday, gave an
At Home at Bishopsbourne to-day. All his
old pals turned up and sampled the pious tea
ana scones. If there was any pious whisky it
was kept a dead secret from me and a horde
of other pagans.
"Bob” Philp is going to part with daugh-
ter Blanche early next week, and a youth
named Henderson will in future be respon-
sible for the lady’s hat bills. On Thursday
Aunt Forsyth gave a hop or collective sara-
band in celebration of the transfer, and this
afternoon Mr. and Mrs. Philp took a per-
sonally-conducted party down the river, in
the same good cause.
The Perry-Cameron wedding on Tuesday
was an Event. The Anglican Cathedral was
almost covered with flowers, even the ex-
quisite sacredness of the pulpit being blotted
out. Other decorations were choir boys,
h.' mns, wedding bells, archways, and a white
satin cushion that worried a bald verger
nearly to death. The bride stage-managed a
wedding garment that Aunt Griffiths brought
from Paris, and which had more real lace
about it than most people see in a misspent
life-time. The three bridesmaids, Stella
Bruce-Nicol, Gladys Markwell and Beryl
Macdonald, wore pale blue dresses and halos
of silver tissue., like the angels in the Ger-
man prints which are sometimes priced at
seven for sixpence.
The large number of uninvited guests who
filled , the Cathedral leaned against the de-
corations, and conversed affably while the
clergyman read the service. Twice he
stopped, and gently suggested that the re-
marks would keep till later, but the audience
was cheerful under the reprimand, and went
the whole inhabitant of the sty to the finish.
Last week Miss Dorothea McConnel, elder
daughter of Mr. and Mrs. Harry McConnel.
of Cressbrook, changed her name to Mayo,
without any of the usual fuss and frills.
Professor Michie, of the Queensland Univer-
sity, lent a friendly shoulder to the groom,
who is a lecturer or something appallingly
learned at the same place. There were no
bridesmaids; in fact, womenkind was rigidly
excluded from the tattlesome news until the
newly-fledged wife asked some of the species
to a friendly garden party, where congratu-
lations couid be deposited on the lawn.
THE NEVER-STILL TONGUE
BILLO: “iM a doctor—im? Blime! he come
here tars week, looked at me missus's
tongue, and recommended exercise! ”
Amongst those who attended were Governor
Macgregor’s wife and daughter.
Two weddings are menacing in the near
future. One is another Crombie man to
Mina Macdonald, and the other is that of
Miss Wardrop, of Charters Towers, to Tom
Cusack. The increased expenses that young
people so cheerfully face nowadays leads me
back to the old _ conundrum: “Oh, debt,
where is thy sting?”
Mrs. R. M. Collins, who is one of the most
graceful and well-liked of station hostesses,
gave a fancy-dress ball at Tamrookum on
Thursday night. As the meteorological re-
ports say. it was a small, high-pressure dis-
turbance operating over one centre without
any squalls.
__ Mrs. Frank Newton (who was Miss
Kathleen Verney, a sister of the Chelms-
ford A.D.C. who married Hard Cash in
N. S. Wales) christened a small daugh-
ter at the Cathedral last week. Mother and
daughter are staving with Miss Newton, at
Coorparoo.
The first-nighters filled the theatre last
Monday, to see “The Sunshine Girl.”
Blanche Browne, who has lots of friends
here, gleaned an armful of local flowering
vegetables, and Grace Palotta received a
large helping of glad hand. Grace, by the
way, is domiciled under the O’Shea roof at
Toowong.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
The coming man in the Q. Liberal party
is apparently Colonel Rankin, who repre-
sents the Burruin in the State Parliament.
Rankin is a colliery owner. Quite a see
tion of the Government crowd regards him
as cut out for the Premiership when Den-
ham retires —an event probably not very far
in the future.
“Kobbi”: One Magoffin, a councillor of
some bulk in Western Bananaland, has made
himself immortal by being the first defendant
in this State on a charge of “having started
an artesian bore without having first ob-
tained the consent of the Department.” He
was fined £1 and that beast Costs, and the
beast Cists was so ravenous that the hole in
Councillor Magoffin’s bank balance ran (if
a holevCan run) to £9O. The papers hasten
to speak of it as “this restrictive and annoy-
ing statute.” But, after all, the artesian
water supply isn’t unlimited, and some kind
of regulation is necessary.
“Kobbi”: Bananaland’s law bearing on
magisterial inquiries and the like, could well
do with a decent bit of patching. Suppose,
for instance, that Death calls a teamster
named Fitzclarence de Montmorency to a
fresh job in the next world by some untoward
means. Well, the magistrate, in his crown
and robes, proceeds to gather up sundry bags
of evidence. Witnesses are collected from
their jobs.and hurried (after a delay of a
month or so) before an official, who scratches
down in a big book all that Everywoman has
to say about it. The newspapers fill several
columns on the sub-
ject. which occa-
sional people read,
more especially Bill
Jim while he is eat-
ing his crib. The
official sends the
bulky missive to
headquarters, where
someone else possi-
bly reads through it.
This point is not
certain, but there is
no doubt that it is
poked into a little
ultimate box of its
own and allowed to
rest. There is no
finding, no verdict,
nothing only the
cove in the street.
He tells the fellow
up against the horse
trough what he
thinks about it. This
Act was passrfi in
Que e n sla nd ’way
back in 1866, and it
still sits and dreams
on the statute book,
a monument to a
progressive State. As
an example, there is
the case of the big
railway smash near
Toowoomba, wherein
five men lost their
lives. A magisterial
inquiry was held,
but there was no
finding. True, the
Commissioner held a
Departmental in-
quiry, and found
almost ever ything
except the wav to
pay off the Public
Debt. The Com-
missioner is not the
Mogul of Banana-
land. Yet he has the
nower to give a verdict on the evidence nut
before him, and to fine the guilty (according
to his view), while a full-blown magistrate
can’t. The Act shrieks loud for amend-
ment.
T-lome Secretary Apnel is the Denham
Government’s patron of the Brisbane Sta-
dium. Barnes, conciliates the Wowsers:
Appel the sports. The vinegar and the oil
—and a wise Ministry eaters for both.
“Rangan”: The inmates of Peel Island
lazarette still seem to come and go as they
please. The last one out returned a few
days ago after a six-weeks’ sojourn some-
where. It is unpleasant to think that one
may have been buying apples or potatoes
from a leper, or rubbing shoulders with him
in a crowd. They seem to recognise the
absurdity of the situation, too. “The last
three who returned solemnly stated that
they had been cruising about in an aero-
plane.

THE MODERN MARTYO
MR. NOB: "But don't you Gel warW in those
urs this weather?"
MRS HLOB: “H ain't a question of 'ow I
feels — it's 'ow I looks.”
In two years John Connolly, of Gay ; a h
will have completed his century, and Mrs
John Connolly will not be far'behind him!
John has not yet wholly acquired the tired
feeling attributed to Queenslanders by
southern ignoramuses, but takes his seat on
the Bench as a J.P., chops and splits his
own firewood, and pours forth his reminis-
cences of the early days with wonderful
fluency- and accuracy. The Northern cli-
mate, however, is commencing to worry him
a bit, and the chances are that, when he
has completed his century, the old warrior
may confess to a certain feeling of languor
and lassitude. Centenarians are apt to wilt
under sub-tropical influences.
Says the Warwick Argus, re the running
ota third (Independent) candidate for Fas-
sitern: “With Mr. Jenkinson’s determina-
tion to stand there can be no particular dis-
agreement. but there is room for a whole ship-
load of disagreements with a Government
which permits a state of things under which
any political jackanapes can come along and
dislocate an election.” Which means, in
plain English, that no individual outside the
recognised sacred bounds of Liberalism or
Labonsm has any right to offer himself to
the people as a candidate. The Argus ought
to know' that all great movements have com-
menced and succeeded just through this very
kind of “dislocation.”
Sow that' the King of Greece has passed
away, and King Alfonso of Spain has just
escaped the King of Terrors, it is only fitting
that mention should be made of the demise
of Tommy Green, last king of the Warrego
blacks, wffio closed up his 65 years’ accounts
a few days ago, to the regret of all Charle-
ville, in which town he was the most popu-
lar of local identities. Tommy used to tell
stories of fearful droughts in the early days,
when blackfellows died like flies. Banana-
landers need reminding that the days of

FIFTIETH REPORT.
Presented to the Shareholders at the Ordinary General Meeting on 30th April, 1913.
The Directors have pleasure in submitting to Shareholders the annexed Balance-Sheet and Statement
of Accounts, for the Half-year ending 31st March, duly audited and certified.
The net profits (including £4407/9/3 brought from last Half-year) amount to
which it is proposed to deal with as follows, viz:—-
To pay a dividend at the rate of 8 per cent, per annum.
transfer to the Reserve Fund (making it £190,000) .
and to carry forward the balance ..
By order of the Board,
H. T. WILSON, General Manager.

FORTY-SEVENTH ANNUAL REPORT OF THE DIRECTORS TO THE
SHAREHOLDERS.
The Directors have pleasure in submitting herewith, for the information of Share-
holders, the Balance-Sheet of the Bank, as on 31st March, 1913; also Statements of
Profit and Loss Account, and the Reserve Fund on that date.
The balance to credit of Profit and Loss is .. . . .. £78,221 17 10
Which it is proposed to appropriate as follows: —
To Reserve Fund (making it £490,000) .. .. £30,000
To Dividend at the rate of 10 per cent, per annum .. 25,000
55,000 0 0
Leaving a balance of . . .. .. .. .. .. £23,221 17 10
to be carried forward.
An Interim Dividend at the rate of 10 per cent, per annum was paid in November
last.
Since last report Receiving Agencies have been opened at Callington and Eastern
Well, and the Agency at Lochiel has been closed.
An opportunity having occurred to extend our London premises, a 99 years’ lease has
been arranged, and the office rebuilt.
At the Head Office extensive alterations have also been made, and in both cases the
additional accommodation secured has been rendered necessary by the growth of the
business. The increased facilities are greatly appreciated by the customers of the Bank
and the Staff. The sum of £6BOO has been provided out of the year’s profits towards
cost of the improvements in Adelaide.
It is with much regret we refer to the death, on 21st April, 1912, of our late
Accountant, Mr. R. T. J. Mahnke. He has been succeeded by Mr. C. F. Wreford.
The General Annual Meeting will be held in the Board Room, at the Head Office of
the Bank, King William Street, Adelaide, on Wednesday. 7th May, 1913, at 12 noon, at
which the Shareholders will be called upon to elect two Directors, in place of Hugo Carl
Emil Muecke, Esq., J.P., and George Brookman, Esq., J.P., who retire by rotation, but,
being eligible, offer themselves for re-election.
The Shareholders will also have to elect Two Auditors for the ensuing year, in place
of Messrs. W. L. Ware and J. S. Lloyd, the latter of whom has intimated that he will
not seek re-election. The remuneration of the retiring Auditors will have to be voted.
By order of the Board of Directors.
JOHN SHIELS, Manager.
Dr.

CHICAGO


The late Earl Belmore, who was Governor
of N. S. Wales (1868-72) was the first bound-
less potentate for whom a country residence
was provided. That was at Throsby Park,
Moss Yale, where the economic earl grew
cabbages enough to pay off the encumbrances
on his Irish property. He was the only
viceroy who lived there, other Governors
were provided for at Hill Top, Sutton For-
rest. The fact that royalty’s agent hung
out thereabouts brought a crowd of snobs to
the neighborhood. Most of them lost their
properties one way or the other. There has
been a dreadful commercial mortality among
snobs in recent years.

ALL AUSTRALIANS visiting London
should read the British Austra-
lasian, 6d. weekly—an illustrated paper
containing mail and cable news from Aus-
tralia, and news of Australians in Europe
and at home. Australians are cordially in-
vited to visit the British Australasian
Reading Room at the offices, 115 High Hol-
born, London, W.C., where they can see the
home papers, meet their friends, use the
telephone, and obtain information, without
charge.

HEIRS WANTED.
AN ACTION of Multiplepmnding aud
Exoneration has been raised in the
Court of Session in Scotland (Lord Dewar,
Ordinary, Mr. Brown, Clerk) at the in-
stance of Archibald Bain Birkmyre Scott,
Chartered Accountant, 116 Hope Street,
Glasgow, as Judicial Factor on the estate
of the deceased JOHN McINTYRE, Rose
Cottage, Blairlogie, Pursuer and Real Raiser,
against DUNCAN McINTYRE. Solici-
tor, Inverell, Sydney, and others Defenders.
The fund in medio in said action consists
of the estate and effects left by the said
John Mclntyre who died at Blairlogie, Scot-
land, on September 4, 1912, aged about 78
vears. So far as known to the pursuer, the
said John Mclntyre died intestate, un-
married and without brothers or sisters sur-
viving him. In said action Lord Dewar,
Ordinary, has pronounced the following
Interlocutor: —
“March 20, 1913. Lord Dewar. Act.
Maconochie. The Lord Ordinary finds the
Pursuer and Real Raiser liable only in
once and single payment of the fund in
medio, and Appoints all parties having in-
terest therein to lodge their Condescend-
ences and Claims by the 20th day of June,
1913 ; Further Appoints intimation of the
dependence of this action and of this
Interlocutor to be made twice at an in-
terval of a fortnight, in each of the follow-
ing newspapers, viz.:— The Times, The
Scotsman, The Glasgow Herald, The
Inverness Courier, The Stirling Ob-
server, The News of the World,
the Melbourne Argus, and the Sydney
Bulletin, for the information of all
parties having interest in the fund in
medio.
“ARTHUR DEWAR.”
Of all which notice is hereby given.
NORMAN M. MACPHERSON. S.S.C..
2a Hill Street, Edinburgh.
Hill & Whyte, Solicitors, Stirling.
Agents for the Pursuer and Real
Raiser.
March 20, 1913.

was covered by a firm, yet soft, black growth of hair

pound. I hold over one thousand letters from people

The Inky Way.
“Sardonyx”: I knew the recently-retired Jim
Middleton well. The Melbourne Herald office in
my time (mid-’7o’s) was in a lane between Big
Bourke and Little, and, though “Swearing Sam”
Winter was then the editor, the names cited by
“H.C.C.” (B. 3/4/’l3) are as strange to me (bar
that of Walstab) as to him will probably be thos<
of Harry Dix, George Collier, Grosvenor Bunster,
Ned Wheeler and Billy Bannon. Banncm was the
printer, and, when the paper happened to be a bit
late, it was a treat to see Billy placidly tinkering
with the formes and gently whistling “In Happy
Moments,” while Winter raged round him in a
tornado of profanity.
* * * *
Ex-Queenslander Ross, who has been running the
Maoriland Worker, and going fanti on the anti-
Defence business, takes over the Red Rag of the Mel-
bourne Socialists again. Or, rather, he assumes the
Crimson Tatter of one section of the Melbourne
Socialists—the one that paints “Socialist Party Hall”
over its tabernacle in Elizabeth-street. The other
section, which also claims to be the one and only
true Magennis, paints “Socialist Party Hall” over its
tabernacle in yictoria-street, where Wilson, ex-secre-
tary of the Elizabeth-street coterie, is now the pan-
jandrum.
* * * *
“G.Y.W.”: Despite “Evan Blessus” (B. 6/2/’l3),
the fact that the views expressed may not be a
writer’s personal convictions does not affect the ques-
tion of signed journalism. The object of the signed
article is not to lay the writer open to persecution
because of his opinions, but that the public may form
an opinion as to the motive. This point is recog-
nised by the Bananaland cockies, who are asking the
Legislature to enact that all forecasts in connection
with agricultural produce shall be signed. The way
in which markets have been forced up or down on the
strength of forecasts written by interested individuals
13 well known. The difficulty about leader-writers who
write under express instructions from the manager of
the paper can be met by stating that the article
has been so written. If the writer can success-
fully write up a bad case, such as Anti-Sosh, then
his reputation will be great in the land, just as the
reputation of a barrister who successfully defends a
notorious criminal. No one clamors for anonymous
barristers, anonymous artists, poets, generals, ad-
mirals, Polar explorers, miscellaneous heroes, novel-
ists, or even merchants. Why, then, the demand for
anonymous pressmen ?
* * * *
The S. M. Herald has reviewed H. M. Hyndman’s
“Further Reminiscences.” The leit-motiv is a species
of mild regret, tinctured with amazement, that a rich
man, and a man in English Society to boot,
should go on so. The Herald finds that Hyndman
“has achieved a lively and interesting volume.” But
“it is rather a nitv that a man of the author’s
acknowledged ability and standing should have
thought it necessary to repeat all the old shibboleths
of Socialistic pamphleteering.” How the old Lower
Tooting-cum-Conventicle viewpoint sticks ! Barrister
’ournalist George Burnett Barton (a brother of Bar-
ton of the High Court) was no Socialist, but he wrote
of the S. M. Herald years ago:—
“The Herald was brought into existence by men
who knew nothing of literature, and cared nothing
for politics except as they affected ‘business.’ It
adopted a policy which could not possibly give
offence to men of property—a policy which advocated
the claims of rank and wealth with unflagging
ardor The paper was little more than a sheetful of
advertisements and news.... The leading features of
its character have been faithfuly preserved up to the
present day.... It contains all the advertisements and
all the news of the passing day—and it maintains
the cause of good old English Toryism in all its
g10ry.... The principal feature of the system appears
to be a bland adoration of wealth and position. . . .Its
politics are Tory politics, and of the most inveterate
stamp: politics not only repulsive to the bulk of the
people, but such as few educated men can agree
with.”
And every scornful word of the above holds equally
good to-day.
Brother Harry Holland, the press leader of the
complete hog Socialists in New South Wales, has
been appointed editor of the Maoriland (Wellington)
Worker, in place of Robert Ross, who has moved
on to the Melbourne press.
THE INKY WAY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
On fretted flint and graven stone
And quaintly figured ivory,
On tooth and shell and carven bone
And old Egyptian papyri
They wrought the rune of History ;
Good people read it as ye may,
The beacon lights of Used to Be
Illume the good old Inky Way.
In antique garden, cloister lone,
White fingers plied their ministry,
With seed of pearls for largesse thrown
And gem-encrusted broidery,
And eke in blazoned heraldry
From monk in monastery grey
The beacon lights of Used to Be
Flame down the good old Inky Way.
Down medieval highways blown
By gusty winds of heresy,
By rotting church and ruined throne,
And drifting wrack of Destiny,
From serfdom to Democracy
O’er changing systems of to-day,
The beacon lights of Used to Be
Flame down the good old Inky Way!
Envot.
Per Hoe press and telegraphy
In modem mood the game we play,
While beacon lights of Used to Be
Flame down the good old Inky Way!
VICTORIA.
MARIE E. J. PITT.
STATE IT BRIEFLY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
State it briefly ! Put it tersely, toiling Brother of
the Pen!
Cyanide your information—sift, and boil it down
again ;
For the world is in a hurry and the traveller in
the car
Skips the long-drawn special article to gulp the little
par.
Get the essence of the story, analyse it how you will,
For the man who’s in a hurry wants it packed into
a pill ;
Cut the fireworks and the flowers —and the struggle
to be “bright” ;
“Constant Reader” wants no banquet, but a biscuit
is “just right.”
Carve your lingering heroics. Let the snow lie not
too thick ;
Our capacity for sorrow is so much. More makes us
sick !
Keep in mind this limitation when your lagging
brain you la«h;
Then allow a safety discount of (say) half a col.
for cash.
Keep it down, O Spreading Brother, if you’d win at
last to grace!
Shame the writer’s special Tempter when* he talks
to you of Space!
Never go and fill a hogshead with the contents of a
tub,
Or you’ll never— never —NEVEß be a print-con-
densing “sub” !
N.S.W.
THE RELIGIOUS EDITOR.

hear of a simple and scientific device which restores

her to hear better. Unlike spectacles, the Wilson
Uommon-sense Ear-Drums are invisible and comfort-

rest the ear nerves by taking the strain off them —the
strain of trying to hear indistinct sounds. What a
relief to have done with that tiring strain. Being
made of the softest Para rubber they cannot be felt,
even when the head rests on a pillow. For the same
reason, a blow on the side of the head will not hurt
the ear. They protect the tender inner parts of the
ear, that are so sensitive, from cold winds, dust, sud-
den and piercing sounds.

Magnifiers restore hearing, we give an illustration of
one as it lies in the ear. You will notice that the

"THE A.A.” AUTOMATIC SELF-FILLING FOUNTAIN PEN
is the pen for which there is no substitute. Its self-filling method
is exclusive. It is filled by simply twisting the button, holding
the pen point in the ink and, by a reverse twist, the pen is filled.
As the ink is held in a durable reservoir, the pen cannot leak.
No soiling of fingers or any annoying “filler” necessary to keep
“The A.A.” ready for service.
Fitted with i4kt. gold pens, any style or size, with plain,
chased or mottled holders, or mounted in sterling silver filigree,
gold filled and mother of pearl. They are for sale at all shops.
The cut herein shows the newly patented “ A. A. ’ Clip attached to the
cap of the pen. It excels by far any fountain pen clip yet produced. It
holds perfectly firm, and can be attached almost instantly.

The Golden Shore Hotel.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
XF Providence should say to me : “Good
son, ask thou a boon,
And it shall granted be ere Time has
sued another moon.
Thy many virtues earn thee this. Seek not
the how ol- why.”
Id say: “Q Lord, let me be young, and never
let me die.”
But as I cannot hope for this, that Provi-
dence to me
Should offer any special gift, I bend in piety,
And beg a boon : “Oh, hear me now, from
whom all blessings well—
And guide me when I perish to the Golden
Shore Hotel.”
I know that pub; I see it now; the final
house of call;
I see the sign above the door: “Free drinks
to one and all.”
And all the friends I had on earth, the men
I loved so well,
Will gather in the parlor of the Golden
Shore Hotel.
And all the girls we used to love will serve
behind the bar,
To fill and pass the crystal globes of Provi-
dence three-star—
To laugh with us. to chaff with us —Eulalie,
Doll and Nell—
We'll find the girls we want to find at the
Golden Shore Hotel.
No Ghucker-out will bawl at us: “Now,
gents, it’s closing time.”
Tire lights will flash eternally; no warning
clock shall chime.
And late souls landing from the craft which
Charon’s arms pronel
Will find the boys still going strong at the
Golden Shore Hotel.
High up a ruby star shall blaze to light them
on their way.
And show the tariff sign, which reads, “All
guests ten laughs a day.
No sorrow stocked; no tears on tap; our
brands of joy excel.
(Signed) Providence, proprietor of the
Golden Shore Hotel.”
The counter-lunch will beat the band ; the
beer will flow from wood ;
We’ll sleep on down of angels’ wings, have
angels serve the food
(Those angel« with the bosker hair and eyes
of asphodel)
We’ll hear them calling, “Chops for two,”
at the Golden Shore Hotel.
And after tea we’ll light the pipes and let
the blue smoke "o,
And yarn about the foolish things they do
on earth below ;
Or play a hand at devil’s pool, or poker for
a spell.
With little stars as chips to bet at the
Golden Shore Hotel.
Far down in gloom and dark we’ll see a large
untidy Pit.
Each stranger will be taken out and told the
use of it:
“That’s what they used to lie about the
times they preached of Hell—
It’s used for stacking “ dead marines” from
the Golden Shore Hotel!
H.S.W.
THE SNAKE.
Romance and the Caterpillar.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IT was the fag end of a lovely summer
evening. The stout, perspiring man
with the music roll in his hand and the
silk handkerchief stuffed between his red
neck and his collar, stopped at the gate and
■‘shot a hostile glance at the bent back of the
gardening householder. “Good evening,
■Dredglin,” he remarked curtly.
The gardener straightened up and re-
settled his spectacles on his long, thin nose.
He w T as a long, thin man with sparse whisk-
ers and patient brown eyes. “Oh, it’s you,
Storpor,” he remarked without enthusiasm.
“I’m just trying to get the place clear of
caterpillars.” lie sighed wearily, and waved
his hand at the drawing-room window.
“You’ll find the wife inside.” Then he re-
turned to the chase, and the fat man grunted
and went panting up the path.
The sun slipped down behind the horizon,
and the gardener w’ent on his knees and
peered closely at the bushes in search of his
prey. He had captured four hairy destroy-
ers, and dropped them into the bucket, when
a series of wild brays, accompanied, or fol-
lowed, by music, rang out on the cool air:—
“/ 10-ove thee! 1 10-o-ovc thee
Till the st-ar-s /” (Mumble and growl
of chords.)
It w r as the fat man inside passionately
howling “The Bedouin Love Song.” The
kneeling gardener looked over his shoulder
at the window, now golden in the gathering
night. “Damn his cheek!” he hissed
viciously, and groped in the mould for the
caterpillar he had dropped. Shortly after-
wards the song came to a noisy end, and the
garden drowsed in the stillness. Night
closed down rapidly. The gardener lit the
lantern he had placed close handy, and went
on with his search.
Indoors, the fat man, instead of singing
one song after another as usual, was lean-
ing heavily on the piano and declaiming to
the florid, nervous woman on the piano
stool. “You must fly with me,” he was
saying, in his stout, important way. “You
have endured him too long! Run away
with me, and let us be happy together!” lie
extended a white, trembling hand, and
mopped his brow pathetically. “I love you !”
he gasped. “Can’t you see I love vou !”
She bit her handkerchief ami looked over
her plump shoulder at Ihe door. “Hush,
Bertie! ITe might hear you!”
“He can’t!” cried the fat lover, so in-
aptly named Bertie. “He’s d"wn at the
end of the garden collecting filthy cater-
pillars!”
The stout ladv laughed bitterly. “That’s
so like him! He would rather spend his
time picking caterpillars off plants than
stay with his wife !”
Storpor took her up instantly. “Dar-
ling, let him spend all his davs collecting
caterpillars. Come away with me!”
Mrs. Dredglin shivered so that her ex-
pensive golden fetters ungled musically.
“Oh, I can’t do it! I can’t do it!”
Her lover started to fume jealously.
“Surely you don’t want to stay with him!”
he protested.
She gave him an appealing look. “It’s
tlierscandal that frightens me ! Think of the
scandal, Bertie!”
“I’ve thought of everything}'' 1 retorted
the fat lover passionately, with a backward
sweep of his arm. “The real scandal is that
you and he should continue to live together
when everyone knows that you don’t get
on. You must end it, Sybil! Things can’t
go_ on as they are going !” (The stout lady
shivered and hung her head.) “What would
happen, do you think, if he found out that
you and I loved each other! He would
speak bitter words to you. and I would
strangle him!” (“A 7 0, no, Bertie!") “Yes,
Sybil, I know —I feel that I would strangle
him ! Now you must decide to-night! To-
morrow I start for England to fix up about
those soap agencies. You must come with
me! /”
At this the stout lady rose out of her
chair like a laced wave and foamed to the
window, where she stopped, holding on to
the curtains as the stage female does when
she is perturbed.
“Oh, Bertie!” she murmured chokingly,
and dabbed at her lips.
“To-morrow!” repeated Bertie, staring
tragically at her broad back.
“How you must love me, Bertie!”
Bertie mopped his brow again. “I would
give my life for you, Sybil,” lie puffed be-
hind his silk handkerchief. Summer with
him was a time of limp collars and per-
petual face polishing. Still he had the re-
putation of being a tremendously energetic
and successful soap agent.
“I hate him!” Mrs. Dredglin swung
round and faced him resolutely. “I hate
him with all my soul!”
Berth' stretched imploring hands towards
her. “Then leave* him, my darling! Fly
with me !”
“Oh, I will! I will!” But, half-way to
the piano she wavered. “Oh, Bertie, what
will they say?”
“That you loved me!” panted he. “The
world will be lenient, my own ! It always
is when Romance triumphs over Convention.
Ah, my dear!” He gazed at her rapturously
for a moment. Then, with a startlingly
business-like gesture, he dived one hand into
a tail pocket and groped in its depths.
“Where did I put that time-table?” he
mumbled thoughtfully. Mrs. Dredglin shud-
dered and turned to the piano to hide hel*
nervousness.
They parted formally on the verandah
after arranging to meet next evening at the
junction 20 miles away. At the steps they
encountered the tired husband with his
bucket half-full of caterpillars.
“What! Going already, Storpor? You
et'ople didn’t have a very long practice this
evening, did you?”
Storpor muttered and slapped his music
r ill against his ieg. Mrs. Dredglin glanced
uneasily at the shadow with the lantern.
“No: Mr. Storpor was rather husky to-
night.”
“Storpor’s getting too fat,” said the
caterpillar-hunter judicially.
“Nonsense! How can you say such absurd
thing's !”
“Well., my dear, it’s no disgrace! Fat is
one of Nature’s provisions against old age.
And Storpor’s getting old like the rest of us.
I notice there’s more fat than fire in his
rendering of that Bedouin’s love song these
evenings. By the wav. Storpor, how are
you standing the heat this summer?”
The stout lover coughed violently. “Oh,
fairly well—fairly well!” Then, to the
wife: “ Au revoir, for the present!"
“Good —night,” faltered the stout .lady,
taking his outstretched hand and submitting
to his fervent clasp.
“Good-night, Storpor. Come up again
whi'n it’s cooler,” said the shadowy hunter.
“Er —thanks. Good-night!” Like a dis-
contented elephant, lie, bundled down the
path and disappeared snorting in the gloom.
As soon as he had gone, the stout lady
turned on her husband. “Why are you so
cruel?” she demanded hysterically. “Why
do 'am deliberately torture people?”
“I don’t!” was the simple reply.
“Yes, you do!” insisted the stout lady
fiercely. “You torture and humiliate people
in a thousand different ways! You spoke
to him as if he was positively bulging out!”
“Well” (mildly) “he does bulge a hit!"
“He doesn’t, I tell you!”
“All right: have it your own way. In
future we will regard him thin.”
“Now you’re sneering! You’re always
sneering! I believe vou’re jealous of him !”
“Yes,” said the shadow calmly. “I’m
jealous of his thinness.”
The stout lady stamped on the step. “Oh,
how provoking you are! You irritate me
beyond endurance ! Why do vou bait people
because they’re stout? Why do you do it?”
“But you said just now that he wasn’t
stout at all!”
Mrs. Dredglin emitted a half-stifled scream
of rage. “Oh, you beast! I would rather be
heaten than jeered at. Why,” she de-
manded wildly. “Why don’t you heat me?”
The shadow looked at her plump, almost
pillowy shoulders, and sighed meekly. “Be-
cause neirher of us would enjov it. my dear.
Look at the fine haul of caterpillars I got
to-night!”
But she gathered up her skirts with a
swift movement and rustled indoors from
the triumphant bucket, whispering tensely:
“I can’t bear it! I can’t b°ar it anv more!
My heart is breaking! I will go to Bertie!”
And the self-contained caterpillar hunter,
being left to himself, went swearing round
the house and executed the caterpillars by
strewing them on the ground and thrashing
them savagely with a shovel. It was an
untidy proceeding, hut he got a lot of satis-
faction out of it. For, underneath his calm,
sarcastic exterior, he was a verv wild man
indeed, and fiercely jealous of the stout
singer.
It was the next evening, and the gar-
dener was once more amone-t his shrubs
groning after caterpillars. The gardener’s
wife, who had been watch in" him furtively
from an tupstairs window, at last screwed up
her courag" and decided to make a start.
After sqbbing a little, she resolutely pow-
dered her m se. knotted her veil and went
downstairs for the very last time. She
walked lingeringiv down the garden path—
for she had a whole hour to put in before
the train which was to bear her to perspir-
ing Bertie would rush into the station at
the end of the street. The unconscious <mr-
d«ner did not l<mk up as she approached.
When she arrived opposite him. she stopped

ARE YOUR KIDNEYS WEAK?
Weak Kidneys Cause Backache, Gravel, Urinary
Disorders, Dropsy, Lumbago and Rheumatism.
Every moment of your life, poisonous waste keeps
getting into the blood from your food, and from the
wear and tear of the body. But the kidneys filter it
out and keep the blood pure.
But when the kidneys are weak, they cannot do
their work thoroughly. Then some of the p isonous
waste gets left in the blood, and you begin to feel
dull, heavy and drowsy; your back is bad, your head
aches; dropsical swellings appear in the ankles or
under the eyes, and the heart becomes affected. You
may get rheumatic twinges, lumbago attacks, or dizzy
spells. The bladder acts too often, or there is pain
in seeking relief. There may be sediment, gravel, or
cloudiness of the water.
When the kidneys are ill they need a kidney medi-
cine—ordinary medicines won’t do—they cannot
cleanse and relieve the kidneys. Doan’s Backache
Kidney Pills act quickly and directly upon the
kidneys and bladder—help the kidneys to filter the
mischievous kidney poisons out of the blood and
dispose of them through the urinary system. Doan’s
Pills give the best results of any kidney medicine
known.

made the above statement, and I am now pleased to bring
it up to date by telling you that my husband is still quite
free of every symptom of kidney trouble. My son’s cure
was also permanent, for he has never had backache since.
J
Backache
Kidney
f
and stared at his bent back. It was abso-
lutely expressionless. At last, “I’m going
out!” she cried in a voice which sounded
like a tolling bell —to herself.
He groaned, straightened up cautiously,
and displayed an immense green captive.
“There’s a beauty for you ! There must he
a plague of them about.”
“Good-bye!” she cried, and rushed out
the gate.
“Good-bye,” he echoed in his preoccupied
way, and went on with his job.
In a minute or so she was hack, wild-eyed
and desperate. “I am going out! Do you
understand? Did you hear me?”
“Eh?”
“I say I am going out! Good-?>j/e/”
He met her eye wonderingly. “Yes, I
heard you. You’ll find me here when you
come back.”
She rushed away frantically, telling her-
self that he couldn’t possibly love her. Else
how could he go on in that cold, brutal way?
The station clock gnawed through the
hour at last. With a heavy, premonitory
rumble and a long, hoarse hoot, the hot and
heavy express locomotive rushed importantly
into the station and came to a standstill,
panting and dripping oil. The stout lady
clambered into a first-class carriage won-
dering tremulously why the engine reminded
her so of Bertie. Being of an incurably
romantic temperament, she decided happily
that it must be because it was to bear her
to him. She felt she could have kissed the
great, strong masterful engine. What could
prevail against it? What could stop it
from taking her to Bertie? Nothing, of
course!
As the train moved out, she glanced
guiltily at the washhouse chimney—all that
was visible of the house from the line. Never
again would she warn the girl not to be
wasteful with the wood. Then the engine
broke into a furious gallop, and she went
clattering furiously across country. For a
quarter of an hour the express skated round
curves and roared down hills and along
levels. Then the long climb to the junction
was entered upon, and the great locomotive
in front started to puff furiously and crawl.
The carriages clacked monotonously and
crawled after it for awhile. Suddenly there
was a furious scurry of wheels, some hard
swearing by the engine and a great clash-
ing of buffers. Then the whole caravan
started to slip gently backwards downhill.
The suddenly-applied brakes checked the
retreat with a series of unpleasant jolts.
Presently the engine took a deep breath
and tried again. It didn’t succeed. By this
time, heads were protruding from every
compartment window.
“What’s the matter, guard?” asked a
high-pitched, quarrelsome voice.
The steady crunching of heavy boots on
the stones of the permanent wav ceased Dr
a second. “Swarm of caterpillars on the
line, sir.”
Caterpillars again ! The stout ladv leant
pleadingly from her window. “Guard ! Will
we catch the train at the junction, do you
think?”
“I’m afraid not, mum. This is a stiff
bank here, and there isn’t any engine to help
us.” He went on to hold a useless consulta-
tion with the engine-driver.
They got into the junction an hour behind
their scheduled time. The other train had
fled south long ago, and the platform wore
a desolate appearance. The stout lady
bundled out and attacked the gold-laced
station-master. lie, after inquiring her name,
thrust a soiled envelope into her hand and
fled. This is what she read in the shade of
the empty drinking tank: —
My Own,- SUNSET JUNCTION, 4.25.
Why are you not here? Must go on though it
causes me agony. Cannot break journey. All our
future happiness hangs on those soap agencies. What
has happened? Wire to hotel.
Till the stars grow old, my Precious.
BERTIE.
The stout lady rose up and pursued the
S.M. once more. He was a slow man, and
she soon captured him. . “There’s a down
train in 15 minutes, mum. l r es, you can
send a telegram from here.” He gave her
pen, ink and forms, and escaped again. She
tore up 14 forms before she got her tidings
into what she considered a concise, non-
committal message. This she lodged, and
then sat on a shady seat and jabbed holes
in the gravel of the down platform, while a
boy porter amused himself by dragging milk-
cans about.
It was dark when she arrived back at the
front gate she had left for ever some three
hours before. A golden lozenge of light hov-
ered and danced sedately in the garden. She
went in and slammed the gate. Her shadow
husband grew suddenly' out of a rosebush.
“Back again!” he groaned, pressing the
small of his back with his disengaged hand.
Ilis wife amazed him by- bursting into
tears. “Why do you taunt me?” she sobbed.
“I’m not taunting you,” he protested
dazedly. “I’m not taunting you, woman!
I’m just collecting caterpillars !”
She rushed inside, passionately assuring
herself that Bertie —who, of course, under-
stood her thoroughly—would soon return
and take her away. But Bertie never did.
After reading over her curt telegram 43
times, he came to the conclusion that “Foi’-
give me. Caterpillars again.” Could only
mean that she had decided at the last minute
to stay with her husband. So —thoroughly
misunderstanding her—he went fuming off
to England, where he met and married a
musical widow who liked the sincere wav he
rierspired when he sang “The Bedouin Love
Song.”
KODAK.
How They Did It.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
SYDNEY is a nail-right place. You can
take it from me that Thargomindnh
an’ Hungerford don’t give ye the
faintest idea. When I was down there last
Easter I’d Billy McGuire with me the first
two days, an’ he knows Sydney as well as
he does the horse-paddock. We didn’t get
bushed more’n once or twice, an’ the nex’
day I went out on me own.
I was standin’ at the corner o’ King-
street. an’ a cove comes along an arst me if
I could direct him to the post office. This
was right in to me, for I’d just come from
there, an’ I showed it to ’im about two chains
away. “Strike me,” says ’e, “we country
blokes must seem mugs to you towniesJ”
When I told him I wasn’t a Sydney cove Be
wudden believe me.
I always did say Jimmy Dixon kep’ the
most stylish rig-outs north of the Darling.
This bloke came from Narromine, an’ he
had to see if there was a letter for him from
his old man. We got yarnin’, an’ the cove
went in for his letters, an’ got a couple.
One of them had been lyin’ there, an’ had
15 quid in it that he’d lent to a mate at
Warrego once. lie was real glad to get the
sugar, a tenner an’ a fiver, ’cause it would
let him pay an aunt of his seven quid he
owed her up in Surry Hills.
We went an’ had a couple of beers, an’
took a tram up tc his aunt’s place, but she
was out, an’ only her two daughters was in.
Nice girls they were. Y'ou wudden ha’ took
’em for sisters if ye hadn’t a-known, an’
they were so pleased to see me mate, hug-
gin’ an’ kissin’ him all over the shop. I got
on pretty well with ’em, too. One of ’em
wanted to give us a drink, but the other, a
shy piece, said she was frightened for what
Aunty would say if we touched her stuff.
So I sent for some.
Beer is pretty dear in Surry Hills. It
cost me five bob for two bottles.
Then the old lady came in, an’ I was in-
terdooced, an’ the cove offered her the ten-
ner, but she only had a fiver an’ a few bob.
I would have changed the note but I had
only six notes an’ three quid an’ a bob or
two, so I changed him his fiver, an’ the old
lady shouted.
Then the cove an’ the other girl an’ their
aunty went away to see a cousin, an’ the shy
tart stopped with me. I don’t know how it
was, but I got muggin’ her about, an’ she
took on dreadful. You’ve no idea how shy
she was.
I offered her the fiver I got from her
cousin if she’d let up on it, hut she wudden
take it. At last she said she’d take a quid
or two to buy a mementer, and I passed her
three quid, an’ promised not to kiss her
again. Then her aunt and Billy an’ his
cousin came back, an’ I got ready to go.
Billy was goin’ to put the balance of his
sugar on a horse for the Ding-dong Handi-
cap at 15 to one. Spitsure was its name, I
think. He wanted to put a bit on for me.
so. I offered him the fiver, but he reckoned
he couldn’t get 13 quid on without frighten-
ing the bookies —he was splitting up the
eight quid with the three ladles an’ himself
-—an’ a tenner was the most he reckoned lie
could use. So I gave him 25 bob, although
I’d ha’ liked the lot on at the odds.
I was goin’ to see him the next day, which
was the day before the race, an’ get him to
nut another bit on for me, only I got run
in that night for shovin’ a bad note on the
cove at the pub.
The swine Billy lent the brass to paid him
back with a cronk fiver !
The magistrate was a suspicious old mre.
He said I’d been took down, so he’d dismiss
the case provided I cleared back home at
once
Gripes ! I had to hoof, it most of the way
because every bean I had excop' the notes
which they said was cronk was the twenty-
five bob on Spitsure. who ran last.
D. H. SOUTER.
The Prism of Tophet,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Occult persons declare that they are able r-,
colored emanation surrounding men ami se<> a
and that the size and tint of the aura‘u an °eS
indication of the character. txac '

V W lifting
The corners of Tophet’s lid,
The souls come down from their sifting
With never a failing hid,
And the tall cook-devils toiling
With fork and with flaming rake
Gather them in to the broiling
In the spume of the brimstone lake.
Like Orient gems that cluster
In the crown of a first-grade king
Are the spirit colors that muster
To the red-hot welcoming;
For every sin has its fashion,
And every crime has its hue,
From the crimson that throbs in passion
To the lying that burns in blue.
Yellow as daffodil dawning
The person who gathered our gold
When our young clothes went to the
pawning,
And the world looked shame on the old
And purple as (Ja'sar's trousers
That shone in the sunlight strong
Are the souls of the deep earousers
Who looked on the wine too long.
Green are the w T arlike persons
Who jabbed at each other’s throats—
Ajaxes, wild McPhersons,
And people with various coats
Of arms which the herald mentions
As things of resplendent sheen;
In spite of their red intentions,
Their souls are a viv.d green.
Sometimes in opaline glory
A Herod may fiutter through,
With a mixed and curious story
Made plain in his varied hue;
Or a terrible, bad. black hero
Who took in his crimes delight,
Will blend like Judas or Nero
All sins in a ray of white.
Rut we who have only taken
The easy and primrose wav
Ha v<> our colors mixed and shaken
Till we look but a smoky grey;
A sort of a cloudy blending
That fails of one big, bright sin
Gives us a rayless ending
When the fiendkins rake us in.
When the sulphurous winds are lifting
The corners of Tophet’s lid.
And the doomed come down from their
sifting
With never a failing hid.
Our own little souls that smash on
The thoughts of the deeds men do
Are somewhat out of the fashion
When the large, bright lights leap
through.
